The Red Page
Out-Back Verses by
Charles Henry Souter
.His German dialect verses will be read
with delight—by Germans; but it is in his
“On the Land” section that he makes his
claim to recognition as an Australian poet.
Sold Up,” “Pum’kin Time,” and the
samples quoted above show his individual
contribution to our verse; and in future
anthologies there will always be room for
one _ or more of these graphic bits of Aus-
tralian character-drawing. He is doing
here what no other of our minors can do so
well.

The “colonial” edition of G. Herbert
Gibson’s Iroribark Splinters, at half a
crown, has been sold out, but a few of the
English edition (5s.) are still locally avail-
able.
Mary Grant Bruce’s latest story, Glen
Eyre (Ward, Lock, per JDymock, Sydney),
comes in ample time to solve the question
of a Christmas present for the Australian
kiddie.
MUMMER'S SICK.
’Ave yer fed them ’orses, Percy?
Martha! Jus ’ go in ter Mum.
{Now th'en, Stan; don’t cry, my sonny!
Pupper’s goin' ter give yer some.)
Perce! You go an’ put the saddle
On the yaller mare; be . quick!
Ride across to Andy Hogan’s;
Tell yer Auntie, Mummer’s sick.
Leave the panel down. ’Ere, Stanley!
Let the dawg alone, my son!
Gome along and play with Pupper,
An’ ’e’ll let yer see ’is gun.
Is the kettle boilin’, Martha?
Take yer Mummer in some tea,
Then you go an’ get yer supper,
No, yer needn’t wait for me.
Pupper put his boy to bye-bye?
Daddy undress little Stan?
See if we can get ’is boots off!
That’s ’is Daddy’s little man!
Lay down, Patch ! ( I’m cornin’, Mummer !)
Martha, fetch a bit more wood.
(All right, Mummer!) Good night,
Stanley!
Go to bye-bye, and be good.
’Eres a bit o’ somethin’, Mummer!
There now 1 Let me raise yer ’ead !
W’at ioas it the doctor told me?
i W’y, ’e said —’e said —’e said—!
Oh, my Gawd! ’e couldn’t meant it!
' Bess! you ain’t a-going ter dieV
.Bess, my darlin’.... A ll right, Stannie!
. Pupper’s cornin’! Don’t you cry.

W’at the answer may be,
For you’re all I cares for, Emily Smith!
From “Irish Lords, and Other Verses”
(The Boolifellow, Sydney).
Charles Henry Sou ter, of South Aus-
tralia, hitches his Pegasus up to the Com-
monwealth homestead fence and strolls in-
side. He is welcome. In the exquisitely
produced little volume of his verses—the
title page is dignified and delightful, and the
type on the slightly sunburnt paper gives
his verse a decorative charm—there is
poetry, not of the highest quality, indeed,
but from the heart. Irish Lords, and Other
Verses (The, Bookfellow, Sydney) intro-
duces a delightful personality, an artist who
can say simple things simply, touched, too,
with the humor that is near to tears. His
chanteys have the right rollicking note:—•
Eh! W’at-cheer, Polly, with the eyes so
hlue!
(An’ a Hai-ya! we're shovin’ 'er along!)
Here's Jack a-swearin’ 'e'U he always true!
{Halleyeh! come pull 'er over!)
Just kiss him cheerily, and say good-bye,
He's a wife and family in Peckham Rye,
But he’ll soon be back again, so don’t you
cry!
(With his Hai-ya! Johnnie is a rover!)
Marriage
The novel of the future, proclaimed H. G.
Wells recently, was going to discuss con-
duct, analyse conduct, suggest conduct. And
the latest Wells novel, Marriage (Macmil-
lan), is already the third of these novels of
the future. In Ann Veronica and The New
Machiavelli Wells discussed conduct; in
Marriage he is discussing married conduct.
There is little plot, and hero and heroine
are conventionally married, and neither looks
for, nor finds, an affinity outside the marriage
bond. The story is merely that of Marjorie,
who drifts into an engagement with one
man and falls in love with, and marries,
another, Children arrive, the husband makes
money, and both partners grow dissatisfied
with life. A way out is found in a winter
spent in Labrador; and they return to their
children with new resolves that point to the
making of something noble of that unsatis-
factory business called marriage.
Into this book Wells puts everything
about which lie is interested —which means
everything that the modern reader will be
interested in. For Wells has the sanest brain,
possibly, in the writing world. It is the
brain of a disinterested scientist, righteously
annoyed by the prevalent muddle of things,
and enthusiastically intent on indicating the
steps that might be taken to clear up the
mess. He considers, for instance, that the
present stage of human evolution is hardly
beyond that of the cave man. Everything is
yet to do for the race; the paramount need
is not action nor “movements”-—for which he
has a zealous scorn—but clear thinking. And
in Marjorie he shows us the result of the
present muddle in education and at the
same time paints probably his most convinc-
ing portrait of the modern woman. She, at
least, has not stepped out of a book, as even
Maurice Hewlett’s ladies have a habit of
doing: she has stepped out of life.
Here is Marjorie as a girl
At school she had been taught what I may
best describe as Muffled Christianity—-a tem-
perate and discreet system designed pri-
marily not to irritate parents.... She was
growing up like the great majority of res-
pectable English people, with her religious
faculty, as it were, artificially faded....
Indeed she did not live in a world of right
and wrong, or anything so stern; “horrid”
and “jolly” had replaced these archaic
orientations.
Wells can be deliberately dull; and Mar-
riage begins with an analysis of Marjorie’s
people that is not worth the space. But in
indicating how she became engaged to the
impossible Magnet Wells explains how so
many girls do get engaged to the wrong
men. They simply have not had the educa-
tion that would enable them to know the
right man ; and they are forced to compro-
mise with the nearest and most pertinacious
male. But Trafford, a scientific investiga-
tor, drops into Marjorie’s life from an aero-
plane—his first and last ascent —and the
fate of Magnet is settled. They make a
runaway marriage, “and the Poor Hears
haven’t a shadow of doubt they will live
happily ever afterwards.” But life is not
a fairy tale. For Trafford finds that
Marjorie needs money—she has more than
the usual girl’s dislike of paying bills —and
scientific research does not make a man
wealthy enough for an extravagant wife. So
he has to choose between science and the
wife. And by this time the honeymoon was
over;—•
And now, indeed, the Traffords were com-
ing to the most difficult and fatal phase of
marriage... .Now, as far as sex was con-
cerned, there might be much joy and delight
still, but no more wonder, no fresh discov-
eries of incredible new worlds and unsus-
pected stars. Love, which had been a new
garden, an unknown land, a sunlit sea to
launch upon, was now a rich treasure-house
of memories. And memories, although they
afford a perpetually increasing enrichment
to emotion, are not sufficient in themselves
for the daily needs of life. For this, indeed,
is the truth of passionate love, that it
works out its purpose and comes to an end.
A day arrives in every marriage when the
lovers must face each other, disillusioned,
stripped of the last shred of excitement—
undisguisedly themselves.
Trafford has, as a matter of fact, discov-
ered how to make cheap synthesised rubber.
To him comes a rich Hebrew promoter:—
“I don’t believe that the majority of
people who make money help civilisation
forward any more than the smoke that comes
out of the engine helps the train forward.
They’re about as much help as—fat.
They accumulate because things happen to
be arranged so.. v .lf you’ve got big gifts
and you chose to help forward the world, if
you chose to give away everything you can
do in the way of work, you’ve got to give up
the ideas of wealth and security, and that
means fine women and children. You’ve got
to be a deprived sort of man. ‘All right/
you say. ‘That’s me!’ But how about
your wife being a deprived sort of woman?’
That s where it gets you ! And, meanwhile,
you know, while you make your sacrifices
and do your researches, there’ll be little,
mean, sharp, active beasts making money all
over you like maggots on a cheese. And if
everybody who’d got gifts and altruistic
ideas gave themselves up to it, then evi-
dently only the mean and greedy lot would
breed and have the glory. They’d get every-
thing. There wouldn’t be an option they
didn’t hold....It isn’t worth it....It’s like
building a temple for flies to buzz in.”
And Trafford sees reason. He joins the
Hebrew syndicate, and reaps his reward in
a fortune. But, having achieved, what is
there left for him to do? His wife is given
up to society; he has lost his . interest in
research; games or hobbies do not attract
him:—*
The serious and responsible life of an
ordinary prosperous man fulfilling the re-
quirements of our social organisation fatigues
and neither completely satisfies nor com-
pletely occupies. _ Still less does the respon-
sible part of the life of a woman of the pros-
perous classes engage all her energies or hold
her imagination. And there has grown up a
great informal organisation of employments,
games, ceremonies, social routines, travel, to
consume these surplus powers and excessive
ci’avings, which might othei’wise change or
shatter the whole order of human living. He
began to understand the foxced preoccupation
with cricket and golf, and so forth, to which
the young people of the economically free
classes in the community ai'e trained.... He
began to undei-stand something of the psy-
chology of vice, to comprehend how small a
part mere sensuality, how large a part the
spirit of adventure and the craving for ille-
gality, may play, in the lives of those who are
called evil livers....
Surely never before in the history of the
world has there been so great a multitude of
secure-feeding, satisfied-looking, uninquiring
people as there is to-day. All the tragic
great things of life seem stupendously remote
from them; pain is rare, death is out of
sight, religion has shrunken to an inconsider-
able, comfortable, reassuring appendage of
the daily life. Never before has the world
seen such multitudes, and a day must come
when it will cease to see them for ever-
more.... He wondered if the King ever lay
awake at three o’clock in the morning and
faced the riddle of the eternities, or whether
he did really take himself seriously and con-
tentedly as being in himself the vital func-
tion of the State, perfoi'med his ceremonies,
went hither and thither through a wilder-
ness of gaping watchers, slept well on it.
Was the man satisfied? Was he satisfied
with his empire as it was and himself as he
was, or did some vision, some high, ironical
intimation of the latent and lost possibilities
of his empire and of the world of Things
Conceivable that lies beyond the poor tawdry
splendors of our present loyalties, ever dawn
on him?
He resolves that it is necessary for him to
get away somewhere and think. His mother
persuades him to take his wife—now the
mother of a family—with him; for, if the
race is to progress, the male must take the
female along the track he means to tread.
Mai-jorie leaves her children in safe hands
and follows her man. The winter spent in
Labrador is vividly described, and the hard-
ships of that sojourn, the new aspects of
their humanity that this strenuous life
brings to the surface, re-make these two
souls over; and they return to study human-
ity, to examine and discuss and criticise the
basis of human life, the first necessary step
to the endowment of life with a finer mean-
ing. And Wells nicely indicates that the
woman will not succeed as the man will
succeed in living up to their new ideals.
For the moral is that woman is greedy—
greedy of the good things of life, greedy of
the man’s love and attention. She must
become the squaw. And in marriage she
must demand less of the man: she has no
right to compel him to give up his chosen
work to make money for her to spend, even
if, like Marjorie, “a born snatcher and
shopper,” she spends it on beautiful things.
Marriage is a great book. Women will
doubtless find it occasionally a dull book.
But it is a, courageous book, and the brain
of H. G. Wells is here seen at its ripest.
And there is no other man in English litera-
ture whose thought is better worth knowing.
In the preface to his verses, Under the
Open Shy (B, J. Clarey, Sydney), Harley
Matthews asks us that we “forget the teach-
ings of all school-day pedants when reading
in this book; that, for example, we pro-
nounce the word ‘wind’ as we would speak
it.” Certainly. And in his dedication “To
all Good Australians,” he further asks: —
My land, will you not hear me in this hush,
This songless hush that now is on the
world?
Somehow, we think not. Harley Matthews
has browsed on Shelley, and here he echoes
his master faintly, very faintly. There are
no faults to be found in his metre, there are
no excellencies to be praised in his verse;
he rambles on, vaguely, prettily, telling at
length the thin, conventional thoughts that
come to youth, with much about bees and
birds and the death of innocent maidens.
Only in “The Breaking of the Drought,”
published in The Bulletin —the rest of his
verses blossom here for the first time —does
he touch reality, and then but diffidently.
Fritz Nietzsche
Fritz, not Friedrich, you notice. For The
Young Nietzsche, by his sister, Fran
Forster-Neitzsche (Heinemann, per Svdntv
Book Club), deals solely with J the
youth of the great philosopher, from the
view-point of the woman to whom this
genius was always her brother Fritz. The
world knows the older, solitary Nietzsche-
Ufe' 6111 We See the firSt tappy years °f his
“Maybe this life-history is important
chiefly in this respect, that it presents us
with one great problem. For it is a pro-
blem that Friedrich Nietzsche, who denied
our present moral values, or at least traced
them to sources absolutely unsuspected
hitherto —this Transvaluer of all Values—
should himself have fulfilled all the loftiest
and most subtle demands made by the
morality now preached among us. And he
did not do this because of any moral im-
perative, but from a perfectly cheerful in-
ability to act otherwise.”
Fritz’s mother, a bride at 17, was de-
scribed as ‘‘a gorgeous savage,” a tomboy
and a beauty. His father was a gifted
pastor, with an extraordinary talent for
music. Fritz was the eldest child—a re-
served, _ decorous, pious schoolboy, “extra-
ordinarily good.” His sister is unable to
recall any mad pranks or misbehavior. A
brilliant scholar, he was early marked out
for advancement. On the whole, his school
and student life shows him to us as rather
a prig. From the first, he took him-
self with extraordinary German seriousness.
“Throughout his life lie never once fell into
the clutches of a grand passion or of a vul-
gar love. His whole heart was absorbed
by the world of knowledge, and for this
reason everything else only aroused temper-
ate feelings. Later in life, he was really
sorry never to be able to reach the heights
of a regular amour-passion.'’ At the Uni-
versity of Bonn he resigned from the Stu-
dents’ Association on the Wowseristic
ground that in that company there was too
much “beer-materialism.” And he deter-
mined to devote his life to that chaste
science, Philology.
Though debarred by his shortsightedness
from serving his time in the army, he had
repeatedly applied for military service. But
at the age of 23 he was accepted and joined
the horse artillery, where his duties were
arduous and uninteresting. As the result
of an accident during drill he was incapaci-
tated, after completing only five months of
his service, but he was credited with a year’s
service, and his illness, giving him six
months of perfect liberty from his univer-
sity lectures or his horse drill, enabled him
to work unhampered at his special ambi-
tions. And before his twentieth year he
had an extraordinarily diversified know-
ledge of books and an equally remarkable
wide range of information. In explanation
of his early specialisation on so dead a sub-
ject as philology, it is suggested that
Nietzsche, recognising the danger that lay
in the desire for universal culture, desired
to be master of a well-defined specialty. And
the very dryness and sterility of the subject,
the . rigor of its method and the minuteness
of its detailed studies, attracted him. But
chiefly he liked philology because it was
dead and useless; he hated all utilitarian
sciences, he distrusted questions of the day.
All this indicates the very young Nietzsche,
Fritz, rather than Friedrich.
So we find this priggish student a pro-
fessor of Classical Philology at the Univer-
sity of Bale at the age of 24. Here he made
an extraordinary reputation as a lecturer.
But the outbreak of the Franco-Prussian war
in 1870 led him, though he was now a
naturalised Swiss, to offer his services to
his country; and he was granted leave by
the university to serve, though only as an
ambulance attendant. As a member of the
Red Cross Society he was sent to the seat
of war; and it was the sight of a charge
that, as he writes, made him feel, “for the
first time,, that the strongest and highest
Will to Life does not find expression in a
miserable struggle for existence, but in a
Will to War, a Will to Power, a Will to
Overpower.” But a severe attack of dysen-
tery and diphtheria incapacitated him, and
laid the seeds of his subsequent ill-health.
Had Nietzsche a stomach that digested his
food properly there might have been an-
other Nietzsche. The same reflection applies
to his short sight, which was the cause of
his incessant headaches. The rest of the
book is a chronicle of breakdowns in health.
Chronic gastric catarrh and strain on the
optic nerve —these sum up the physical side
of the great moralist.
The author promises a companion volume,
The Lonely Nietzsche, dealing with the
better known part of his life.

AN ALMOST MIRACULOUS RECOVERY.
Mr. Stott, of 8 Crosby Street, Holbeck, Leeds, wrote us that his aged grandmother was down with Chronic
Bronchitis and a Weak Heart, and so ill was she that all the relatives were summoned to the bedside. She ex-
pressed a wish for “Wincarnis,” which was immediately procured, and within half an hour of taking the first
dose of “Wincarnis” the struggle for breath ceased, and from that moment she began to mend. Five days after- t
wards she was .able to get up and sit near the fire. ' ‘ ' •

According to an official of N.S.W. Wickerworkers’
Union, imported Chow-made rattan chairs, on which
there is a dutv of 7s. 6d. each, are being sold locally
at a similar price. As even a Chow chair-vendor,
subsisting on the occasional whiff of an oil-rag, has
to show anything above the duty in order to keep
up the supply of rags, the inference is that the fur-
niture is merely , given away as discarded wrappings
of a valuable import—opium, to wit. In the circs.,
Minister Tudor might gimlet the limbs of some of
this hollow furniture just to see if they’ll bleed.
“Clif”: Officers of the Mercantile Marine on the
Australian coast have, after a .strenuous battle, man-
aged to get a very fair Arbitration award with re-
gard to wages, hours, overtime, etc. Yet holes can
be picked in it. For instance, one large coastal co.
carries five officers in each of its large vessels, three
for , watchkeeping, the others to assist in watch-
keeping. The former work the regular four hours
on and eight off, while the two juniors have to split
the 24 hours between them, as the overtime and
eight-hour day only allude to “in port” in their
agreement. Wherefore they plod along doing the 12
hours per diem, vainly wishing the five years of the
agreement were up, so that the position might be
rectified, and hoping to rise out of juniordom shortly.
Meanwhile the owners rake in the extra labor gratis
with hearty satisfaction.
Hickey, M.L.A. for Alexandria (N. S. Wales),
drew attention, the other evening, to a queer anomaly
in the Weights and Measures Act.. It is administered
by a special police department. Elaborate arrange-
ments are made for the testing and certification of all
weights and measures, and any cozening or careless
trader whose scales pull three or four ounces light is
liable to be instantly torn to pieces by wild legal
asses, or, in default, fined. On the other hand, there
is nothing to prevent a man from sell ng a 7 51b. bag
of flour (say) as a 1001 b. bag, and charging for
it proportionately. A man may not have false
weights, but he may sell false weight with all the
impunity that happens to be lying about.
The police authorities of Berlin have issued an
edict banishing horse-drawn cabs from the streets of
the city. The vehicles in question, it is said, are so
obsolete as to be unworthy of an up-to-date place
like the German capital. To each of the 60 licensed
drivers (the 60 remnants) affected by the new regu-
lation 150 thalers are to be paid by way of com-
pensation, and applications from any of them for
permission to drive motor vehicles are to be favor-
ably considered. In a short space the horse will be
as extinct as the rooster that cock-a-doodled at the
holy apostle Saltpeter.

“Mr. Ocky”: Has all Australia gone raving mad
on borrowing? 1 am absolutely full up of reading
day by day of the loans that are contemplated, or
are being negotiated by the host of governments, de-
partments, municipal councils and other public con-
cerns. Victoria’s Watt, in his Budget speech, spoke
proudly of the State’s loan indebtedness being only
£60,737,216, or £44 11s. 4d. per head, as against
£66 2s. 3d. per head for the average of Australia.
And because it is only a paltry sixty millions, the
Government feels it has plenty of excuse for making
it bigger and more staggering to the generations that
will follow. This careless craze of begging, bor-
rowing, pawning, lending; of providing miserable
sinking funds and paying interest to the far-away
Cohen ; of keeping staffs of men pottering with
figures and statistics to periodically remind the coun-
try how much it has to pay back —these things
surely are humiliating. If the money value of all
the time, ink, pens, stamps, printing, paper, light-
ing, worry, bad language, beer and other things con-
sumed or devoured in (s& y) the last five years by
Australia’s army of beggars and their assistants, were
added to the interest paid during the same period,
there would be enough, I believe, to build a do cent
navy. There might even be something over for the
railway to the Northern Territory, the development
of neglected ports, and many more serviceable things.
But the fever of cadge is rampant; it is nothing but
borrow, borrow. Even the church down the street
is up to its steeple in debt. When is it all going to
stop ?
“G.V.W.”: Whenever I hear the large, local Tory
howling against alleged confiscation by the Labor
party, and mouthing sweet nothings about the sacred
rights and liberties enjoyed in the drear old Mother-
land, 1 am reminded that he is on shocking terms
with the truth. 1 recall, per medium of authentic
history, the year 1562, in England, when good old
Privateer Enterprise proved its incompetence or
greed (probably greed) by holding down the crops
so long that the rains destroyed them. The loss of
grain was so great that Parliament had to turn to
and feed the multitude. (In those days England
mostly grew its own food.) The next job Parlia-
ment did was to pass the following law: “All persons,
of whatever degree to thresh and sell their corn
at once ; any stack found standing on the 10th July
to be confiscated.’’ Observe that no option in the
way of fine or imprisonment was offered. It was just
plain, bald, blown-in-the-glass confiscation. And the
next time I hear a loud political bookmaker calling
the odds against the animal’s performances in Eng-
land I’ll put him out of business.
AUSTRALIAN DAY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Melbourne has just celebrated an Australian day,
when the windows of the shops were filled with showy
goods of local manufacture. The display, from the
point of view of the Age, was “entirely satisfactory.”
Prizes were given for the most excellent window
shows.
I searched the city round, and saw
The windows piled with many things,
With hats of velvet, fur and straw,
With shirts, and cabbages and strings ;
But prowled the city o'er in vain,
So in the suburbs searched again
For what, despite big immigration,
Is still most, vital to the nation.
In shops suburban I descried
Their hams and bacon smooth and sleek,
The suit that valiantly defied
All competition. Still I seek
The tradesman who for credit bids
With windows full of fine, fat kids —
The one who .took the highest prizes
For native babes, assorted sizes !
VICTORIA.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At Deya.
I lie on a large damp rock,
With the world and the village below;
And above, from the peaks,
Fall silvery streaks
From a field of eternal snow.
Below and beyond is the sea,
And a grey old tower that mows
At its dimpling smiles
That were wont, old whiles,
To herald the pirate prows.
Behind are the beetling crags
That frown on the bush-clad steeps,
And the clefts, deep-cut,
From the daylight shut .
Where the shadow of midnight sleeps.
Ripe olives, all purple-black,
Fall pattering here from the tree,
As the soft breeze moves
Through the ghostly groves,
With a sound like the distant sea.
Below, on its sentinel hill,
A church of an ancient day
Holds high its head
In the camp of the dead,
And watches the living way.
In yonder cove are the huts
Where hardy men abide,
Who rack the bay
By night and day
For fish, and for much beside.
Up, back, for a thousand feet
Are the rock-built homes that cling
To the tottering edge
Of a terraced ledge
As the swallows build in the spring.
’Tis festa day to-day
Of who knows what old saint,
And the villagers go
Aye, to and fro, .
To the sound of the church bell s plaint.
By night and day they have gone,
. And the bell is tinkling still,
And its note, far blown,
Makes one with the tone
Of the sheep bells here on the hill
Just so is the soughing voice
Of the mountain torrent swift,
Blent in with the rave
Of the rock-pent wave
As it breaks in a caverned rift.
But the sun beats warmly down
On my large, damp, somnolent rock;
And the world below
Is a spirit show,
That the clouds and the mountains
mock;
And I but a speck—a fly,
A spot of moss on the stone.
It is well for such
To abide the touch
Of the Vast: and to be alone.
palma.
WAIF.
The Blasphemy of Meroz.
The curse of Meroz must rest upon all who do not
come to help in the fight against the throne of the
drink iniquity.— Rev. Thomas Davies . his swear.
The curse of Meroz falls like dew
Upon a thirsty land,
Where Wowsers range afar to view
Our soul-girths that expand;
And speak dark sayings very few
Can hope to understand.
The curse of Meroz sparkles bright
In shallow glasses wide,
Where sons of plenty sit at night
With beauty at their side —
Oh, costly is the young delight
Its golden capsules hide !
The curse of Meroz softly dwells
In ruby liquid clear,
Where love its new-found rapture tells
In some pink, listening ear,
And eyes grow soft as mountain wells
When some white god is near.
The curse of Meroz swells and grows
In private bars and fair,
Where some clear spirit gently flows
Morn’s ravage to repair,
And Hebe of the glasses glows
Amid her borrowed hair.
The curse of Meroz faints away
Upon the midnight deep,
Where oysters and the rosy cray
Have heard the black stout weep,
Till lonely wheels, at dawning grey,
Pursue suburban sleep.
The curse of Meroz wakes again
When light is in the sky,
In noble thirst that longs to drain
The vats of Bacchus dry—
A splendid curse that maketh vain
The Wowser’s awful cry.
N.S.W.
CURSE O’ MOSES.
A Cautious Pessimist.
I met McCann the other day.
A very solemn man was he.
He sadly tracked me up to say.
That want and woe .had come his way,
And he would finish in the sea.
He hated life. The end was nigh,
And someone had informed him that
By far the easiest death to die
Was drowning. Here he asked could I
Oblige a fellow with a sprat.
He felt a man was better dead;
Existence now was foul and grim.
He shed a tear-drop from his head,
And heaved a racking sigh, and said
I needn’t put on crape for him.
Nothing in life or death he feared.
Here smartly from the pave he ran.
I at his superstition jeered.
“To go beneath a ladder reared
Is dashed unlucky!” said McCa^~
VICTORIA.

WHEN THE BOTTLE COMES HOME EMPTY.
N.S Wales Government's attempt to borrow £3,000,000 in Paris has failed. And a subsequent attempt to borrow £1,500,000 from
J. Bull-Cohen has been only a partial success. Men who have lately been employed on loan works are therefore being cacked, and
labor Ministers are beginning to have a bad time of it.

How to Address Letters .
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor , but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin, is
the proper superscription for ‘'copy.” Delay, and,
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to V G f~
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.

Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused. . _ . . ~
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.

ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence' col-
umn all contributions submitted, whetlier in the

tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns m
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.

dential. . 0
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable Alo.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or

To Amateurs in Black and White .
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original
humorous or other topical drawings in line or wash,
of sufficient merit and interest and adapted for
direct reproduction, or any interesting photograph
illustrative of a current event, will be paid for same
on acceptance. Any picture containing a good idea,
but unfitted for direct reproduction — i.e., any pic-
ture which has to be re-drawn —will be paid for as a
suggestion only. The conditions under which such
artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, NOVEMBER 7, 1912.
THE BULGAR WHO DID.
Whatever the final issue of the wai now
raging in Eastern Europe may be, the Bul-
garian resurrection is something of which
nations and statesmen must take account.
At the moment of writing, ancient Thrace
is under a cloud of battle smoke, and through
the blackness and uproar a very steadfast
and enthusiastic person in homespun khaki
is chasing a flying Ottoman to the verge of
his European possessions. Long ago the
Bulgar was a mighty man of valor, with an
empire of his own which at times was strong
enough to levy tribute on Byzantium itself;
but by the beginning of the nineteenth .cen-
tury his name was mud, and in the miser-
able and blood-stained Balkan villages he
crouched in terror of the Turk, helpless,
ignorant and apparently hopeless. The
wind of liberty which stirred the Greeks
and Servians to a patriotic struggle did not
rouse him ; vet somewhere in his native val-
leys native 'thought began to find expression
in native writing, and with that literary re-
vival a new spirit awoke in the downtrodden
■peasantry who had endured the b'tter yoke
if Islam for so long. In this the Bulgarian
differed from all liis neighbors. The move-
ment for Greek, Servian and Roumanian
liberty came from without. The awakening
of Bulgaria was from within.
Five hundred years of hopeless slavery to
a barbarian master had not crushed the
fighting spirit in the nation, and to-day
there is nothing that looks so likely to bring
Turkish rule to an end as that very race
which was among the most despised in the
whole peninsula. Given no interference
d'rbin without, Bulgaria seems like taking
the Grand Turk himself by the beard and
dropping him ungently into the Bosphorus.
Moreover, interference on the part of the
Powers with modern Bulgaria has not hith-
erto been much of a success. British inter-
ference in the drawing up of the Treaty of
Berlin separated Eastern Roumelia from
the main State. Bulgaria added the separ-
ated province to its own dominions, in spite
of Turkish threat and protest, and Britain
did nothing. Russia sought to control the
infant principality in whose cause it pro-
fessed to have fought in 1877, but Bulgaria
threw off the control, and the big northern
Empire had to be content with a cold and
increasingly distant friendship. Servia
sought to interfere by pouring an army
across the Bulgarian frontier at the very
time when Turkey had massed its battalions
in the south ; but Prince Alexander led his
ill-conditioned and badly-equipped soldiers
against the invader, and in the battles of
Slivnitza and Tsaribrod smashed his enemy
on his own ground. A country which does
things in that way does not look good to in-
terfere with, and the chances are that if the
Turk can be driven out, Bulgaria will do it
and ask leave of the Powers afterwards.
The phenomenon of the new nation of
warriors which is thus presented is one worth
a lot of study from the Australian stand-
point. The Bulgarian has known his
enemy, known that sooner or later a war to
the death must be fought, and has been mak-
ing ready for the crisis when it should come.
When the hour struck he went out without a
moment’s delay, and took his enemy by the
throat. Bulgaria is about equal in popula-
tion to Australia, but has the immense ad-
vantage of having a small, compact and
mountanious territory to defend. Australia
has an immense empty continent, but in-
finitely greater wealth and resources. The
lesson of Bulgaria, whether her success con-
tinue to the end or not, would seem to be
that a small nation really fit, ready and
determined, can still give a good account
of itself against a much bigger foe. There
ought to be hope in that knowledge for a
country like Australia, which may at any
time be called to defend itself against the
armed and drilled hordes of Asia. Despite *
our larger area and enormous seaboard, ear-
nestness of purpose, foresight and thorough
preparation might enable us 'to meet an
enemy in the gate, or, what is still better,
make the enemy think twice or even three
times before he knocks at the gate. Unfor-
tunately, Australia’s political leaders seem
to imagine that a few patriotic words and
some citizen soldiers several thousand miles
from the danger-point are sufficient force
with which to resist the invader. Fit, ready
and determined we might win through ; but
the Government which has the matter m
hand seems determined that we shall be none
of these things. Also, the Bulgarians had
a railway to . take them to the trouble ;,while
Australians will, on the look of things, have
nothing better than a dispirited camel where-
with to get to the front. And it will be a
pretty ugly front by the time the camel gets
there.
THE CIVIC PLANK THAT REALLY
MATTERS.
Most of the candidates for the forthcoming
Sydney City Council elections started their
campaigns this week. The vast majority of
them base their claims to the support and
assistance of a grateful populace on the
number of drains they have had attended to,
the assiduity with which they have caused
the corpses of dead animals to be shifted
from the public view, or the particular line
they have taken in regard to the enlarge-
ment of this or that street. The wider
vision is not theirs. They are almost
unanimous in regarding their bloated heri-
tage, not as one vast city, with the problems
inseparable therefrom, but as an agglomera-
tion of tiny hamlets. They are the proto-
types of those State Rigliters who refuse to
see Australia,, or, indeed, anything whatever
beyond the particular parish which they in-
habit. The' tragedy from the point of view
of Sydney—a tragedy, too, which is being
intensified with every year that legislation
and the State’s railway policy causes the
vast place to expand—lies in the fact that
in this attitude about 90 per cent, of the
inhabitants are in enthusiastic accord with
their civic representatives. Hardly any of
the citizens really appreciate the discomfort
and squalor and dirt and insanitariness in
which they live. This is due partly to lack
of imagination; partly to The absence of any
handy city run on progressive lines which is
capable of supplying a standard of com-
parison; but more than anything to the
lamentable local habit of acclaiming Sydney
as the fairest and best thing that the Com-
monwealth has evolved. That is a delusion
which it is up to the genuinely public-
spirited resident of the infamously-planned
and rottenly-administered town to dispel
with all his might.
As this paper has been remarking for the
past many years, the first thing Sydney
wants, if it is to,get into line with the rest
of the great cities of the civilised world, is
the underground railway service which it
does not possess, and which, under its ex-
isting system, it is never likely to possess.
It wants the harbor bridge,- which lias been
mentioned encouragingly by successive Gov-
ernments for the past half-century, and re-
ported on by innumerable and costly Com-
missions. Also, it wants wider better-paved
and cleaner city streets and footpaths;
suburban roads, which more • resemble roads
and less suggest convulsions - of Nature;
municipal wash-houses, and at least one
municipal Turkish bath; municipal meat,
vegetable, fruit and other markets, on the
lines of the fish markets established when
Aixen Taylor was consul: and municipal
lodging-houses—a particularly vital require-
ment if the Government continues to intro-
duce indigent immigrants, while doing all
within its power to intensify the house
famine. It would probably be better for a
system of municipal telephones, and if drinks
get much higher in cost and lower in qual-
ity, it might turn approvingly the idea
of municipal pubs. A central civic Parlia-
ment should control the whole of the city’s
essential services—the water supply, the fer-
ries, the gas supply and the street cars, as
the councils of up-to-date towns like Glas-
gow and Berlin do. Uniform traffic regula-
tions, under which the casual nuisance or
calamity in charge of a dray or what not,
who lumbers about on the wrong side of the
road, would be leapt on violently, regardless
of whether he offiends in George-street, City, or
the Avenue, Newtown, and uniform street-
cleaning methods, whereby the same effective
and modern implements, and the same com-
petent employees would operate from the
Circular Quay to Botany, are likewise urg-
ently required. These, and a heap of other
innovations, are not so much called for as
howled for. The trouble is, so very few
people hear the uproar ; and, anyhow, as
aforesaid, nothing could be done if it were
audible to the entire world.
The only possible panacea is the institu-
tion of a Greater Sydney. Sydney has not
the remotest hope of getting the State to do
for it those things which are, in their
essence, its own concern. The present State
Government, like every previous Govern-
ment for years past, gathered up the reins
of office with a welter of glozing promises in
regard to immediately embarking on the con-
struction of a North Shore bridge. Money
has been “blewed,” with almost _ criminal
freedom, in a thousand other directions since
then. A herd of expensive public works,
some successful, some dismally otherwise,
has been set going. And now the money has
petered out, and the overseas pawnbroker
has closed his till with a bang. There is
not even enough cash available to pay for
the enterprises that fare already in train.
The usual stage has arrived where the Min-
ister who spoke seriously of getting to work
on the bridge or underground schemes, would
be examined sympathetically by his colleagues
for further signs of lunacy. That stage
always, arrives with N. S. Wales ad-
ministrations sooner or later; the only
thing that distinguishes the present
case from its predecessors is the fact
that it has arrived more suddenly. than
usual. In any circumstances, no Gov-
ernment, affluent or semi-insolyent, is ever
going to dare to provoke the hostility _of
country constituencies by doipg anything
considerable for Sydney. It must tackle its
own problems, or be prepared to remain, for
all time, the ugly, dusty, uncomfortable,
badly-served Early-Victorian institution that
it is. But it can never tackle anything m
a serious comprehensive spirit so long as its
administration is split up among between
40 and 50 jealous and warring and occasion-
ally bankrupt municipalities. It follows
that the first plank in the platform of
every honest and intelligent reformer
must be the wiping-out of these unprofitable
survivals and the substitution of a central-
ised council with the largest sort of powers.
As long as such a body remains the un-
realised ambition of a comparatively few
folks whom the good, easy business man is
disposed to look upon as futile 'cranks, so
long will Sydney’s transport jservices con-
tinue tp be subjects of jest and scorn to
overseas visitors, and so long also will its
filthy streets go on ruining health and pro-
perty, what time its unspeakable roads
make vehicular travel a species of. costly
purgatory. Sydney needs unification as
urgently as the Commonwealth does and for
an analogous reason; because, namely, all
those reforms which are most vital to its
welfare are impossible while it-clings to the
old. played-out forms of government. Mean-
while. the electors worry hardly at all about
a matter which is of the first importance to
every one of them; and their chosen repre-
sentatives, and the persons who hope to be
their chosen representatives, turn from a
consideration of all the larger issues and
blither hard about the dead dogs which they
have buried in the past, and which they
will bury again if a discerning democracy
accord them another term of municipal
power.
A SERMON ON THE ADVISABILITY
OF BEING THOROUGHLY STUPID.
The discourse of “10-2-4” :
This is the day of the handy pocket digest. Ex-
perts are prepared to tell the world, in pamphlet
form, the way to do most things, from writing blank
verse to boxing, and from dry-farming to spotting
winners at the races. Presently, without a doubt,
we shall have a small manual, containing potted, yet
comprehensive, instructions as to how to succeed in
politics. And if the author has any glimmering of
knowledge of his subject, Rule I. will certainly be:
Never get ahead of your time. Australia is littered
with the political remains of men who saw too far
into the future. Its multitudinous Parliaments are
so many Golgothas of keen, progressive brains. Apd
they are the pleasaunces of the good, honest, com-
placent and blastiferously stupid citizens who are
only capable of seeing the merits of ,an idea when it
has developed into a platitude and a truism. The
case of B. R. Wise, K.C., affords one., of the most
horrible examples on record of the folly-of .moving
out of the ruck. Intellectually, he is among f.the
half-dozen best men in the Commonwealth to-day.
He is an admitted master of the English language. ,
He holds a secure place at the top of his profession..
A list of his attainments and' achievements on the ■>
mental side would be a formidable affair indeed.,,
. Yet such is the detestation in which his name is
held among local Conservatives that they only em-
ploy him professionally when they must. No con-
ceivable anti-Labor party would give him a Parlia-
mentary nomination. And, since there are numerous
points on which he does not see eye to eye with
Labor, he is a species of political outcast; and the
country loses the services of a unique brain, and of
a man of education and of vast experience of affairs
and of the world. Wise’s alleged offence in the eyes
of the worthy little burgher who, in a muddled,
feverish manner talks the politics which he has
cleaned from the daily paper that morning, in
his favorite bar or tea-shop, is a matter mainly of
“unreliability.” It has never been possible to de-
pend on him to do or say the obvious thing. In a
community where fully 90 per cent, of the business
and professional men are tirelessly employed in their
leisure moments propounding, with perfect gravity,
and as though they were brand-new thoughts, ideas,
social and political, that have the moss of eld, not to
say the decay of the grave, all over them, such conduct
is reprehensible, in fact loathsome. To some it ap-
pears revolutionary, to some unchristian, . to others,
disloval to Throne and Empire. The writer well re-
members hearing a roomful of Sydney lawyers, im-
porters, doctors and so on, denounce, it might be
said unanimously, the Australian navy project as
“an insult to the Flag”—meaning the emblem whose
honor Admiral Kino-Hall, that enthusiastic advo-
cate of an Australian navy, upholds in these parts:
and he recalls when Old Age Pensions were regarded
as the last cry or howl in the way of pernicious
Socialism. * *
Amongst the well-to-do classes of Australia, the
views which to day are regarded as anarchic and
mad and immoral, will to-morrow be accepted as
eminently sound and reasonable. That is an in-
evitable rule. There is no applause nowadays tor
the State Frights fanatic who vapors about ' taking
up arms against the Federation.” Freetrade is dead,
and “Anti-Socialism” has degenerated into a J°k<‘-
Opposition to universal service—the scheme whicn,
but a year or two ago, used to be referred to witn
triumphant scorn as “conscription” and an un-
English interference with the freedom of the sub-
ject”—only survives amongst a few half-dementeu
folks. And so it is with most of the notions whicn
have won Wise his unpopularity. It seems strange
to us moderns that as recently as 20 years ago
trade unionism . was spoken of in right-thinking
circles in much the same language as is often used
regarding Socialism to-day. It was represented as a
menace to property, liberty and religion. After the
maritime strike in 1891 the victorious employers
girded up their loins and prepared, in the name ot
“freedom of contract,” to crush Unionism utterly.
Wise wrote to the papers deprecating the attempt as
a step that was both foolish and hopeless. He men-
tioned that freedom of contract implied freedom on
both sides; that Unionism was to the worker what
Capital was to the employer, inasmuch as it enabled
him to wait, if need be, till he was offered a fair
market for his goods ; and, in general, he stated what
everybody novv unquestioningly accepts. In the
United kingdom such views had been rife since
Thornton first propounded them in 1870 ; and from
the time Mum adopted them in 1875 they were ac-
cepted, among educated people, as the veriest of
common-places. However, they hadn’t yet .arrived in
the commercial circles of Australia. In fact they
provoked stupefaction and’ a perfect frenzy of dis-
approval. Briefs were torn from the utterer of them
by main force. It was suggested that he should be
expelled from his clubs as a fomenter of lawlessness
and disorder. Of course he was nothing of the sort.
His entire crime or error lay in the fact that he
had seen and rashly mentioned, in 1891, what the
vast majority of his associates were not going to
drop to till 1911, or thereabouts.
It was the same, broadly speaking, with every
acutely controversial question which came up for
discussion thereafter. When Federation was im-
minent, Reid, amid loud cheers from the Tories,
announced that if he had anything to do with it, the
fight over the tariff would be waged before, and not
after, Federation. Wise' urged that a saner and
more patriotic procedure would be to conduct the
tariff fight in the Australian Parliament. For this
he was ousted from his party as a renegade to the
sacred cause of Freetrade. Yet, five years later, in
.1899, practically all hands took his view, and in
their ranks was Reid himself, who said at Welling-
ton (N.S.W.): “I admit that, hitherto, I have only
thought of one thing, Freetrade. Now, with a
larger vision, I see that Federation is more im-
portant.” No one assailed him as an untrustworthy
turn-coat. He had timed his conversion too well.
In 1901 Wise, got his Arbitration Act—the same,
to all intents and purposes, that had been flung out
of the N.S.W. Council in 1900—on the Statute Books.
The uproar against it, in well-to-do circles, was
astonishing. For years the barrister, or house agent,
or attorney, or dentist,, or what not, who spoke in
terms of other than fury of the Arbitration prin-
ciple, lost business broadcast, and went in a certain
amount of peril of being signed up as a lunatic by
his relatives. How many people toj-day would seri-
ously suggest abolishing our Courts 1 of Conciliation
and Arbitration? And what sort of reception is ac-
corded to the intellectual back number or cave-man
who still blithers, in this connection, of the “folly
of attempting to control the inalienable law'of supply
and demand”? Before the Federal Government’s
Referenda proposals were put forward, Wise,' in his
book, “The Commonwealth of Australia,” pointed
out that it would be necessary, shortly, to alter the
Constitution in a drastic fashion; and when those
proposals were submitted to the electors he, con-
sistently enough, supported them. The customary
scandalised outburst occurred in the usual quarter.
Yet, after less than three years study of the possi-
bilities of. altering the Constitution' along the lines
suggested, every one of the more intelligent anti-
Laborites, from W. 11. Irvine, M.P., downwards, is
agreed that as regards Commonwealth control of
corporations, as regards the ‘New Protection, and as
regards the Conciliation and Arbitration proposals
of the' Government, nothing really hostile can be
said. They jib at the amending of the Trade and
Commerce clause alone, and on past form it is a mil-
lion pounds to a jettisoned tram ticket that, within
a very few years, they will be wondering vaguely
what Menace or Horror they were ever able to dis-
cern behind this suggestion.
Take it how or where you will, the entire career
of men of the Wise stamp sheets home the priceless
advantage of being ordinary. Fools do not suffer
the intelligent gladly. In some societies careful
provision is actually made for this idiosyncrasy- on
the part of the mentally deficient. For;..example, in
a British regimental mess it is not -good form to
discourse on art, literature, politics or'any such sub-
jects. Sport and innocuous personalities are about
the only really safe lines of discussion. To put it
shortly, the talk is deliberately levelled down to the
understanding of the stupidest person present. A
very similar condition of things obtains in Australia
to-day. Anyhow, experience shows that all a pro-
minent man has to do in .order to get himself thor-
oughly disliked and distrusted by squatters, business
men, medical practitioners and so forth,* is to voice
the opinions that have been current among culti-
vated persons here and abroad for the previous five
or six years.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Exit Turkey.
With the Bulgarian at the gate of Constan-
tinople and the Turkish Empire crumbling
in ruins before the uprising of the petty
States which were onCe crushed under the
heel of Islam, any number of new problems
are presented to the statesmen of Europe
and to the daily newspaper scribes who ex-
plain tilings to the statesmen. The Turkish
power is numerically a 'very small one, de-
spite the fact that it has again and again
caused modern Europe to lose its sleep. In
the" whole Empire directly rilled by the Ot-
toman the population is only 25,926,000, and
of these but 50 per cent, belong to Islam.
Of; the Turks themselves there are ten mil-
lions, many of whom are widely scattered
among The alien peoples of remote provinces.
In the'European section of the Empire there
are but 1,500,000, of whom nearly half are
in the capital city of Constantinople. The
problem of governing the Turk when his
empire lias been completely wiped out does
not, therefore, appear to be the stupendous
one which it has so often been represented
to be. There does not appear to be any very
sound or good reason why he should remain
in official ownership of any tract of country
vat all. Of the science of government he does
not possess the most elementary knowledge:
as a fighting man he is shown to be a back
number; and as the prize religious fanatic
of the globe, the experience of five centuries
has shown that no people of alien creed can
ever be safe under his rule. The varied and
assorted Christians of the Balkans and Asia
Minor are well enough endowed in the mat-
ter of fanaticism, but there is an essential
difference between the intolerance of Islam
and that of the various more or less Chris-
tian churches in his dominions. The Chris-
tian may slay and destroy in spite of his
creed ; the Mohammedan slays and destroys
in accordance with his. Were it not lor
bitter international jealousy there should
not be the slightest doubt about the con-
clusion of the present wrangle. For the
safety of Europe and the world, the Turk
should be outed, not merely from the Euro-

Old Men’s Pensions and Young Men’s
Thrift.
Iceberg Irvine, who grows more irritating
in his politics the further he gets away from
unqualified Toryism, makes a specialty
these times of long talks to little _ women.
To them he presents simple truths jn a de-
ceptive way—offers hot food for thought on
a cold plate, as it were. Last week he met
the ladies of the Austral Salon, Melbourne,
and deplored the “moral anaemia” of Young
Australia, who seems “anxious to have a
post to lean against—a Government post
for choice.” (Applause.) As a matter of
fact, there is probably a larger average
amount of self-reliance hereabouts than
elsewhere in the world ; but Irvine can dis-
tinguish very little of it, and he sees the
impending loss of what little he thinks we
have got. It “would be a disastrous thing,” he
told the ladies, “if a permanent system were
introduced in which boys and girls were to
grow up in the belief that whether thrifty
or thriftless, careful or careless, a benevo-
lent Government would look after their old
age for them.” It would be a disastrous
thing; but what grounds has Irvine for
dreading anything of the sort? Is he dull
enough to believe that Young Australia re-
gards an old-age pension as a lovely thing to
be looked forward to like a first prize in a
Tatt.’s sweep! If Irvine believes that
absurdity, nobody else will credit tin* gro-
tesque idea that the far-off prospect (not by
any means the certainty) of a poor half-
sovereign a week will induce Young Aus-
tralia to deliberately live a life of improvi-
dence for 50 years. The Bulletin has no
brick to throw at Irvine’s suggestion of
National Insurance; but it has a ton to
throw at those who undermine the old-age
pension system on the pretext that it in-
duces Young Australia to be unthrifty. If
the system has any effect on Young Aus-
tralia at all, it probably makes him so
terrified of poverty, that he resolves to do
his best to escape it. To the hopeless old
person, in short, the pension is a beautiful
thing; to the young person the possibilty of
needing it is mostly a nightmare.
—
University Reform.
N.S.W. Minister for Public Instruction
A. C. Carmichael puts in a word for his
bill to remodel Sydney University
As far as the principles of the Bill are concerned
they may be briefly summarised thus: (1) Abolition
of life tenure of the Senate, with provision for an
elective Senate on a wider franchise, the election
being quinquennial; (2) provision for representation
of the State upon the Senate; (3) provision for a
scheme of scholarships in all faculties, exempting the
holders from payment of all fees; (4) provision of
evening tutorial classes to students whether matricu-
lated or not; (5) provision for increasing the'endow-
ment from £lO,OOO to £20,000.
Objection is raised in the Council and elsewhere
to (1) on the grounds that an election will “turn
the place upside down.” No one denies that the
Senate has done .useful and great work in the past,
and the noble buildings, the present staff, and the
brilliant scholars turned out, are monuments to its
work. At the same time the necessity for reform
has to be faced. Since the University was instituted
in 1852, the number of degrees conferred, up to the
end of 1911, was only 3713. As presumably this
includes all degrees, and many students have gradu-
ated in several faculties, it does not mean that even
3713 students have received the University hall-mark.
William Charles Wentworth, in his famous
speech on the second reading of the University Bill
(October 2, 1849) said: “I see in this measure the
facility given to the child of every man of every
class to become great and useful in the destinies of
his country. . . .1 see in this measure the path opened
to the poor man to the highest position which the
country can afford him.' So. far from this being an
institution for the rich, I take it to be an institution,
for the poor.” How far has this prophetic vision
been realised? It may be claimed that the Senate
has made the best use of the means provided ; but a
democratic elective Senate would have so stimulated
public interest in Higher Education that the Govern-
ment of the day would have been forced to provide
whatever money was needed.
One objection to the proposals of an elective Senate
is the fear that certain present-day Senators will not
submit themselves to the indignity of an election,
and that their services would be lost to the State.
Could anything more undemocratic possibly be im-
agined ?
As regards the provision for representation of the
State upon the Senate, it is somewhat significant
that Professor Anderson Stuart (one of the pro-
fessors who proposed to shelve the Bill by referring
it to the Royal Commission), in a “Review of
University Life in Australasia,” published in 1892,
wrote: —-
“In a country where the State does so much for
primary and secondary education, it is rather anomal-
ous that it should be quite powerless in the matter
of the highest education.... It is still more anomalous
when we remember the large subsidies that Sydney
University has’ received from the State. In no other
Department does the State grant away public money
without having something to say about its expendi-
ture. Indeed, it is hardly possible that public atten-
tion will not be awakened one day to this extraordi-
nary condition of things. Then we may expect either
State support to cease, or perhaps a larger measure
of State control will be demanded than would be
good for the University, or than at present would be
accepted -as adequate.” Further on he adds: “It
seems to me that a governing body (i.e., the Senate)
made up chiefly of Fellows elected by Convocation,
but also of representatives of the teaching staff, and
of Government nominees, is the ideal constitution.”
Professor Anderson Stuart has been an ex-officio
member of the Senate since 1883. The views ex-
pressed were held in 1891. This is 1912—and no-
thing has been ‘done. The demand for Reform be-
came so acute that in 1907 Mr. Leveriiiuu was
elected for life, pledged to reform. Dr. Purser,
another “reformer,” was elected in 1909, and Mr.
Piddington in 1910. What has been done since?
Does it not show the absolute necessity for an
elective Senate ? And does not the manner in which
the permanent officers of my Department have grap-
pled with the problem of educational reform during
the past five years mark them as desirable acqui-
sitions to any Board or Senate, which has to keep
abreast of modern requirements?
Now, in regard to the proposed Scholarships.
Hitherto, State Scholarships (or bursaries) have
been tenable only in the faculties of Arts or Sciences.
The consequence is apparent. This year, for example,
there are 20 bursaries carrying remission of fees
and a money grant of £5O a year. Yet, while these
bursaries are open to all students, whether attending
State or private schools, there are only 40 candi-
dates. Whv? Because thev only give admission
to the faculties of Arts and Sciences. I propose to
make the scholarships tenable at the various “bread
and butter” faculties, such as Medicine, Dentistry,
Law, Civil or Mining Engineering, Agriculture or
Veterinary Science. The total cost of graduation in
these faculties is—Medicine. £163 4s. ; Dentistry,
£154 165.; Civil or Mining Engineering, £H 5
Bs.; Mechanical and Electrical Engineering, £ 110 9s.
These amounts are sufficient to completely discount
the aim of the University as set out by Wentworth,
and their mission will throw open the professions
to the son of the working man.
There is another phase. It is claimed that the
creation of so large a number of scholarships will
tend to depreciate the standard of entrance. I
nropose awarding 100 scholarships next year, 150
in 1914, and thereafter 200 each year. Competition is
unrestricted throughout the State, and offers equality
of opportunity for equality of intellect, irrespective
of social status or financial position. This ensures
that none but the most gifted students will benefit,
and each student will benefit in the direction of his
special qualifications. The fields of scientific agri-
culture and of veterinary science are practically
virgin soil; mining, electrical and civil engineering
offer unlimited potentialities in a new country; so
the expressed fear that the new proposals will flood
the medical and legal professions is unfounded.
The institution of tutorial evening classes in
economies, ancient and modern history and sociology,
with other sub.fects to follow, is one that must appeal
to every thoughtful democrat. The student need not
matriculate in trigonometry if he wishes to cultivate
a love for history; the student of political economy
need not pass examinations in Latin and algebra. He
will be able to attend classes just in the subjects in
which he is interested ; and, since the standard of a
nation’s education is . the sum of the attainments of
its individuals, every man who attends these lectures
will be of benefit to the State.
In regard to finance. I propose to increase the En-
dowment under the Bill to £20,000. and will be pre-
pared later, when the full number of Scholarships are
tenable, to increase it to £30,000. The Uni vers'tv
will have to forego certain fees, but it is the justly
nroud boast of the Senate that, in deserving cases,
the fees are already remitted. All our Scholarships
will be “deserving cases.” in that they constitute
the absolute pick of the State’s intelligence, but the
State will ppv an additional fi 10,000 a year to
make up for this loss in fees. This amount will be
more than adequate for the next four years: in fact,
during that term, the University will benefit to the
extent of, roughly, £20,000. When the scheme is
in full working order, with a popular democratic
Senate leading public om'm'on in the matter of
Higher Education, it would be a brave Government
that would dare to refuse an endowment necessary
for the proper carrying on of the scheme.
The Bulletin need not fear that “the shining star
of a great opportunity” will be extinguished by any
amendments that I will accept. The Government is
determined that every boy in the State shall come
into his heritage—the right to the highest education
for which his intellect is qualified.
About Public Groqr.
“Rtjrtj” rises with a bottle sticking out of
his tail pocket:—
There is a chance, however faint, of the question
of State control of the liquor traffic becoming a live
issue once- more in Maoriland. It is a reasonable
proposition that a country with M.L.’s enormous
obligations to meet in the near future might very well
get a cut out of the most profitable form of trade it
possesses. Hitherto the people of the Shaky Islands
have had no choice -of moderation given them at the
local option polls. Either they must proclaim their
satisfaction with the existing pubbery by voting
for a continuance of. the present svstem, or tbev
must vote against it. Yet the size of the drink bill
is an emphatic declaration that a very large number
of citizens use the pubs, and presumably use them
because they want facilities for getting stimulating
liquids. No opportunity has ever been given to
these more or less thirsty folk to say whether they
care for the way in which the liquids are dispensed.
If they dare to say that they don’t like the privately-
owned pub they 1 are told that they can go thirsty
altogether. ,
State control of the business, with brewing and im-
porting of liquor in the hands of a public department,
tremendous safeguards with regard to the quality
and purity of the stuff sold, and the elimination of
all private interest in sales by payment of fixed
salaries to those who do the work of distribution,
must, on the face of it, be a vast improvement on
the present system. Even a rabid Prohibitionist
might, in his lucid intervals, be willing to admit
that much. Of course, the drunker the people as a whole
became the more the State would profit, and this
would be, and has been, urged against the proposal;
but the Wowser must have little enough faith in
his own wowse who does not believe that he has
enough influence on public sentiment to prevent the
State attempting to make undue profits by keeping
the people in a state of perpetual blither. Further,
if education really makes for temperance—and every
temperance advocate declares that such is the case —
what would be wrong with devoting a proportion of
the profits of the monopoly to the ! cause of educa-
tion ? The schoolmaster is about the worst-paid
worker in Maoriland, and at the very time when least
cash appears to be available to meet the needs of the
immediate future, it is becoming more and more
evident that the . amount devoted to education re-
quires to be about doubled. If education will kill
the dr-r-ink, why not' let the dr-r-ink pay for its
own slaughter and thus cut its own throat?
The State couldn’t help standing on velvet in
a financial way, by making the experiment. If
Maoriland’s consumption of liquor were suddenly re-
duced by about half, through the change to State
control, the business would still be more profitable
than almost any other thing which the public could
undertake. At present the proposal is to put the
new issue on the ballot papers, side by side with
No-license and Continuance, and such a course seems
so reasonable, sane and altogether desirable that the
Wowser crowd will fight it to the last whisker.

Tick.
The deputation which waited on Minister
Beeby, the other day, to ask him to elimin-
ate from the N.S.W. Industrial Arbitration
Act the power to recover penalties by a
garnishee upon wages; raises the question
whether the time isn’t ripe to abolish the
whole garnishee business. There are two
things in particular to be said against it.
First, it encourages credit trade, and often
a credit trade of an exceedingly doubtful
character. The man who says that he would
lose a beau of money if he couldn’t garni-
shee is probably quite wrong. The chances
are that he would lose less than he loses
now. If he couldn’t garnishee he would
take fewer risks and make less bad debts
than he does nowadays. He probably can’t
recover half those bad debts through the
agency of the garnishee; so the garnishee,
by encouraging him to give credit, and then
leaving him in the lurch, really does him
more harm than good. The system is, any-
how, an enemy of C.O.D. principles, and
for that reason has this paper’s hostility.
Also, in the second nlace, the recovery of
any sort of debt by this process usually in-
flicts greater hardship on the debtor than he
deserves. Nobody has a right to get into
debt beyond a point from which he has at
least a reasonable chance of extricating
himself; but there are always two parties
to a bargain, and the person who gives un-
reasonable credit is at least as much to
blame as the person who seeks it. In very
many cases where doubtful business has been
unreasonably pushed, the creditor absolutely
• deserves to lose his cash, and should be pro-
vided with no special facilities for recover-
ing it bpyond the ordinary process of obtain-
ing judgment for the amount. Recovery by
garnishee is too much like destroying the
power of the wage-earner to earn any more ;
and as upon his power to earn wages his
very existence depends, the garnishee is in
the nature of a knife or gun or hangman’s
rope or something to finish and make an end
of him. Once the law in its unwisdom used
to send the debtor to gaol until his debt was
paid. As he couldn’t earn any money in
gaol, the debt couldn’t be paid, so the whole
remarkable business defeated itself; both
debtor and creditor lost by it, while the
country paid the expenses. The garnishee is
very much like that in its operation, and
the sooner it is thrown on to the scrap-heap
to join the other out-of-dates the better.

Some Old, Old Gags.
The idiotic campaign which William
McMillan, ex-M.l’., is conducting against
the taking over of Federal Government
House, in Sydney, by the N. S. Wales Gov-
ernment, has had at least one good result.
It has demonstrated the uselessness of the
classic old gags, “disloyalist!” and “pro-
Boer !” for the purposes of present-dav elec-
tioneering. For years and years the Tories
of Australia, like the Orangemen of Ireland,
have impudently adopted the Sovereign as a
kind of political patron saint, and they have
laid it down that those who did not vote
Liberal were traitors to Flag and Throne.
And, to a great extent, the swindle has suc-
ceeded. When the Australian navy move-
ment —a movement which, from the very
start, had the warm approval of the British
Admiralty—first got under weigh here, per-
sons of the McMillan type did a good deal
to delay it by representing that it was a dis-
loyalist plot to dismember the British Em-
pire. Liberal leaders, such as Joe Cook and
W. E. Johnson, seriously contended that
one of the intentions at the back of the
Nationalist defence policy was to “turn the
guns of the Australian ships on British men-
of-war.” Canting humbugs have, at various
times, claimed that “disloyalty” was at the
bottom of the Commonwealth’s policy of Pro-
tection, and that immigration restriction
legislation and the White Australia ideal
were manifestations of the same offence.
George lleid had the superb nerve to try and
make loyalty to Throne and Empire an issue
of his Anti-Socialistic campaign—a campaign
designed solely to prevent such reforms as
Federal control of monopolies and the New
Protection getting on to the Statute Books.
And there is no doubt he eot many votes by
those unworthy tactics. For upwards of a
decade it has been the regular Conservative
practice, throughout Australia, to repre-
sent any progressive or social-reform proposal
as something designed to offend and insult
the Sovereign and the Royal Family gener-
ally ; and when the bewildered author of the
measure has denied that this was so, he has
been mostly met with the utterly irrelevant
yelp of “pro-Boer!” The winter once heard
an inoffensive citizen accused of pro-Boer-
ism because he objected to the break of
gauge between N. S. Wales and Victoria, and
he has seen returned wayfarers scarified for
“disloyalty to the Crown” because they have
cast aspersions on the London climate. The
attitude that the egregious McMillan has
taken up in regard to the Federal Govern-
ment House business is no less imbecile.
And at Bathurst (N.S.W.), last week, an
intelligent crowd of electors made him and a
number of his associates wise to the fact that
the old order has changed, and that some, at
least, of the ancient gags have joined the
snows of yester-year. In regard to the evic-
tion of the Governor-General, McMillan
blithely took the customary point that those
responsible were guilty of “discourtesy to
the King” ; one McHattie, a local doctor,
“told the audience plainly that the people
who were guilty of turning the Governor-
General out were guilty of disloyalty” ; and
an elderly Liberal grocer cried wildly that
the deed amounted to pro-Boerism in its
most loathly form. It is unlikelv that the
crowd bad the least sympathy with the oust-
ing of the Governor-General —a paltry bit of
State Rights pettiness that was worthy of
the Carrtttiiers Administration, from which
the idea first emanated. But it was in no
mood to have its collective leg pulled. The
ranting Empire-savers were leapt on by what
the local papers term “an overwhelming ma-
jority.” An amendment to the resolution of
the effusively “loyal” McMillan was unani-
mously carried, deprecating
the introduction of the name of his Most Gracious
Majesty into this controversy, as calculated to place
our Sovereign in a partisan light before the people
of this State and Empire.
The Liberal defenders of Throne, Flag,
Empire, etc., passed out into the night,
doubtless feeling as though one of the main
pillars of the Constitution had given way,
causing the edifice to fall on their righteous
skulls.

A Row and its Relation to Australia’s
Futile Tariff.
A trade dispute that threatens to presently
cause a lot of bother is pursuing the
'uneven tenor of its way in Mel-
bourne. Some three months ago David
Mitchell’s cement-making employees, who
were then getting the inadequate wage
of 7s. Gd. per day, asked for a mini-
mum of 9s. Mitchell offered Bs. He
claimed to be able to prove by his books that
if he paid 9s. he would have to raise the price
of his cement, and that if lie did such a
thing the imported article would supplant
his entirely, and there would be neither Aus-
tralian cement nor Australian cement-work-
ers. As the Australian wages, low as they
are in this trade, are much higher than
British or German, and as the Customs duty
on the article is only Is. per cwt., with a 3d.-
per-cwt. preference to Britain, David
doubtless told the solemn truth. Shortly
after the said deliverance, lie closed his works
for a' few days to overhaul the machinery,
and when he desired his employees to resume
toil, at Bs. per day, they declined. Whether
this was a strike or a lock-out matters no-
thing; the fact remains that the works re-
mained idle for 12 weeks. Then the lament-
able Packer, of the Independent Workers’
Federation, perceiving a chance of at last
doing something and getting a mb for some
of the unemployables on his books, stepped
forward and offered to supply “laborers” at
Bs. per day, which offer Mitchell accepted.
One lamentable result is that the building-
trade unions have declared a boycott against
Mitchell’s cement —and that piay mean
anything up to a complete stoupage of the
construction trade of the city. The unions are
naturally cursing Packer and all his works,
and considering ways and means of squelch-
ing him and his “blackleg” union; but they
would be better employed ignoring that
bumptious by-product and turning their at-
tention to the real causes of the trouble —
the failure to grant Australian cement ade-
quate protection. Also, they would do well
to bound furiously on the men who
are primarily responsible. The employees
wanted higher wages, and the employer pro-
mised to give them, but he first asked suffi-
cient protection to preserve his industry—
and he didn’t get it. As to whether he actu-
ally needed it, a Tariff Board could deal
with that matter in an hour. As it is, there
is no protection, either for manufacturer or
employees—only a little poor cement, _ a lot
of ill-feeling and a promise of a big indus-
trial struggle. And for that the foreign-
traders in the Federal Parliament and the
Ministry are wholly responsible.
A few words from J. D. Fitzgerald:—
writing in B. 10/10/’l2, said that,
“Looking at the map of the proposed Greater Sydney,
it is a question if the business hasn’t been overdone
and turned into a sort of jest. The suggested area
extends away up the coast northwards past Narrabeen,
goes out north-west into the bush far past Hornsby,
and west beyond Parramatta.” For the information
of “H.H.” may I point out a few things: (1) The
, inhabitants of the area included in the map are
already more or less involved in the civic vortex of
Greater Sydney, and, at the present rapid rate of
building and expansion, these sparsely populated
areas will soon ,be overlapped by the house-wave.
(2) The great British municipalities have, periodi-
cally, put their boundaries further back, and have
taken into their girdle; the outside villages and towns.
Thus, Birmingham and Glasgow have had greater-
city boundary expansions within the last year; and
Bristol has had four greater-city movements. In
Germany, by Imperial rescript, municipalities are
encouraged to enlarge their territory, and to pur-
chase suburban waste land because (a) the muni-
c'pality will be able to plan the outward growth of a
city, and preserve the natural beauties ( which won Id
surely be destroyed by the jerry-building vandal),
and (b) it will be the ground owner and get the
unearned increment. (3) The producers at the outer
rim of the inhabited area of Sydney have a greater
common interest with the City of Svdney than with
the suburban councils through whose bad-roaded terri-
tory they have to pass to get to the central markets.
They are consequently nearly all red-hot Greater
Sydneyians, and should be included. (4) The in-
habited area of. Greater Sydney extends pretty con-
tinuously in'ia stream of houses to Parramatta, Guild-
ford, Cronufla, 1 Narrabeen and Dundas. It would
be to the advantage of the inner core and the outer
fringe alik'e if the area were made very wide. (5)
A bigger-Sydney Council, having territory bevond
the inhabited area, would be able to take virgin
bush for park . spaces—such lovely bush places as
those beyond Hornsby, as far as Berowra, for example.
(6) The Greater Sydney promoters are trying to do
in one bite what Glasgow, Bristol, Birming-
ham and a whole lot of other cities have
done in several bites. after an infinity of
trouble, red tape and appeals to Parliament.
Lastly, .“H.11.” has overlooked the fact that
the boundary set out in the Greater Sydney Bill
is only * for the election of a consultative convention
which is to report to Parliament; so his phrase that
this wide area is to be “ruled by the new council”
is not quite apt. The area for the election need
not be the area of the future Greater Sydney
Council, though it ought to be. But the question of
territory is really of no great importance; it is
quite overshadowed by those of franchise, and of the
powers, the finances and the amount of control to
be given over public and private monopolies.
