The Fat press discovers the worker to be
a splendid fellow on occasions like the Mt.
Lyell catastrophe, and praises his courage
and other tine human qualities with superla-
tives ; but the adulation merits a sniff. In
the-first place, public sentiment has to be
recognised as a matter of business; in the
second place, it is necessary by enthusiastic
applause to divert attention from the gentle-
manly proprietors, whose cupidity may have
been responsible for the horror. In this noble
cause public subscriptions and general hul-
laballoo about the horny-handed heroes are
extremely useful. Compare the lukewarm,
or wholly unconcerned, attitude of the same
papers when that splendid fellow, the under-
ground worker, is demanding laws and re-
forms that will serve to give security to life
and limb in the mines, with their hectic
enthusiasm over the afflicted diggers after the
mischief is done. The only time the worker
gets heartv praise is when he is dead and
incapable of presuming upon editorial gene-
rosity. Even then he has to be dead in
large quantities. The individual face-man
slain in pursuit of his calling occasions no
amount of newspaper concern, it being quite
safe for newspaper concern under such cir-
cumstances to confine itself to its old job
of insisting on the sanctity of private enter-
prise. ..4,.»
Attorney-General Hughes’s new. Patents:
Act, though aU improvement on the. old!
state of things, appears to suffer from more-
than one aperture in the fence. Under the
new Act candidates for registration as quali-
fied patent attorneys are to be examined by
a Board, that will include two members,
elected by the registered agents already prac-
tising. There are not more than 50 of ffhese-
in all. But the Act recognises ordinary solici-
tors as patent attorneys for the purpose off
electing those two members of the examin-
ing Board. Solicitors in ordinary do not
practise as patent agents. They leave that
business to men who have, specialised in-
patents. trade-marks, etc. Still, if they like,
they can be registered as .patent attorneys;
without paying fees or passing any examina-
tion. And .being legally qualified to do the-
work they'don’t try to do (because they-
recognise their inability to do it properly)„

Five or six years ago, when the Ma State’s
Government threatened to become moder-
ately honest, it was decided to establish a
Public Works Fund so that renewals of old
works and non-productive new works should
not be paid for out of loans. The Labor
Government inherited the scheme; in fact,
it thought so much of it that it was going
to make it a bigger thing by transferring all
land-sales money to it. But the Labor Gov-
ernment has got drunk on loans, and part of
the furniture it has smashed is this Public
Fund. A little over 12 months ago
the Estimates provided for a contribution
of £1 >039,065 to the works and closer settle-
ment funds. The actual contributions made
by the Government were £400,336 less than
that; and now £290,065 of even the trifle
that ‘ was paid into the Works Fund is to
be sfolen and paid back into revenue. The
immediate consequence of this somersault is
that the Labor Govenment, which was
pledged to spend borrowed money only on
pernjanent and reproductive works, has now
decided to pay for all its school buildings
out of loans, though the interest earned by
school buildings is exactly 0 per cent, per
annum.

“H.S.” on the dangerous illiterate: —
It begins to look as though the community will be
driven shortly to treat its illiterates like tigers and
jaguars. Last week an immigrant who was
unable to read or write rose up, bright and
early, and slaughtered one whose sentiments towards
him .were of a mosc friendly nature. His trouble
was a haunting impression that the other members
of the household were writing untruths to his wif§
in Ijngland about him. On Sunday a State-school
boy, ,of indifferent education and low mentality,
killed his employer in the casual fashion, that the
normal person swats a fly. His reason was. that
“the • old man had been rough on him lately.” In
comparatively recent times the illiterate was not
atnicced with constant humiliation and self con-
sciousness and bitter misgivings owing to his dis-
ability. The majority of his associates were in the
same plight as himself. To-day he is a thing apart.
The sensation of being an eternal outsider gets on
his nerves ; and unless he is a particularly well-
balanced person he is liable to contract a feeling of
resentment against society—that huge crowd, namely,
which is able to read and write. Where he is ill-
balanCed his resentment crystallises into blind hatred
of some particular member of society, and there is
a murder. By the way, Britain is supplying an
undue number of these dangerous folks just now.
According to one of the Sydney papers, Monday was
“immigrants’ day” at the Darlinghurst sessions—
three out of six prisoners called on to plead had
been assisted hither by the State. It might save a
deal'of crime if the provisions of the Immigration
Restriction Act, which were intended originally to
be applied to niggers only, were used to keep the
dangerously illiterate white man out of the Common-
wealth.
Tasmanian Assembly’s picturesque old
Sergeant-at-Arms, Avho is nearly 90, and has
been retained as a matter of sentiment these
good many years, the other day had to
vacate his chair and his ruffles and his
sword OAving to a sudden indispostion. Asked
to declare Avhat his ailment Avas, the. tottery
old chap returned it as “Anno Domini.”
South Australia is a-going it. On Tues-
day, October 29, the State Assembly author-
ised the construction of railways to cost
(with rolling stock) over £1,000,000. On
Wednesday it passed the Repurchase of
W*harves Bill, involving an expenditure of
£1,500,000. On Thursday it sanctioned the
borrowing of £343,000 for abattoirs and the
building of a railway to cost £70,000. The
average is about £1,000,000 a day. But, as
Bigjohn Forrest once exclaimed: “Pooh,
what’s a million!”
“Norbell”: Possibly Chidley, the night-
shirted gentleman whom all Sydney was
lately talking about, thought he had finished
with the Lunacy Department when it opened
the gates of the asylum and let him Avalk
out; but, if so, he was wrong. Chidley
aaturally didn’t want to go to the asylum ;
and he wouldn’t have stayed there five: min-
utes if the matter had been left to him.
Nevertheless, he hadn’t been out many days
aefore he got a bill for board and lodging:
I am directed by the Master-in-Lunacy to inform
you that the sum of £7 14s. 3d. is due by you in
respect of maintenance during detention, at the
estimated rate of 18s. per week, and to request pay-
ment of the amount at your earliest convenience.
“What’s the use of growling about it!” says
Chidley. “I do nothing, remembering an old
Spanish proverb : ‘He who washes an ass’s
head wastes his soap.’ ”

“The Quaker Girl” came back to Her
Majesty’s, Sydney, on Saturday night. The
features of the evening were the splendifer-
ous appearance of the renovated theatre and
the stirring jiu-jitsu dance that is bounded
or wrestled or fought or what-not by Ivy
Schilling and Fred Leslie. There is a touch
of genius in this turn, to whose weird un-
usualness is added the magnificent Schilling
physique and the strength and grace of
Leslie. By the way, -how does the manage-
ment stand as regards compensation to the
loved ones left behind, if Schilling should
break Leslie’s neck? She looks the type' of
maiden who might accidentally fracture an
iron stanchion in the act of playfully fond-
ling it. Miss Grace Palotta plays Madame
Blum on less uproarious lines than her pre-
decessor did; and in appearance she is one
of the most peach-like circumstances of the
cast. Bertie Wright works as strenuously
as ever to be diverting, and the harder he
toils, and the louder he groans, the more
boresome he becomes. A Society to Coerce
Comedians into Behaving like Human
Beings will yet have to be formed by a few
progressive spirits. Higginson—modest and
self-depreciatory as of yore—is there; like-
wise the tuneful Frank Greene; and the
debonair and agile Leslie Holland. Miss
Ivy Bickford is a princess that any royalty
would be proud to have about the premises:
and, using the clearest and truest of light
sopranos, she sings the numbers which the
departed Godwin lady familiarised this city
with. Lastly, there is Miss Blanche Browne,
gracious as a flower, and with the romance
of an alleged recent matrimonial venture
dogging her every step. Altogether, a fine
show in a gorgeous setting.

“Struck Oil,” revived for the 479,328 th
time at Sydney Criterion, “appeals not alone
to the sense of the humorous, but to the
deepest feelings of Home-Like Sympathy”
(vide programme). “While its Humor is
simple and unaffected, its Pathos is equally
simple and True-hearted. One Chief Ele-
ment in the Play’s Great Prosperity is its
Constant and Unvarying Purity. Not once
in its long Career has an unfavorable Com-
ment been made upon its .'.Tone or Language,
but its Moral Composition has always been
praised.” Perhaps that explains it. On the
other hand, perhaps the success is
due to the manner in which Maggie
Moore’s vivacity overcomes her avoir-
dupois and annihilates criticism. The
play slaughters every rule of modern
play construction; and to this critic
it is of value chiefly as the original instance
in dramatic history where the phrase, “I’d
sooner beg my bread from door to door,”
occurs. The present production is note-
worthy for the John Stofel of H. It. Rob-
erts, the gaiety of Maggie Moore, the oily,
underlined wickedness of Charlie Taylor as
Deacon Skinner, and John Stofel’s mouth-
organ. “Struck Oil” is adequately staged,
and was-received with great joy on Satur-
day, the opening night.
“Ben Hur,” the great religious horse-
drama, came back to Sydney Royal last Sat-
urday. Since Sydney last gazed upon it,
Leonard Willey has assumed the brass
waistcoat and fireman’s helmet of Messala,
formerly supported by Harcourt Beatty.
Leonard is a much softer-voiced warrior—
one that would talk gently to his legionaries
even in the heat of battle; but he has the
advantage in years and inches. The play
moves at varying speeds towards a
dull, devotional end, and, judging by the
watch, some of the superfluous moaning has
been cut out to enable the audience to
escape on the right side of 11 o’clock. The
furious chariot race in Act 5 is now con-
tested by three teams instead of two; but
the third unknown is not mentioned in the
betting outside the circus, and does not
seem to be regarded seriously. Eric Maxon
wrestles in debonair fashion with the heavy
part of Ben Hur, and makes love prettily to
his meek and beautiful Esther (Dorothy
Dix). Gwen Burroughs makes a very
spirited Iras ; Eardley Turner is suave and
reposeful as the faithful Simonides; but
Arthur Styan, as the Shiek Ilderim, is
patchy, especially as regards his tan. J. B.
Atholwood’s Balthazar is an excellent old-
man study; Susie Vaughan makes a few
brief but touching appearances as the mother
of Hur; and Leslie Victor and Winter Hall
(Drusus and Arrius, the Tribune) stand
out from the procession of characters. The
incidental dances in Act 3 give. the heavy
show a touch of almost pantomime bright-
ness, but it soon passes. General impres-
sion left by “Hur” is that the flock of cap-
able artists are only there to inhabit the
scene-painters’ string of triumphs, wear the
picturesque garments, and work the stage
mechanist’s interesting props. It is an ex-
cellent entertainment for a deaf man, with-
out being a bad one for the man who can
hear.

“Cripple Creek” has not changed much
since it last rippled through the Adelplii; in
fact, there is only one noticeable variation,
and that one is for the worse. In the fourth
act, for no apparent reason, a snag, in the
ishape of a song, has appeared, possibly
caused by some subterranean unheaval. On
another page of this paper certain explosives
are advertised, and if nothing else will re-
move this song the eye of the management
is respectfully directed to that advt. Other-
wise the piece is almost refreshing after a
long series of “Girl-who-took-the-wrong-turn-
ing” melodramas. The It of the show, when
this paper made an inspection, was Ethel
Buckley, whose rendering of Maggie Mason
should satisfy that young person. A very
close second was the Tatto of Vera Spaull, a
little lady who improved wonderfully during
her “Blue Bird” season. Nellie Fergusson,
the woman who mended her ways; George
Cross, the hero; and C. It. Stanford and
Vivian Edwards, the two villains, were satis-
factory assistants in the scheme of things.

STRUCK OIL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“I cannot stand,” my uncle says,
“These grim, depressing modern plays;
Your problem dramas simply make me boil!
Who wants a play that’s woeful?
Ah, give me Lizzie Stofel —
Delightful, dainty Lizzie in ‘Struck Oil’!
“That was a play to see, my boy,
A play a fellow could enjoy !
And acted! Oscar Asche I can’t endure.
And Ethel Irving’s rotten—
These days the art’s forgotten—
But give me dainty little Maggie Moore!
“And Melba,” said my uncle, “may
Be a splendid singer, in her way;
But operas and those Italian things !
They’re simply roar and bellow —
But Maggie’d please a fellow:
There’s a toon in every song that Maggie
sings!”
I took him, to his vast delight,
To see “Struck Oil,” the opening night,
Although I felt that it would be a bore.
For Maggie, for example,
Has grown—well, rather ample ;
She’s Maggie still; in fact, she’s Maggie
More!
It was, of course, the same old play,
The product of a cruder day,
When everybody would soliloquise.
I knew that I had blundered,
And uneasily I wondered
How this old play would seem to uncle’s
eyes. • <,
But, bless you, Uncle John Avas blind
To all that hurt my modern mind ;
“Struck Oil” was Bernard Shaw for uncle’s
sort.
Though Maggie wasn’t thinner
(No more was Deacon Skinner),
Did my old uncle notice it, or snort?
There was “the paper” that was hid ;
There were “the old home” and the kid,
The flapping “front scenes” and the grim
“My God !”
And the pathos of John Stofel
Was laid on with a shovel,
And the humor—Gt was brought on in a
hod !
He sat and gazed with joy entranced
When lightsome little Lizzie danced.
The play to me was mouldy and inept:
But how my uncle’s laughter
Rang out, and rumbled after,
And when the fiddles trembled, how he wept!
For he was back in the old days,
“When plays,” he said himself, “ were
plays,”
Before the happy zest of youth had flown.
I needn’t have been frightened,
For now I was enlightened :
Tfle Maggie _was the Maggie he had knowD.
Ana as 1 sat and waited, sore,
I heard the house with laughter roar
At Stofel’s drill and Lizzie’s tomboy fun.
They laughed and wept, enchanted:
This rvas the stuff they wanted- —-
'The audience were uncles, every one!
N.S.W.

Amongst the constellations that are glitter-
ing noisily every week-night, and at periodical
matindes, within the walls of the National
Amphitheatre, the pride of place is held by
Miss Violet'Black. She and a useful com-
pany do a sketch which would go better if
the various London allusions were more
clearly understood by the multitude. Phil
and Phlora are funnier than most of their
eccentric tribe, and the Bartz Trio do some
marvellous things on what they call an in-
visible wire—that is, a wire which is well-nigh
as indiscernible as a clothes-line. That ster-
ling comedy-actor, Aubrey Mallalieu, who
has been appearing in a comedy sketch, is
in his last nights ; likewise Ward and Vere
and Tom Lee.

One of the best war pictures yet unwound
in Sydney is contained in “Lucile,” a
Lyceum production of the very lengthy
poem-novel by “Owen Meredith.” The love
story which runs through this Crimean war
yarn is well interpreted by the Thanhauser
Co. “Lumbering in Hungary” was a satis-
factory “also ran.” “The Midnight Wed-
ding” is announced for next week.
* * * *
“Get-Rich-Quick Wallingford,” hero of the
Yankee novelty that conquered Sydney and
came to Melbourne Royal on Saturday last,
is first-cousin in melodrama to “Alias Jimmy
Valentine.” Jimmy was a born burglar, who
turned honest under moral suasion and
carried his pals with him. Rufus Walling-
ford is a born wild-cat company inventor,
who flaunts himself into the lethargic town
of Battlesburg with the intention of annex-
ing all its dollars, but is turned honest by
force of circumstances. He also carries his
accomplice with him. On wings of roguery
they soar to heights of commercial respecta-
bility, and remain there as going concerns.
It is an unlovely story, but it is at least
amusingly farcical, which the tale of Jimmy
Valentine certainly was not. From first to
last, “Wallingford,” on the stage, is a bur-
lesque of the Yankee novel that inspired it.
The novelist’s hero is the typical up-to-date
Robert Macaire, passing from swindle to
swindle, and suffering a number of incidental
indignities as he proceeds, a red-faced, pim-
pled bounder of 40 or thereabouts. When at
last he gets nabbed by the Law, the man
who compounds his felony says: “You’re
only the logical development of the Ameri-
can tendency to ‘get there,’ no matter how.
It is the national weakness, the national
menace, and you’re only an exaggerated
molecule of it.” The end of Wallingford (in
the novel) is that his rescuer offers him the
chance to turn his champion impudence to
profitable account in a legitimate business
way. The end of Wallingford (in the farce)
is an assured position as Battlesbursr’s all-
round benefactor and supreme millionaire.
Heaven smiles upon his accidental refor-
mation.
* * * *
The plot bristles with bright absurdities
and redeeming points. The “boobs” are im-
possibly boobish, but their distinctive charac-
ters are neatly caricatured. With one ex-
ception, the women of Battlesburg ar: mere
brainless inducements to matrimony, the one
exception being a typist© who sits at work
in the entrance hall of the hotel, and is
sufficiently sane to have doubts of Walling-
ford’s hona-fides. But her spark of intelli-
gence flickers out when the gaudy impostor
makes love to her. Miss Josephine Cohan
plays the stenographer part as effectively as
its narrow limits permit. Miss Holloway,
Miss Wingfield, Cambourne, Edwin Lester
and others are well placed in minor charac-
ters; and seeing that Wallingford and his
accomplice, “Blackie” Daw, make no real
claim to be taken seriously, it doesn’t matter
that they are utterly unlike their originals
in the novel. Harry Clarke, as Blackie,
appears to be inviting suspicion rather than
disarming it. Fred Niblo’s Wallingford
hasn’t a suggestion of the experienced mag-
netic spieler about him. He might be a
beautiful young man from a drapery store
who had cultivated a Family HERALD-hero
style of fascinating address' by acting in pri-
vate theatricals. But the style suffices for
the part. It is a “glory” part. Walling-
ford carries everything before him. When
he frowns, you know that the brain behind
the forehead is working wonders. Also, you
feel that no misfortune could ever befall such
a head of hair.
* * * *
_ Melbourne was introduced to “Night-
birds,” at Her Majesty’s, last Saturday;
therefore, some of the already familiar melo-
dies which happen in the entertainment, but
are not in complete harmony with it, will
he hummed around town for the next few
weeks. “Nightbirds” is neither musical
play, comic opera, nor vaudeville. Its elu-
sive frivolity contains a little of each of
those things, and its ruling sentiment is
expressed in Prince Orloffsky’s toast —
“Here’s to Wine, Women and Song, and
may the Wine and the Song be always good.”
* * * *
With the exception •of Countess Cliquot,
who shows some concern for the purity of
the home when a Hungarian gentleman pro-
poses to stay with her for five days, the ladies
of this frisky narrative are no better than
the men would wish them to be. The
Countess’s parlor-maid turkey-trots and
bunny-hugs, with the Prince, in the reception
room of the New Age Prison. All the other
charmers on the bill are members of his
Highness’s private ballet, and their dancing,
in abbreviated muslin skirts, does credit to
his royal taste. Edmund Sherras, as the
potentate in question, suffers awhile from a
settled melancholy, and defies the worst
efforts of W. S. Percy to make him laugh.
Jack Cannot, Talleur Andrews and Reginald
Roberts are mostly engaged in seeking im-
propriety in the most likely places, whereas
Percy wanders up and down the foreground,
delivering yards of patter to the first person
he sees. As a rule, the small _ comedian
ranges from funny to humorous in “Night-
birds.” Andrews sings a couple of solos ad-
mirably ; Miss Arundale scores in dance, song
and dialogue; Cannot is droll as the Count
who gives Miss Florence Young much cause
for anxiety, and she sounds to great advan-
tage in the concerted numbers, whilst look-
ing her very best in a series of handsome
gowns. Also, she divests herself of a gar-
ment with great success.
* * * *
Melbourne farewelled the Rudd family on
Friday night, at the King’s, and on Saturday
Bilanderson’s company returned to the scene
of its former misdemeanors, with “The Span
of Life.” The plot careers round a tutor,
Richard Blunt, who becomes a lighthouse
keeper and goes to Zambesi, because he
loves Kate Heathcote, who is robbed of her
inheritance for the sake of the insurance,
and is only saved from penury by crossing a
chasm upon the backs of some wandering
acrobats, Avho happen to be touring Zam-
besi, which is well-known as a happy hunt-
ing ground for theatrical companies. Any-
way, it all looked like that on Derby night.
Cyril Mackay, who plays the heroic Blunt,
defies his enemies in three attitudes and four
acts. Rutland Beckett, whose villain seems
rather like a mixture of Iceberg Irvine and
the last of the dandies, is assisted in his knav-
ish tricks by one Joshua Gurnet (Charles
Brown), who is full of grog and hero-hatred.
Miss Duggan chants the lines of the girl
whose money-box was robbed for the sake of
the alimony, and Miss Olive Wilton gasps
her way through the part of the villain’s
most distressful mother. Temple Harrison
and Miss Fanny Erris are the comic relief.
The Bulletin can only suppose that they
need the money.
* * * *
“The Sins of Society,"' ac Melbourne Prin-
cess’s, still contains the perfect gentleman
of a money-lender who advances £7OOO to the
semi-heroine without even hinting at in-
terest. Lady Marion loses the sympathy of
at least half the audience when she attempts
to poison this nice-mannered gent. Times
are not quite what they were, it remembers,
and the best of us may want a kind uncle
at almost any moment.

Encased in a gown which beggars mascu-
line description, and with her osprey feather
floating on high like the banner of Romance,
Margaret Cooper nightly coaxes smiles of
delicately modulated amusement from all
who call in , at Melbourne Athenaeum, be-
tween 8.15 and 10.30 p.m., or thereabouts.
The tall, masterful-looking charmer has been
drawing first-rate houses to the Collins-
street hall, which is mostly devoted to flower
shows, lectures on the human inside, and
similar impertinences. •
* * * *
“Well, here I am, as fat as ever,” was the
way Miss Florence Baines introduced herself
to Opera House patrons, on Saturday, this
being a variation of the lady’s old jest when
she was in here some years ago, with “Miss
Lancashire, Limited.” Apart from this joke,
Miss Baines provides a fairly bright perform-
ance. She exploits her rather thin voice and
exuberant personality in a number of “imi-
itations” of music-hall characters—which
suggests to the present critic that there
should be money for Maggie Moore in a vau-
deville stunt on similar lines. Bert Bilton.
another newcomer, fails to commend himself
to a section of the house, which prefers a
comedian who wears a red nose and can
turn back-somersaults, to one who sports a
biled shirt and plays his own piano accom-
paniments. Some more new arrivals are
Taylor and Arnold, whose “musical non-
sense” sometimes sounds like a sort of rag-
time Lewis Carrolling. This turn so appeals
to the audience that they forget that it is
possible to have too much even of a non-
sensical thing. Other good turns are pro-
vided by the acrobatic Max Franklin troupe
and Thos. Bentley, Dickens impersonator.
* * * *
A three-act circumstance at West’s Mel-
bourne house last week was “A Fatal Dance,”
the usual story of a passion that begins in
sweetness and ends in slaughter, and has
much amorous incoherence in between. The
plot follows a rut worn to the size of a
canyon by the multitude who have had
designs on the Seventh Commandment; but
it provides opportunities for a Danish
actress, Asta Nielsen, who plays the chief
part with grace and blandishment. More
sensational fare was “The Inner Circle,” a
fiction of bombs and a Black Hand plot,
with the Long Arm of Coincidence working
overtime. Two other features on the bill
were “The Missing Finger” and “A Study
in Faces”; so it is more West’s than the
writer’s fault if this par. concludes more like
a report of an anatomical demonstration
than a passing reference to a picture show.

St. Kilda (Vic.) now finds a refuge from
its sadder thoughts in the entertainments
of the Dandies, who occupy part of the
Esplanade, and loudly announce themselves
by electric signs. There are about 10 or a
dozen Dandies, their commander-in-chief
apparently being one Calvert, a consistently
melodious performer who can deliver a song
like “Parted” without spoiling the arrange-
ment of his features. A similar artist is
Renn Millar, who in “My Steed and I” dis-
plays a good-enough voice that is never per-
mitted to run to waste. Harry Graham is
declared to be the humorist of the co.; while
Arthur Mack, who warbles ditties like “Nora
Malone,” sports a green hat to top-off the
innuendo that he is specially expert in Irish
character. Of the Danderinas, Misses Tessa
Byrne, F. Henderson and Dorothy Manning
are all vocally attractive ladies whose smiles
are triumphs of modern facecraft. Miss
Manning has also in her repertoire a start
of surprise, which she used with great effect
last Friday, when she debuted and took de-
livery of large consignments of foliage.

and the accidental finish of the female in

Adelaide Royal has made a quick change
from the brisk and snappy humor of Ameri-
can politics in “The Woman” to the Hiber-
nian humor and sugary melody of Allen
Doone. The new man arrived from Mel-
bourne in a big motor, and found the Ade-
laide plain and hills worth exploring. The
Tivoli has said good-bye to the marvellous
Schenks, and is welcoming a batch of people
mostly new to Australia. The King’s puts
up a strong bill, with certain Bohemians
(who include the King’s College Boys) re-
sponsible for the first part of it.. Joyous,
teams are getting frequent again with the
coming of summer. Arcadians are at Henley
Beach, and at the Exhibition Branscombe
presents the very popular Dandies, includ-
ing the Dampiers and George Morgan-
very much the team of two years ago. West s
pictures are dragging in a lot of unusual
patrons with the fine Queen Elizabeth and
Essex drama —English history seen through
French eyes, and brilliantly played by the
only Sarah. The other films mostly rely on
the Mount Lyell disaster.
“Busk”: Martin Harvey comes to Australia shortly,
with his own company. In stating this a few weeks
ago, he mentioned that one strong card would be
“The Playboy of the West” ; but since then he has
expressed a doubt whether he will play the piece
any more. Rival comedians named Fay and O’Doherty
have done good business with it in America, but it
has roused a fearful hubbub amid the Irish. It
seems to be in the Handy Andy vein, depicting Pat
as a bungler with two left hands. Yet the London
Times and Pall Mall Gazette (edited by Irishman
Garvin) declare it is a first-rate play, worthy to be
placed beside the current London successes of
“Bunty” and “The Milestones.” I fancy Harvey
will experiment with it in Sydney and Melbourne,
remembering how Pat told the Piper, “Play me the
Byne Wather jintly. and see how I can shtand it.”
AT POVERTY POINT.
Drury Lane had a centenary the other day. That
is, the present building, which was opened on Octo-
ber 10, 1312. There were two others previously on
the same spot. The new shop started with riots and
alarms. A prize had been offered for the best
poetical address to be delivered on the open-
ing night. None of those sent in were con-
sidered good enough, so Lord Bacon was com-
missioned to write one, which he did. The
115 rejected poets were furious. They turned
up night after night and attempted to recite
their poems to unwilling audiences, only to
be ignominiously chucked out. For a long
time nobody could make money out of the
place. Everything was tried—-tragedy, com-
edy, farce, grand opera, circuses, masked
balls, performing lions, etc. E. T. Smith,
father of Melbourne’s L.L., held on longest.
It was under his management that G. V.
Brooke appeared there for the first time.
Augustus Harris was the first to make the
Lane pay. He started management with
George Rignold as his bright particular star.
George R. brought good luck to the house,
and it has been making money ever since.
Coincidence once more. Charles Frohman, who has
captured half a dozen London theatres, commissioned
Pinero, Barrie and Bernard Shaw to write each a one-
act play for the Duke of York’s, with a view to pre-
senting a record triple bill. Each dramatist wrote
without knowing what the other two were doing.
To his astonishment Frohman found that Pinero and
Barrie had hit upon the same theme, and had worked
it out on practically identical lines. A thunder-
storm played an important part in both pieces, and
as no audience could stand two thunderstorms in one
evening, Barrie went to his cupboard and produced
another piece of confectionery.
A few Randolph Bedfordisms from “The Unseen
Eye,” now attracting theatregoers to the Sydney
Palace: “There isn’t any devil. If there was, you

11. B. Irving will soon be off for a 12 months’ tour
of S’Africa. Mrs. Irving (Dorothea Baird) is not
accompanying hubby. She “finds the strain of acting
too much for her nerves, and has determined to retire
from the stage.” ' S’Africa has all the luck.
“Avon”: I look forward to the Melbourne
Shakspeare Society’s forthcoming production of
“The Tempest.” G. Rignold was the last
to act Caliban in Melbourne. He had made a suc-
cess of the part at the Queen’s (London ), under the
management of Henrietta Hodson (afterwards Mrs.
Labby), in the era when Irving, Toole and Ellen
Terry were engaged at that house. Brooke acted
Prospero in Melbourne, with Heir as Caliban. With
Rignold, Cathcart was Prospero, and Miss Simmonds
took Ariel. Charles Kean’s London production was
about the most sumptuous of all that he did at the
Princess’s. Bob Brough acted Caliban in Liverpool.
Recent London productions have shown Beerbohm-
Tree and Benson as Caliban. Neither good. Asche
would be great in this weird r6le.
“Reka”: The early-door mania in Auckland
(M.L.), mentioned by “Kohu,” developed even more
madly during the latter part of the Asche season.
The booking queue began to form at 9 p.m., and a
party of idiots with camp-stools and provisions pre-
pared for an all-night wait. A coffee-stall drew up
alongside in anticipation of business, and kindly
friends homeward bound from the theatres looked in
and spoke encouragement. The booking office opens
at 9 a.m., and after their all-night vigil many of the
bookers did not get in until nearly 11. Which was
a better fate than they deserved.
“II.”: When Sarah Bernhardt was playing in Aus-
tralia, books of the plays, with the French words on
one page and the English words opposite, were
obtainable at a bob each. The simultaneous flick, as
the leaves of several hundred books were turned, was
a feature of the performance. The gods, who were
not particularly occupied in following the French
words, grasped the situation early. With their eyes
fixed on the books beneath, they were ready for the
turning of each page, and augmented the rustle with
a “fer-lick” from above. Sarah did not always stick
to the book, though. Playing in the draughty old
Adelaide Royal, she remarked in the middle of a
tense dramatic situation that she wanted thijit (force-
fully condemned) door shut. And the book-holding
followers of the drama searched madly after the
strong words which were not there.

( Founded, rhymes and all, on the best vaudeville
models.)
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Down Macquarie-street, in the Su-mmer’s heat
Strolled a big fat love-sick coon;
There was lamps alight, for it was at night,
An’ dere was al-so a moon;
As he saw his babe, standing in the shade,
In her low-cut gown so gay,
This big heavy coon stood beneaf de moon,
And unto his turtle dove he then did sa-ay:—
Baby—you are ma lady—
Oh, come and hear me —I’se gwine to speak;
I adore you—Ah’s voted for you—
And Ah’s found you—a good safe seat;
Fair, dear —I hope we’ll pair, dear,
For Ah want you—for all ma life;
I’m starred —-for fame in Hansard:
Oh, come and hear me —rthe time is ripe.
But his dusky babe —didn’t li-ke his trade,
Although he’d caught her eye;
And she told him soon—that he needn’t croon
When all de nigs was passin’ by ;
She was feared she’d find —that he’d talk her blind ;
And his House was hot and dry,
And she’d rather go—to a real good show,
Though eb’ry night this coon might yell and cr-y:
Baby—you’re sweet as pay-day ;
Your voice is like a—division bell;
When you’re near —I’ll always jaw, dear:
On eb’ry subjec’—some fac’s Ah’ll tell ;
You’ll fix—all ma statistics,
And read ma speeches—both soon and late;
That form —-must share ma platform:
You’re ma selected ca-andidate.
N.S.W.

THE MARTYRS
The Women’s Social and Political Union held a
demonstration at the Albert Hall last night, when
300 women, who had suffered imprisonment, occupied
the platform attired in white.— Gable.
“Briner” : Capt. Garside, chairman of Melb.
Marine Board, just back after a world-
wander, left with lofty ideas about the local
port’s up-to-dateism. He returns with a
“Shattered, Sir.” He did not expect Mel-
bourne to be quite up to London, Liverpool
or Southampton, but surely it would hold its
own against Durban or Capetown. It
doesn’t. At Durban and Capetown they have
bluestone or granite docks; Melbourne has
makeshift timber piers. In his whole trip
he didn’t anywhere else see one big steamer
berthed against a wooden pier; and. even
worse, he found the local port behind even
second-rate ports in the matter of electric
cranes and hoists. And nowhere else did he
see an important landingplace not served by
railroad or tram.
“Bosun’s Mate”: N.S.W. Marine Court
had before it last week two shipmasters —
one who tried to hurdle the steamer Rock-
lily across the bar of the Nambucca river
(North Coast), and another who endeav-
ored to plough Our Elsie inward over the
Bellinger river (also North Coast) —and
the .eminent tribunal shook its head sadly
and regretted the damphoolishness of the
coastal skipper when he wants to be a
marine steeplechaser. But the Court didn’t
have the circumstances placed before it in
the plain, bald-mannered way which I pro-
pose to adopt. Here is a skipper outside one
of these bar harbors with the signals
at “Dangerous.” Inside there is about
1,000,000 ft. of timber waiting to be lugged
to Sydney ; also, 1000 pigs for the high-price
market; 1000 boxes of butter for tranship-
ment at the bloated city all deteriorating in
the sun; 300 cases of fish, and so on. There
is a nasty lump on the bar with a sus-
picious northerly set, but the man in charge
of the approaching hooker knows that the
stronger considerations are the crowd of
impatient sawmillers, storekeepers, cocky
agents and fishermen on the wharf, the 10
tons of ice for the fish, the perishable gpods
for the storekeeper, and a family at home
to support. Naturally, the skipper, unless
the odds are 100 to 1, risks it. Ninety-nine
times he gets in all right; the 100th, his
ship is bowled over on top of the break-
water, and a scandalised Court asks him
didn’t he see the flag. In the cases last week,
counsel for the defence plaintively urged
that the ship “had to make its trio.” a
solemn enough truth. Judge Backhouse at
first fell heavily on this argument, but list-
ened to it finally and let off both skippers
with a nominal sentence and a severe lec-
ture. He stated that the next mariner who
came to grief against the signals would
hear from him in a way that would surprise
him. Meanwhile, the skipper who wastes
time and money hanging about for a safe
bar will hear from “the boss” in a way that
will not surprise him in the least. So the
harassed coasting skipper is between the
deep sea and two devils—which is a trifle
over the odds.

“complimentary social” —music, speeches and

“Te Babarantiko”: Concerning Canon
Garland’s utterance (B. 24/10/’l2) that
the Timor rebels were equipped with modern
rifles. I happen to know that in the rebel-
li°n last year on Ponope, Carolines, when
the Governor, the secretary and two other
Government men were killed (the natives
bashed in the Governor’s head and left it at
that, but the secretary’s hands
and nose were cut off, a rope
tied around him, and he was
dragged around the island till
he died), modern breechloading
Winchesters were plentiful
among the supposedly disarmed
or unarmed natives. Most island
men are well aware that the
Japanese supply the natives
with firearms. Once, a Jap
vessel landing goods was sus-
pected of gun-running and was
searched. Cases filled with
arms were discovered; but as
the Japs were not caught land-
ing arms, and as they naturally
declared the arms to be for some
other place, nothing could be
done._ The Japs are graduallv
working their way into the
Archipelago. They are cheap,
and, therefore, in demand. In
connection therewith, some
whites occasionally get what is
due to them. Thus a Ponope
resident a while ago bought a
schooner badly in-need of re-
pairs. As he could get the
work done very " cheaply at
Yokohama he sent the ship There
m charge of a Jap skipper with
Jap crew—cheaper than the
available white skipper and
mate would do it with Kanaka
crew The vessel was repaired
at Nippon and was duly dis-
patched for the Carolines. It has not turned
up yet. The general opinion is that it was
jieaclecl north, and is now engaged in the
lucrative sealing and raiding trade.

Burst of enthusiasm from Melbourne: —
Norman Lindsay’s exhibition, just opened in the.
Oentreway here, has, as usual, put the daily
paper critics in an awkward position. For the sake
of their reputations as critics, they dare not with-
hold praise; yet the infuriated hag Respectability
forbids entire approval. The exhibition is extra-
ordinarily interesting. ,Of the 150-odd pictures, two
alone, “How Pantegruel Found Panurge” and “The
Argument,” should make the reputation of any man.
The former ( £l5O 10s., and cheap at the price), is a
wonderful example of Lindsay’s work. A Rabelaisian
subject, handled in a masterly manner; remarkable
for its grouping and extraordinarily skilful detail.
The scene is crowded with figures, each possessing
an individuality, plus the personality of the artist.
The feet and calves of Pantegruel loom large in
the foreground, and about them are children scrap-
ping, lovers loving, a woman suckling' her babe, and
a delicious little Norman Lindsay kid picking a
butterfly to pieces. Then there are 43 Petronius illus-
trations. Wonderful technique is displayed in these :
drawings, especially in the way the artist produces
his tone and color effects by the most delicate pen-
work. The female figures arrest attention. Lindsay’s
women have been labelled “coarse,” but there is no-
body like him for depicting those splendid animals—
women like Helen and Cleopatra—who belong not to
an age, but to all time. He shows us the mother-
of-warriors type, who, like lole, ask but the privi-
lege—
“.... to bear sons unto my lord,
To bring lives like a vassal to my king,
To feel the gums of heroes at my breast,
And mighty poets drink their fill of me.”
In one of the Petronius drawings, “The Chase,” the
female face at the window provides us with a glimpse
of woman through the ages, from Eve, of Eden, to
La lace, of Little Lon. The exhibition includes many
delightful humorous sketches, dozens of bear draw-
ings and one rich and vital water-color, “The Pirate,”
which was reproduced in The Lone Hand, and makes
one wish for more work of this class from this artist.
“N. or M.” : Australia’s first experiment
in flag-flapping occurred in the ’4o’s of last
century. The unfledged States formed an
Australian League to protest against any
more convict, and displayed a blue flag with
a Southern Cross alongside the Union Jack.
Maoriland endorsed the movement with en-
thusiasm, Sydney imported its last shipment
of undesirables by strategy, and' Van Die-
men’s Land went one better and sent its
last consignment back unopened. Denison
was at this time at the head of affairs in
Tasmania. He fought against the League
tooth and nail, and looked with an embittered
eye on the five-starred flag floating above
T. D. Chapman’s office opposite Government
House. Chapman, a
leading Parliament-
ary light of the time,
was President of the
League, and his rela-
tions with Denison
were hot percept-
ibly improved when,
sallying out from his
den at the head of his
clerks and em-
ployees, he rescued the
Governor and his car-
riage from a moo that
had been expressing
its sympathies. The
Neptune, which
brought John Mit-
chell to Tasmania,
was, I believe, the
vessel that was
turned back in its
tracks. It had on its
voyage out been de-
layed five months at
Capetown, where the
attempt to land pri-
soners almost turned
the white settlers
black with fury. The
colonists instituted a
boycott, tolled minute
bells, hung their towns
with crane, cut off
supplies from all who
were in any way con-
nected with the Neptune, prayed, denounced,
harangued and raised Cain generally; so
the Colonial Office ordered the Neptune to
move on. -- •

SALT O’ THE EARTH.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
According to the Paris Matin, woman is man’s in-
ferior because of the small percentage of chloride of
sodium in her blood. This, the French journal gives
out as scientific fact. In other words, says the
Matin, the blood of the male is more salt than that
of the female, and observation of animal life shows
that the more salt there is in the blood the higher
the intelligence and general development. Woman, it
is declared, is inferior to man in everything—in-
telligence, reason, and physical force. —Daily paper.
Because the salt is in his blood
He does the finest things ;
He leads by earth, and air, and flood ;
The finest songs he sings;
Though Woman fights for “equal rights”
And cries for “equal pay,”
Quite well she knows the way he lights,
x\nd all her little battles fights,
In his big, mannish way.
The jay !
The strong, good-natured jay!
Because sh'b hasn’t got much brine
Save that behind her eyes,
She makes him walk the straight chalk-line
Until the day he dies ;
A few salt tears, and all the salt
That makes of Man a king
Is hers until the white stars halt
In their great march across the vault
Where lady seraphs sing.
Dear thing!
She owns Barth’s salted king!
He slaves for her because she’s not
The slightest bit like him;
Of course, it’s sentimental rot—•
But what is Beason’s glim,
When Love’s own flambeau ’tis that guides
This old World on its beat?
She is the weaker one ; besides
(This honeyed fact the Salt King hides),
She’s very, very sweet!
Yes, sweet!
She wins because she’s sweet!
N.S.W.

At the end of last week, the cables dashed
and dotted the news that the Turk had had
his fez bashed over his eyes, and that the
heard of the Prophet was about to he used
to stuff the Balkans’ sofa-cushions. At the
tail of the news appeared the usual message
about the elaborate atrocities performed by
the thrashed and retreating Unspeakable.
The Turk has probably never run so far and
so fast as he has in the present fiasco. But,
though hurried and harried by victorious
armies, he has always found time to do
a little disembowelling and eye-gouging.
The ordinary kind of barbarian would
not trouble about these dreadful ex-
tras of warfare once he was well
started on the road for home. But the
Turk’s devotion to the outrage business is so
great that he must linger to do his dreadful
ittle jobs, even at the risk of getting a
sword-thrust between the shoulders. He is
an incurable devil, is Atrocity Pasha ; and
none the less dreadful because, as a rule, the
moral odor of his antagonists isn’t exactly
violets.
* * * *
The Hindoo, usually regarded as one of
the dirtiest sores on the face of the earth, can
teach Australia one lesson in cleanliness,
anyhow. When his mother-in-law’s or any
one else’s corpse is left on his hands, he
doesn’t bury it three or four feet deep in the
middle of the town, where it will help to
kill several more of the population. Instead,
he does the right thing by taking it to some
handy nlace and cremating it. At Lismore
(N.S.W.) recently Dicky Harman Singh
died, and the Hindoo population took half a
day off to show the allegedly sanitary Aus-
tralian how the thing should be done. Next
day the ashes and teeth were collected : the
former were thrown into the river ; the teeth
were sent to India to be heaved into the
holy Ganges.
* * * *
According to a Dago merchant of these
parts, the modern Greek army has been
trained by French officers. Turkey is sup-
posed to have had. the assistance of German
experts in bringing its warlike arrangements
up to their present state of imperfection. It
looks as if the two great national systems
were thus going to receive something in the
nature of a test, and even if the Greek
doesn’t make good in the end, his early suc-
cesses go to show that his form has vastly
improved since his last scrap in 1897. Once
the Turk worked mostly by British method,
and some of the things he did in 1877-78
were a credit to Bull. Some of the other
things he did would have been a disgrace to
the Devil.
THE JARGON OF THE STABLE.
TIPSTER: “Be sure to back Knucklebone to win
yer a pot of money , and then EMPTY IT ALL
OUT on Rice Custard ƒor the larst race.
See!”
One John Olderson is asking the law to
give a definition of the word “useful,” as
applied to domestic toilers. John took a job
as boots-and-useful in a Colac (Vic.) hotel,
and was presently requested by the licensee’s
wife to wash the dishes. He consented, but
emphasised the fact that it was merely “to
oblige the lady.” The- next day, on John
reiterating this chivalrous gag, he was briskly
sacked. He thereupon sued for wages, and
a week’s pay in lieu of notice. The honor-
ariums refused the week’s-notice claim, but
made an order for the wages due. John has
now appealed against the decision, and a
Supreme Court Judge will presently chew
the problem. The ex-useful claims that his
duties were merely to clean the boots and act
as porter, whereas the licensee avers that
a “useful” can legally be ordered to do any-
thing under the sun. It is an interesting
problem. This writer has known “usefuls”
who, without demur, polished the knives,
chopped wood, dug the garden, cleaned the
canary’s cage, and performed other light
exercises. He knew one member of the craft
who was even called upon habitually to
“do up” his mistress’s blouse, but, as he
afterwards rose from “boots” to co-re., his
case can hardly be taken as a precedent.
“The Pariah”: The mournful entry of a
Queensland Governor into Dryandusty (B.
24/10/T2) reminds me that Brassey, Vic-
toria’s falling-off Excellency, had a warm re-
ception at Colac. He was passing through
to stay with one of the district squattocrats,
and the town spread itself in a display of
“loyalty.” The most successful effort was
that of the State school kiddies. Major
Robin drilled the nippers in their part. As
three flash four-in-hands sped past the
school, three flasher cheers were given, and
“Gorsave” was set going. As the last notes
pursued the vice-regal party up Cameron s
Hill, a cyclist pedalled laboriously from the
opposite direction of Murray-street. He was
a globular and perspiring cyclist, and his
appearance excited such ribald mirth that
the school pursued the puggareed rider for
half a mile, mostly with rude remarks.
When they came back the horrible discovery
was made that the cyclist was Brassey.
Nature takes grim revenges sometimes;
and bushman Daveney, riding across a
lonely part of Terrick Station, in the Long-
reach district (Q.),was the victim of one of
them a couple of weeks ago. When he did not
return, a search party went out, and found
his dead body, with one arm amputated at
the elbow. Then Sherlock Holmes got to
work, and gradually unfolded a hideous tale.
There were young galahs in a nest in a hol-
low limb, and in the opening to that nest
was found the rest of Daveney’s arm. On
the trunk of the tree were an infinity of
scoi'ings and scratchings, evidently made by
a man’s spurs. The height of the nest
showed that to insert his arm in the opening
Daveney must have stood '.on his saddle.
Then, evidently, his
horse had moved from
under him ; the sudden
wrench had caused the
hand to twist and hold
fast, and the man was
left dangling in the
air too far from the
trunk of the tree to
grip it with his legs
so as to ease the strain
and allow him to re-
lease himself. Grown
d e s pe ra t e, he had
drawn his knife, and,
probably by the use of
his teeth, had opened
it, and tried to hack
through his arm at the-
shoulder. Failing there,,,
he had got to work
on the elbow, and ac-
tually succeeded in
cutting through suffi-
ciently to cause, his
weight to break what
was left. After ..fall-
ing to the ground his
worst troubles com-
menced. Iron resolu-
tion and indomitable
pluck had enabled him
to cut through has own
arm ; but it was!value-
less to stop the bleed-
ing, and he had, of
course, cut an artery.
That was fatal. After
staggering a few
paces, he collapsed and died from loss of
blood, and all his splendid fortitude was
wasted.

“Gr.V.W.” : A thermometer under a black
cloth will register a higher temperature than
one under a white cloth. Maoriland vine
growers put black paint on the walls on
which the vines are trained, in order that the
fruit may get the last ounce of heat out of
the sunshine. People wear white clothing
in hot countries to keep them cool. Yet the
Tory person asserts that the Almighty made
men hlack to enable them to endure tropical
heat! It’s a dreadful reflection on either
the Almighty’s intelligence or the Tory per-
son’s veracity; and of the two I’m dis-
tinctly inclined to suspect the Tory person’s
veracity.

“Kodak”: Some playwright or novel-
ist should store up the printed re-
ports of the recent accident whereby
a , Sydney railway guard, who was
holding on to a carriage stanchion, was
thrown 15 yards into the arms of two ticket
collectors. The lightning stroke which did
it was too hurried to reflect that the ticket
collectors had no immediate need of even' a
well-liked workmate. A more discriminating
force would have aimed a nice girl, or two
nice girls, into the collectors’ arms, _or
thrown an escaping criminal to the pursuing
detective, or hurled a murderous, escaped
baboon off the carriage in which the heroine
sat alone and unprotected. A mere hap-
hazard heaving-round of characters is just
annoying. The sensational novelist would
save up and use his thunderbolt in this
fashion :—•
“Good-bye!” she cried. She wept and clung to the
stanchion, while the monster she had married at the
bidding of her avaricious father tried to drag her
away. There was a peal of thunder and a blinding
flash. When she came to she was lying, a lovely,
rich young widow, in her lover’s arms. Nay, more!
The same stroke that .had thrown her 15 yards, and
ended her brief married life by hurling her husband
under the wheels of the passing train, had heaved
her angry father into the deep engine tank, dashed
the insulting guard into a truck of sand, hoisted
the inquisitive station-master on to the spikes of the
roof, lifted the junior porter behind the coal-stack,
where he could see nothing, and given her golden hair
the wave she had always longed for.
The lightning stroke (supported by news-
paper cuttings to salve a dubious publisher’s
conscience) is fully equal to that other grard
stand-by, the long arm of coincidence. It
is almost impossible to overwork either of
them.
“Kodak”: The Jab-
bers sacked their im-
migrant maid after
one of the recent hot
wind days. Coming
home parched and
shrivelled after a
wearisome day at the
races, they beheld
smoke pouring from
three of the house
chimneys. They
charged in to inquire
the cause, and almost
fainted when the hot
front door was opened
by a dishevelled and
lobster - red female.
The house breathed
smoke at them like a
hungry dragon as
they perspired tremu-
lously on the mat.
“What the devil have
you being doing?”
screamed Jabbers. "Is
the place on fire, or
what?” Miss Brit-
annia took a deep
breath and nervously
wiped her perspiring
face with the corner
of her apron. “No,
sir, there isn’t no-
thing on fire. But I
thought the house
should have fires in
it, sir, it bein’ win-
ter.” Jabbers blew
out his cheeks at her.
“Winter be d d!”
he yelled. “Winter’s
just over, you fool!”
Britannia tottered. “I
alwuz thought Octo-
ber wuz winter, sir!”
It was Jabbers’ turn
to totter, and he did
so; also he cried on
account of the smoke.
“This isn’t England!” he stormed. “Don’t
you know that the seasons are reversed at the
Antipodes—where you are now?” Britan-
nia looked dazed for a minute, then burst
into scalding tears. “I—l didn’t know I
wuz at- Tipodes! I thought this wuz Aus-
tralyer!” Then Jabbers, quite insane,
rushed for the water-jug to extinguish Bri-
tannia’s October beacons, and soon the
sizzling house was full of steam and pro-
fanity and a discharged maid’s snivelling.

Turkey now knows how it feels when
anybody has got the Bulge on him.
* * * *
The news that the Bulgarians have been
storming fortresses with hand-grenades, and
taking entrenchments at the point of the
bayonet, should be of considerable interest
to Australian military chiefs. It proves
that half-trained men who cannot shoot
straight don’t count for much when up
against smaller forces of well-trained sol-
diers. Had the Turks been able to hit the
mark, it is safe to say that no Bulgarian
would have ever got close enough to use
either hand-grenade or bayonet. And if
Australia were attacked to-morrow, its chief
trouble would be that it mostly wouldn’t be
able to find the enemy with a bullet. There
is only one way to remedy that —to do as
England did in the days of the long bow,
and make regular practice of markmanship
compulsory.
THE PROPOSAL.
THE DAMSELS OF THE DAMNED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is only last Sunday that I saw three girls
leave the church I attended dressed —or undressed —
in such a way that, upon close observation, I could
trace practically the whole of the details of their
forms. —Heroic Sydney Sun correspondent.
I watched the Damsels Three
Go past half-nakedlee.
I did not turn my outraged eyes away;
But gallantly I nerved
Myself, and so observed
The “details” of the Damsels of To-day.
I looked, and looked, and looked;
Saw where the bodice hooked;
Saw where the gown to bust and bicep clung ;
Saw each round, heaving breast.
And my consumptive chest —
Ah, curses that / am no longer young!
I weaken at the knees
To know that Methodies
Have figures—flesh and blood, like other folk.
Religion’s dead and gone
Since sausage-skins are on
The forms I can’t reform, nor yet revoke!
N.S.W.

wrong. It was a kind of moral anaemia, one of
the symptoms of which was that it was some-

increasing number of young people seemed to
be anxious to have a post to lean against.

“Yarrum”: Last week I noticed that an
authority on Australian birds, who should be im-
prisoned or shut up somewhere, wrote in the Aus-
tralasian Catholic School Paper (for senior
classes) that the curlew “builds a nest on the ground
the same as fowls.” The curlew does nothing of the
kind. The bird simply drops its eggs on a bare rock,
or among some loose stones, and the sun does the rest.
I’d put all the nest into my left eye, and it wouldn’t
make me blink worth talking about. And, only
yesterday, I came upon a couple of boy readers, who
might have been doing mathematics or helping their
mothers with the housework, hunting for curlews’
nests. That’s why I say that the writer should be
dealt with. I’ve risen rapidly to contradict the silly
statement, lest other boys may be led into wasting
valuable time.

About marram-grass and other related n *
Marram-grass was, in th ® fir f, t special pur-
Australia by Baron von Mueller for the speci 1

yet contest of this or any, of the
many services rendered to his adopted couty
lovable old German scientist. Any We
Warrnambool or Port Fairy newspapers, an} so' ™
of the first potato-growing settlers, or any botanical
authority, will confirm my statement.
At Armstrong’s Bay, behind which lie some oi the
famous potato lands, "there is a
of the encroachments made by the sands m the ie
intervening years between the first settlement and the
adoption of von Mueller’s method of prevention The
original road between Warrnambool and Port Tairy
ran close to the coast, but clear of the hummocks as
they were then. Fiftv years ago the overflowing sand
compelled its abandonment. Now-, m “
covered bv sand-hummocks 40 feet high, in others
it rises like a causeway above the sand, which, after
having covered it, has moved further inland.
Amongst old residents there is a conflict of opinion
as to the Sea movement within the period of local
inhabitation. Some of them can point to good evi-
dence of its having receded m places; others claim
that its general movement is to follow the sand in-
land. The old stone wall at “The Basin contradicts
both’ theories so far as its immediate locality is con-
cerned There is room, therefore, for difference of
opinion as to the site of the old Spanish ship once
lying on the beach here, high and dry at low water,
and about which an ever-recurring discussion is car-
ried on ; but, in my opinion, the probability is that
it is now a good distance below low water., Forty
years ago I spent many Saturday afternoons search-
ing, with other youngsters, for the buried treasure ot
the old ship. (“Them Spaniards were dead sure to
have buried this gold in one of the big hummocks
before the blaekfellers killed them.”) But we knew
better than to bother looking for the wreck itself ;
for from one of a gang of beach-rovers of an earlier
decade than ours we had acquired the secret of its
fate. They were a tough-conscienced lot, those earlier
buccaneers, to stand by and watch successive histo-
rical-research parties, even to the present .day, waste
time and money and sweat and language prodding
the sand in a futile search —for the old wreck had
disappeared in a bon fire years before. But it wasnt
our secret; so we kept it too. These marauders who
preceded our gang worked profitably, for a time, an
old alleged aboriginal burying-ground in the hum-
mocks, the skulls from which sold for a pound each •
most of them going to London curio-collectors and
for scientific purposes. We inherited the unsaleable
rib and shank bones, etc., but nary a skull. Here s
a clue for investigation; for some of these skulls
should still be easilv traced. The odds are against
it; but there’s just a chance that they were really
from the skeletons of the shipwrecked Spaniards.

“Yarrum” : A prohibition order is a tough
problem. A short time ago a stranger from Maitland
way settled down in a
South Coast (N.S.W.)
town. On the first Sat-
urday night, with his pay
in his pocket, he mean-
dered into the principal
street. Up against a
groggery he got into con-
versation with a tall, dig-
nified gentleman, whom he
asked to come and re-
fresh —the Maitland man
was not one to mop liquor
with the flies. They en-
tered the pub and sat
down to it. The first
drink had hardly settled
when the T.D. person
asked his newly-made
friend if he were going
to do it again. “And
why?” asked the friend.
“You’ll see,” said the dig-
nified fellow, “if I go out
and get the sergeant in.
Don’t you know that there
is a prohibition order out
against me?” It was a
new girl, who didn’t know
the prohibited one, that
served the drinks. But
just here the landlady en-
tered and spotted the
offender. Then she or-
dered him off the pre-
mises, and asked the man
from Maitland what sort
of a low-down animal he
took himself to be, and
called him everything she
could imagine for “trying
to get a poor widow
I woman into the clutches
of the law.” The stranger
did a bolt, and hasn’t been into the place since.

“Tiaki”: The remarks of “Brass Button”
(B. 19/9/’l2) re Castlepoint (North Island of M.L.)
revive weird memories. The “rock” pushes its head
high into the atmosphere, and, at the water level, is
pierced through by a cavern where, even in the hot-
test and stillest weather the air is colder than a
Wowser’s charity. When the sou’-west wind romps
in, a mighty shaft of white spray roars through the
rocky tunnel and spews itself over the adjacent land-
scape. Nothing can stand against that zephyr. It
would blow the horns off the Devil. The lower part,
called the “reef,” is a high, smooth bank of rock,
something like a giant bed bolster in shape, and
about a quarter of a mile long. There have been, I
reckon, more lives lost at the “Castle” than at any
other headland in Australasia. The Maoris call the cav-
ern “Te-Rua-o-te-Wheke-o-Rangiwhakoama.” Te-Rua-
o-te-Wheke means “the Cave of the Octopus,” and, ac-
cording to ancient Henares, it was here that the
mighty explorer of the Pacific, Kupe, had an argu-
ment with a giant octopus, which he pursued and
afterwards slew at Picton, a beauty spot on the
brow of the South Island.

“Nunawading” : A few things about yore-
time shearers, gleaned from Spence, of the National
Parliament. A squatter between the Murrumbidgee
and Darling used to keep a bit of raddle under a
glass case on his mantelpiece. The manager said it
built the woolshed. Raddle was the stuff used to
mark sheep not properly shorn, and for which the
owner refused to pay. The manager boasted he had rad-
dled 40,000. Spence has a copy of an agreement under
which the men got no payment until they had gone
750 miles for it to Adelaide. Shearers had to pay
exorbitant fees at Adelaide registry offices. From
£1 to £5 would be given to make sure of certain
sheds. Shearers were usually forced to buy their
supplies on the stations. Here are prices charged
30 years ago at Moulamein station, Riverina. The
town was less than a mile away, and things were
rather dear there. Flour in town £1 7s. 6d. per
bag; on station £3 2s. 6d. Tobacco in town 65.,
and on station 12s. 6d. All other requirements pro-
portionate. Yet squattters bought their supplies
wholesale in big cities as cheap as storekeepers, or
cheaper if the storekeeper happened to be under the
thumb of a city capitalist. On the Murrumbidgee,
60s. per bag of flour was charged at a station on one
side of the river, while at the town just across the
stream it was £l.
“E.O’F.”: One hot Sunday morning, three
months ago, I saw Pudgely sprawling on the veran-
dah of his villa. The lawn-mower stood deserted in
the sun, and some heaps of warm, freshly-cut grass
gave off a delicious fragrance. I leant on the fence
and remarked “Good morning. Looks rather like
rain.” Pudgely’s frightfully limp form stirred, and
he raised his head and stared at me wildly. “Don’t
tell me that,” he cried. “You don’t really think it’s
going to rain, da you ?” I looked at the sky again.
“Looks very like it, old man.” He groaned and fell
back on his elbow. “I suppose it will! And I was
just getting this place trim, too! Never you take
a place that has as much grass as a Queensland graz-
ing lease. I’m always going here! By the time I’m
half finished round the front, the d —d stuff is knee-
high at the back, and the cat is able to lose itself
on either side of the house. Then I have to rattle
round the back before it’s too late; and all the time
I’m toiling and sweating there, I can hear the muck
shooting up in front. Never tried to push a heavy
mower through thick grass, did you? Well, I do it
every Sunday—and I’m never in what you might call
decent physical training. How do Idoit ? I dunno !
Perhaps it’s will power—all I know is that it isn’t
strength. If I allowed myself to give in What’s
that?" He rose up stiffly, like Lazarus from the
dead, as the first of the rain drops pattered on the
roof, groaned terribly, and, without a word of fare-
well, staggered inside. Last week his heart gave out
as he was cutting the backyard harvest, and the
undertaker had to be ’phoned for. Being an old
neighbor, I attended the funeral. When I arrived
at the house the new curate was endeavoring to con-
sole the widow. I didn’t catch the first part of his
speech; but I distinctly heard him wind up with,
“And you know, dear Mrs. Pudgely, ‘All flesh is
grass.’ ”

THE MERINO COW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. J. E. Lord, an English analyst, having con-
ducted a series of elaborate tests, is satisfied that
the hair of a cow is really wool. —Newspaper item.
So, Posie, you are better than I thought,
Aye, better far it seems;
You’re even greater than the cow I wrought
At midnight in my dreams.
For, after all, at best that dreamed-of cow
Would give a bucketful
Of milk, but you, the wise men tell us now,
Will fill a bale with wool!
And yet it’s very sad to think that through
The toil-scarred long ago
A bale of wool was growing rank on you,
And I —l did not know.
Why was this" secret kept so long from me?
Where was this scientist
Through all the years to keep this £ s. d.
From falling in my fist?
To think each morning when I milked you, Pose,
And flies and beetles drowned,
You were potential with new-fangled clo’s
Worth fifteen pence a pound.
To think that when the willy-wagtails plucked
Their nests from off your back,
It was the very stuff I might have trucked
For many quids a sack.
And when my milkmen were not milking, they
Instead of resting at
The work of grubbing stumps, or making hay,
Might well have scissored that
Extensive wealth from your prolific hide
And pressed it into bales
For shipment to the marts at Christmas-tide,
To catch the season’s sales.
But better late than never, Lord declares:
Although it’s mighty hard
To entertain an angel unawares
In my own milking-yard.
And I have lost how many golden sov-
Ereigns through being blind,
When you, good Posie, were a jumbuck of
The pure merino kind.
N.S.W.
R. J. CASSIDY.

“Eddyson”: Brother Veal came to be cele-
brated as the Prophet of Waddy, and was the most
successful performer I have ever known. In fact,
“Willyum” Yeal was the only prophet I have seen
in active operation, and the only one I have record
of who had sufficient faith in his ability to give an
early time and an adjacent place in throwing off a
prediction, so that his contemporaries could put
the chronometer on him and time him to the tick.
Other prophets required illimitable space to turn in,
and dated their prophecies away beyond all reason,
so that they could only be proved liars when they
were dead, and it didn’t matter. Brother “Will-
yum” Veal was not a prophet in what you would
call a large way, but for a couple of years he had
Waddy licked, and was an object of awe when he
stirred abroad. It was not a healthy thing to
awaken the righteous resentment of “Willyum.” When
the prophet disagreed with a man or a family he
always worked himself into a state of frenzy in the
little hump-backed Wesleyan Bethel on the hill on
the following Sunday, and discovered trouble for the
objects of his resentment, and the trouble was never
overdue. Brother Veal might not mention names
when in the course of a frothy and intemperate
prayer ; coming events were expounded, but he indi-
cated the party due for affliction so clearly that no
doubts remained. Just’s red cow was killed follow-
ing a specific prophecy by Brother Veal. Brother
Veal indicated that for Johnson’s contumacy his
stable would be burnt, and Johnson’s stable was
burnt. “Willyum” discovered that Crowther was
proud and perverse, that his charges for vegetables
during a local famine were exorbitant and ungodly,
and promised a visitation to Crowther’s crop on or
before the 18th. On the night of the 17th half the
goats of Waddy got through miraculous breakages in
Crowther’s fence, and laid his cabbages waste. When
Veal made a most circumstantial prophecy respect-
ing terrible danger by water to Dan Anthony, who
was never known to come into contact with water
by accident or design, there was some little derision
in Waddy ; but on the following night, Dan, in a very
drunken state, was fished from the Native Youth
dam; and couldn’t remember how he came to be
there, though he imagined that he was shoved. Veal’s
stock went up sky-high. Probably a score of pro-
phecies came true before “Willyum,” roused to
righteous fury by the action of Scammel in under-
bidding him for a carting contract, predicted, with
much holy fervor, disasters to “They horses of they
wicked and ungodly sinner that taketh away they
living of they righteous!” Then Scammel sat up
and watched his paddock, and caught Brother “Will-
yum” Veal with a sickle, trying to hock his grey
mare. This aroused suspicion, but for lack of
witnesses nothing was done. Later, “Willyum,”
having predicted a loss of corn to fall upon Hodges
for his contempt of the Sabbath, was taken red-
handed at night with his dray loaded with five bags
of oats from Hodges’ barn, and the career of the
most accurate prophet of the century ended in
goal.” “Never prophecy unless you know” should
be supplemented with “Never prophecy unless you
can do it.”
DRIVEN TO DRINK.
THE SOBER ONE (shocked): “Good gracious,
what's wrong with you?”
THE UNSORER ONE (almost tearfully): “Been
drove to it by fam'ly calamity. Got tele-

“Mr. Ocky” : When a dog lies down to go
to sleep, does he doze off straight away? If somebody
could give me a definite answer the world would have
a brighter aspect for me. At school, years ago, J
put the matter to a teacher, and was threatened with
■expulsion, simply because he thought I was asking
a variation of the “Why does a piece of string?’’ or
“When does a mouse if it climbs?” enigmas. I
cannot stand it much longer. Take any given dog
which wants to have a nap. It coils up, closes its
optics, and is asleep—apparently. I want to know
if it is asleep right off the mark, or does it count
imaginary sheep jumping over a stile? Do dogs
suffer from insomnia? The question is not beyond
some of your writers, so in the name of Heaven
please print this par, and let somebody decide the
point. Nobody here seems to know, and I must find
out.

“Tiaki”; Re “Te Ruruku’s” par on to-
hungas (B. 26/9/’l2). There are many and various
methods by which the medicine
man or woman extorts his or her
fees from a superstitious and be-
lieving clientele. The old method
was to pluck a tassel from the
victim’s cloak, or (better still)
obtain a piece of discarded
fowl, of (best of all) sneak
a lock of his hair. The goods ob-
tained were buried to the accom-
paniment of ghostly incantations,
and, as they decayed, the wasting
away of Henare proceeded at
overtime rates. Now, however,
civilisation has made its de-
mands, and, as Shakspeare has it,
-'wizards know their times.” The
modern medicine man is discard-
ing the obsolete hair-burying or
-raising methods. He intimates
casually that he is a “pakeha
doctor” not yet in possession of
his diploma. He writes out
simple prescriptions, which are
made up by the local chemist as
he thinks fit. Sometimes Meri
has to be hurried off to: the local
hospital or cemetery as a result
of the dusky doctor’s diagnosis.
It is always a difficult thing to
get a conviction against a to-
hunga, as even the nearest re-
lations of a defunct Henare re-
fuse to “shelf” the medico.
Whisky, used in small doses ex-
ternally and in large (very large)
doses internally, is the favorite
lotion. Taking it by and large,
Tamata Tohunga has discarded
the curse for the cure business, the latter being much
more profitable and in greater demand. Unfortu-
nately, cheap female labor has been playing havoc
with the tohunga profession of late, and Henare will
need a scale of fees or working award or union or
something to regulate things shortly if the scab ele-
ment is not eliminated.
“Yarrum” : Have you witnessed the wood
duck taking its children for a swim? These birds
build their nests in high trees, always close to water,
and, before the young ones are able to fly, they are
carried down, one at a time, from the nest in the
bill of the mother, and toddle away for a dip, which
they relish immensely. On the return journey to
the nest, dad, who cleans out the humpy and tidies
ft up a bit while mother and the kids are away,
always lends a helping bill. The two parents take
hold, one at each side, of the tips of a duckling’s
wings, and flutter up into the tree with it, At a dam
t’other side of Narrandera (N.S.W.), I used to lie in
a quiet spot and watch the birds by the hour. With
my gun, that was with me every time, I might easily
have murdered whole families of them ; but my con-
science wouldn’t hear of it. Besides, there was
always mutton, and plenty of good, fat beef at the
homestead.
“Chloe”: The barking spider, mentioned
on the “Ked Page” (B. 29/8/’l2), is fairly common
on the central gold fields, and throughout the tropical
portions of West Australia. This contradictory in-
sect has some curious habits. The male of the spe-
cies seems to live on the ground, while the female
spins a very large and intricate web, which she sus-
pends from bough to bough of a tree. When she
desires a mate, she gives an acrobatic performance
on the silk ropes by which her home is suspended,
accompanying her gymnastics by the grating bark,
noticed by Professor Baldwin Spencer. Presumably
the lady spider is an emotional insect, for she is
quite devoid of modesty when she is courting. She
pirouettes, somersaults, spins rapidly on her head, and
then on each leg in turn. She can also keep her
understandings whirling like Catherine wheels, then
leap off her nose and land lightly on her ears. All
this is done to attract the notice of the eligible
male, and you bet it does! After one or more such
displays, the desired, mate appears on the scene. But
he is not precipitate. He adopts a critical attitude
and inspects the dazzling dancer through opera-
glasses, with his crush hat pushed far back on his
head. Of course, he eventually falls a victim to love,
or curiosity, or both, and ascends the tight rope.
His subsequent history is veiled in mystery, for, in
the space of half an hour he has vanished utterly, and
the police are completely in the dark. Clearly he
cannot descend, for his spouse cuts away the
rope as soon as he reaches her. She folds him in her
many legs, and that is the last you see of him. In-
deed, the onlooker might convince himself that the
adventurous lover was a myth, a creature born of
liver and defective eyesight, if it were not that a
young family arrives very soon on the fatherless
scene. A scientist, not more untruthful than others
of his calling, told me that the lady spider being
a capricious creature, has no more use for a hus-
band after the first few moments; and having eco-
nomic principles, she promptly makes a meal of him,
to save post-mortems, and funeral expenses. This
might not be correct. All I know is that he disap-
pears, and the lady of the web house wears a gorged
look, and does no more acrobatic feats until she de-
cides to marry again. The scientist mentioned also
that Nature had been especially kind to female
spiders, in that she gave each an opportunity of ac-
quiring four husbands, young spiders arriving in
those proportions of the sexes. Probably through
untold ages, female «spiders have entirely devoted
themselves to the studies of eugenics and gymnastics.

. “Yairs,_ sir ; rumatiz v’ry bad—hawful pain.. Never

o th world. But th’ rousies are me greatest trial;

“Pick” : A momentous question that might
engage the whole range of theological expert evi-
dence for elucidation has cropped up at Gundaroo,
N.S.W. There an unfortunate, full up of life, took
matters into his own hands and passed himself out.
He was duly claimed by his own sect, but, being a
suicide, it was deemed indecent to plant the remains
where sacred worms could feast upon them, and a
grave was therefore dug in any old place outside
the pale of dedicated earth. Later, however, when
a new boundary fence for the bone-yard was being
erected, all interested were horrified to find that the
line separating the consecrated from the unblessed
area passed fairly across the mound under which the
departed nark lay, and that his head and shoulders
were trespassing, so to speak, on Bishop-blessed soil,
while his lower limbs only occupied their rightful
place in unhallowed clay. It is here that the theo-
logical question rises or writhes or contorts or
something. What is going to happen when the
archangel blows his motor-horn to announce that the
resurrection has arrived at Gundaroo? Will a leg-
less candidate for entry at the Pearly Gates soar up
through the clouds of judgment, or will those un-
consecrated legs be powerful enough to carry the
sanctified parts down to perdition? It is a nice
point, and the whole thing seems to turn on the
actual tug-of-war capabilities of a Bishop’s blessing
and the natural drawing force of Hades.

“Crookspinf : It happened at a sunny
Victorian fishing- town. Two fellows, of the line and
bait profession, one an Irishman and the other a
Chow, were having a game of cards in the shelter-
shed at the end of the pier. It was night, and they
were gravely passing and trumping by the light of
a dimly-burning lantern. One of the lads padded
softly along from his boat with a large and flabby
octopus he had just taken from the nets. Being as
curious as most people are in a slow town, he peeped
in to see what was the cause of the devotional mut-
tering in the shed, and discovered the solemn gam-
blers. They were fathoms deep in play when a mass
of frozen jelly spreadeagled through the air, and,
making a cannon off the yellow, landed with a sicken-
ing squelch on the cards. As the staggered Hibernian
rescued the cards and the startled Chow wiped from
his brow the flakes of cold sea spume, a malicious
snigger of delight was heard, and, the pair looking
up, observed the best fighter on the beach grinning
at them. It was no good offering battle, and they
knew it. But the Chow remarked in a low wail,
“And he calls himself a Clistian!” Patrick, for his
part, swallowed his mouthful of deadly insults, and
moaned in a terribly earnest way, “Be Gud, if it
had happened in Port Milbourne the row would have
lashted a fortnight!”
