The temptation of the Hat feminine has never been
more sumptuously shrined than in Farmer’s (Pitt
Street, Sydney) new show rooms. A carpet, soft as
velvet, leads up to the besetting weakness of every
self-respecting woman—the New Hat. A discreet
bow-window-seat is reserved for the attendant swain
—husband, brother, father or fiance.. Huge glass
cases (dust proof) contain the adorable headgear
which Every woman wants to complete her happi-
ness. _ Off the palatial hattery are the new hair-
dressing rooms, presided over by a smiling goddess,
gowned in silky black draperies. At her command
assisting nymphs, expert in the art of face massage,
marcel waving, shampooing and other beauty-making
devices, come forth from tiny toilet rooms, and turn
mere hair into a crowning glory. When the triumph-
ant coiffure, soft and silky, is achieved, its owner
glows with that sense of perfect peace which only
grows beneath a well-groomed head. She puts on
her hat —the event of the adjoining rooms—ob-
serves the soft texture of her massaged complexion,
and retires to digest her content and morning tea
(11 a.m. edition do luxe) in Farmer’s roof-garden.*

SOUTH AUS. TRAVELLER’S OPINION.
Change of water and food often cause colic and
diarrhoea, and when Mr. F. Bannister, traveller for
Menz and Co., Adelaide (S.A.), is attacked with
either of these complaints he takes Chamberlain’s
Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy. In a recent letter he
states: “Having suffered severely from colic, I have
much pleasure in informing you that. Chamberlain’s
Colic and Diarrhoea Remedy has on several occasions
given me instant relief, and I always carry it with
me.”*
Oscar Wilde received respectful mention
in a Bishop’s sermon the other Sunday. The
preacher was Dr. Mercer, of Hobart, who
recalled that Wilde once said: “A man has
to be a long time dead before he is con-
sidered a saint. I am afraid I shall never
be sufficiently dead.”

Prom the Oldest Inhabitant:—
An application to bring under the R.P. Act a big
area in the Windsor district, some of it granted
originally to James Larra, calls to mind one or two
ancient families. The progenitors of the Bowmans,
who are the applicants, came out in 1798 with the
first free settlers. They took up ground first at
Liberty Plains (Rookwood now), but soon after left
for the Hawkesbury district. Larra was a very early
comer, a Jew, who kept an hotel at Parramatta.
He was the first auctioneer , there, and was one of
the liquidators of the estate of Lieut. William Cox,
of the 102nd Regiment, who, over-speculating, had
to make an assignment which was a nine days won-
der. Simeon Lord was the liquidator at the Sydney
end. Larra was the auctioneer of the farms, the sale
being held at Parramatta. Fifteen farms were sold,
including the one originally owned by George Bar-
rington, the literary pickpocket. That farm, by the
way, was purchased by Parson Marsden. After that
Cox became a Government contractor; the road
across the Blue Mountains, from Emu Ford to the
Macquarie, was one of his jobs.
Dean O’Hea, so often mentioned of late in
connection with Coburg ( Vic.), made his pile
mostly by land specs. When the diggings
“broke out,” every little cocky who had
enough land to grow “a few spuds” sold to
the Dean, who really did not want the soil,
but could not refuse his neighbors, who were
hot on getting off to “make a pot.” The
Dean . agitated for a change of name from
Pentridge to Coburg, because the would-be
humorists asserted that the majority of his
riverince’s parishioners were in Pentridge.
An opportunity offered for change when the
Duke of Edinburgh visited Australia in 1868.
Brunswick, adjoining Pentridge. is mostly
supposed to be named after the Duke killed
at Waterloo. This is, however, probably an
error. In Melbourne in the late ’3o’s and
early ’4o’'s, there was a certain Captain
George Brunswick Smyth, after whom the
main street in Fitzroy was named, and after
whom the suburb also was probably named.
In any case, the Duke of Edinburgh was heir
to the Dukedom of Coburg, and the suggested
coupling of Brunswick and Coburg was, no
doubt, the more readily agreed to on that
account.
Kiamil Pasha, the “grand old” Turk, who
is trying to gather up the tattered remains
of what was once the Sublime Porte, is
nearly 90. The “Arabian Nights” is no-
thing to bis career. He has held high office,
and has been a fugitive—in a palace one
day and driving a cab for a living the’
next. When Abd til - the-Not-T et-Damn ed was
booted off the throne by the more or less Young
(and Foolish) Turk party, old Kiamil was
dug up from his damp hiding-place, and
made vizier. Like a number of Levantines,
he has Jewish blood in his Turkish veins,
and is a linguist, speaking Arabic, Persian,
Hindostanee, Greek. English and French.
He is likewise a famous pistol shot, a
freethinker and an incorrigible. Liberal. Old
Abdul Hamid used, when Kiamil was his
vizier, to signify his royal displeasure with
the vizier by tugging him by the beard. This
the vizier frustrated by shaving cffian—-a
simple device which even Sherlock Holmes
might not have been ashamed to father.
More old land-grabbers and pubs:—
Many years ago one Captain Piper was connected
with the official life of N. S. Wales. Point Piper
was named after him, and there was a pub in Lower
George-street which hung out a shingle in his honor.
A daughter of his married a lieutenant in the navy
named Alfred Thrupp, Who—through Piper’s in-
fluence, no doubt—obtained a grant of-700 acres ,on
the north side, fof .Sydney harbor. Captain Piper’s
affairs became involved open house, horse racing,
etc. —and a surrender , of his estate became necessary.
The original Daniel Cooper, the founder' of the for-
tunes .of . The family, , acouired Point Piper, while
W. C. Wentworth got hold of Vaucluse, and Captain
Piper went over the hills to Bathurst, and became a
dairy farmer. Thrupp’s 700 acres also presently fell
into the hands of Dan Coouer, who, like Sam Terry
and Simeon Lord, was a great land-grabber. In 1853
this Dan Cooper died in London, and left the 700-
acre grant to John Cooper, a nephew, who is still
living. Until the ’7o’s, the estate (which is bounded
on the south by Neutral Bay, Hungry Bav and ad-
jacent waters, and on the north by Middle Harbor
generally) was cut up into huge blocks and let on
lease at low rentals to Louis Bevdon, W. H. Paling.
John Bennett, Henry Parkes (who mortgaged his and
lost it), Captain Barron, Captain Craig, E. O.
Moriarty and others. The land-tax is now giving
him hints to sell his interests, and mostly the lessees
have purchased their holdings. On Saturday last a
part of one of these old leases was brought under the
hammer. E. O. Moriartv, Engineer-in-Chief, used to
hold it, but W. P. Faithful- acquired it about seven
years ago. On Saturday he offered nine lots, of an
average of about 40ft. each, with an approximate
depth of 200 ft. from the harbor’s water edge. Several
of the frontages were sold at £2O per foot. Beauti-
ful unearned increment!
THE RE-APPEARANCE.
THE MAN WHO VOTED FOR McGOWEN:
“’ Struth! I thought that chap was dead!"
Pathetic, though humorous, was Allan
Moore’s comment when the Melbourne Judge
sentenced him to death for missing Geoff
Syme with a Browning. As his Honor
gently fitted on the black cap, Moore, though
he knew well enough what was coming, softly
whistled “Where did you get that hat?”
Died at Mildura, Elliott Crozier, aged 85,
one of the ancients of the Crozier squatting
clan. He owned Cowra station for many
years, and then went in for fruit growing,
lie left 13 children, and great is their mana
in the Lower Darling and Murray district.
Through marriage the clan now includes
Ben Chaffey, of Moorna, a large cannon in
pastoral circles in these days.

A feature of the most sensational Ameri-
can Presidential campaign on record was the
sudden death of J. S. Sherman, Vice-Presi-
dent, in the midst of his hustle for re-elec-
tion on the Republican ticket. The Vice-
President doesn’t cut much ice in Yankee
politics, though the occupant is sometimes
hurled into the Presidency, as Roosevelt was
when McKinley was shot. Sherman is, per-
haps, best known to the outside world as
the author of the Anti-Trust Act. In accord-
ance with American custom, this statute took
the name of its sponsor, and is generally
known as the “Sherman Law.” After view-
ing the results, probably a better name
would be the Sherman Flaw.

“Hullo” : I don’t like to be unkind to an
old friend, but I think that the accession of
Yoshihito to the throne of Japan won’t do
us any good. A German author who knows
Yosh nearly as well as I do states
that he is “the yellow peril incar-
nate.” His policy will be that
Japan must westernise less and
Nipponise more. As Crown Prince
his standpoint was a veiled hos-
tility to things European. As
Mikado his job will be to see that
Japan, having learnt what civili-
sation can teach, will dismiss her
tutors and start out with her
army and navy. The little chap
who now occupies the Japanese
throne is not a son of the late
Mikado by a primary wife; and his
mother enjoys no royal rank
and practically no social position.
They do these things differentlv in
Y osh’s country. For the rest, ho
prays three times a day. and has a
hot. bath before beginning his de-
votions. His wife, whom I have
not the honor of knowing, has “no
arms and no legs”—from a dressmaker’s
point of view. A European lady in the Im-
perial suite has the job of initiating her into
modern dress and the corset; but the result
so far is discouraging.
3 here has been some talk of Archbishop
Carr returning to Galway when his coadju-
tor, Dr. Mannix, arrives, end gets his hand
in. But Dr. Carr says he means to die in
Melbourne; so the talk is stifled. The as-
sistant will be parish priest of North Mel-
bourne (Ilotham) till he “succeeds,” if he
ever does.
Dr. Keenan, a man of much bulk, and
pretty well known as a Masonic chaplain,
died suddenly in Sydney last week. Origin-
ally an R.C. priest, he ’verted to the Church
of England, but confined his attention chiefly
to scholastic work; for years he was head-
master of the Windsor Grammar School. A
couple of years ago'he visited England, and
the struggle to. speak in sufficiently awed
whispers of his interview with the King pro-
bably killed him.
Dropped out of the scuffle of life
last week, at Wellington (M.L.),
VN nliam Jukes _ Steward, politician,
journalist and indifferent writer of
tame odes. He followed the Inky
vvay, as editor or owner of several
M.L. papers, for over 30 years, and
from 1871 till his death he was mostly
'npoliDca l harness. During the year's
1891-93 he was Speaker of the Repre-
sentatives, and until last year was
the “Father of the House.” Then he
dropped out of the Reps., and was
given a corner in the other House.
As a legislator lie meant well, and as
a poet he did likewise ; but his kindly,
gentle spirit balanced the deal. lie
was 71 when Death called round for him.
The new Bishop of Kimberley, Dr. Gore
Browne, is a Maorilander, and has a high
opinion of the country where the principal
unnatural resource is the lou. He is a son
of Col. Thomas Gore Browne, who was
Governor of M.L. from 1855 to 1861.
Talking of fancy names a Bulletin con-
tributor the other week reckoned he had in-
vented one —Shmith. Now H. W. Shmith
writes from Melbourne to say that he is one
of a Victorian grown family of 14 Shmiths.
and has two little Shmiths of his own; and
he can “vouch for the fact that the B. is
read by at least a dozen Shmiths every
week.” By the way, The Bulletin ob-
serves that Mr. Shmith is a hop merchant.
THE IGNORANCE OF COHEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is asserted in the City that the loan has proved
a failure, and that the underwriters have been com-
pelled to take up from 70 to 90 per cent, of the
amount. This disappointing result is attributable to
the general ignorance that prevails with regard to
the resources of N.'S. Wales, arid to the absence of
publicity in connection with the loan. —Cable item.
The lack of knowledge which prevails
In Cohen’s shop beyond the sea
About this State of New South Wales
Shocks and appals and frightens me.
I am amazed they should not know
How crowds in George-street come and go.
I sometimes shudder when I hear
That, in the land where loans should float,
The people living far and near
Know nothing of the Manly boat,
And most in ignorance remain
Of such a suburb as Balmain. -
They never heard of Neutral Bay,
Of Parramatta, Glebe or Kyde,
Nor how the Coogee surf is gay,
Nor how the Lane Cove waters glide;
If these, or some of them, they knew,
They’d rush to cash our I O U.
We have some country at the back —
At least, some people tell us so —
Some pubs along that outer track
Where grain and wool and mutton grow ;
If these old Cohen should despise.
Our men of State might -sympathise.
But here, where boundless wealth is pent,
Our splendid heritage within,
Where bricks produce a crop of rent,
Such lack of knowledge seems like sin—
If Ultimo itself were known,
Cohen would rush us with a loan.
N.S.W.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

As usual lately, £lOOO has been voted to the
Officers’ Provident Fund, and the amount has been
deducted from profits before dropping them on to the
table. The figures, generally speaking, show a dis-
tinct improvement on those of a few years back.
The invariable complaint then was that too great a
proportion of the profits was being cut up among the
shareholders. The lay-by is now very much more
substantial: in the past 12 months it has reached
45 p.c. of the net earnings, and no brick can be
thrown at that. Still, the aggregate reserves are
small. The assets of one sort and another total
£4,856,667, and, as shown above, there is only
£196,267 in the stocking; and it wouldn’t have to
be anything very sensational in slumps to clout more
than £196,267 off the value of nearly £5,000,000
worth of securities. No doubt there may be internal
reserves ; but you can’t take that for granted. If
there are none, then there isn’t very much to protect
the capital in case of trouble. Therefore, in spite of
the recent increase of profits, the management ought
to set its face against the smallest increase in the rate
of dividend. It will be quite time enough for that
when the reserves are twice as strong as they are at
present in proportion to the bank’s risks.
The 12 months’ movements are shown next: —
Sep., ’ll. Sept., T 2.

The position hasn’t changed very much during the
year. The Colonial owes £4,204,745, and has
£1,422,259 in cash and liquid assets; that is
to say, it could pay 34 p.c. of its borrowings on the
nail. The holding is nothing extra—just about what
is usually regarded as safe, with no margin worth
mentioning. Lots of people are asking what the
banks mean by shutting down on new advances so
hard. As a matter of fact, some of them simply
haven’t got the money. A heap of cash certainlv
has come in during the long run of good seasons anil
high prices; but it has gone out very fast too. Look
at this bank’s record for seven years
Proportion of
Advances to
Liabilities. Advances. Liabilities.

In proportion to liabilities—that is to say, to the
public’s money available for lending out, etc. —the
Colonial seven years ago had about 28 p.c. of liquid
assets, which was certainly not enough; it has now
something under 34 p.c., which certainly isn’t more
than enough. If most Australian banks deserve a
kick, it is not for lending too little now, but for
lending so much during the past few years that they
have exhausted their means to lend at present.
The bank’s business appears to be very profitable.
This shows the earnings calculated on capital and
reserves combined:—

Nine p.c. or thereabouts is good; but two things
have to be kept in mind: (1) the bank has nearly
£7 of the public’s money in the business for every
£1 of its own, and that £7 has to be paid back
no matter how bad a knock the securities may get
(that is to say, the securities in which the bank
risks it) ; and (2) the bank has lent out or otherwise
invested all but 34 p.c. of the public’s money, so
that its risks are considerable.

If all the world were wise, and none of it ever lost
its head, there wouldn’t be any need for internal
reserves. If a bank made a heavy loss one half-
year, it would, under those happy conditions, simply
lop the amount off visible reserves previously accumu-
lated. But all the world isn’t wise; hence any such
lopping as that just mentioned would probably
stagger confidence and be very bad for the bank.
There are plenty of people who say that, even under
existing conditions, a bank is not entitled to put
anything up its sleeve; they argue that when it
makes a profit it is its duty to show that profit and
when it makes a loss it should show that loss. That
sounds all right; but unfortunately you can’t tell for
sure when you are making a profit, or, if you are
what it amounts to. A bank may think it is making
a heap of money just now out of its advance busf-
ness, because it has a lot of money out at high rates.
But when it adds the interest on to the principal, it
can’t be sure whether it will be able to collect that
interest, let alone the principal. The profit is there-
fore assumption, and it is a fair thins to put some of
it by to square matters, in case it should not be
possible to collect all the expected interest, or get in
all the lent-out capital.
Supposing years ago the bank lent Bhrown £lO,OOO
on the security of some land at Kensington. Bhrown
didn’t pa}' interest, but let it accumulate, and the
debt grew to £12,000. Then the bank took posses-
sion. Having an idea that the property might not
fetch £12,000, the bank started to drop some of its
earnings (undisclosed) into a secret reserve, upon
which it could draw in case it should presently have
to sell Bhrown’s old property for £9OOO. Obvi-
ously, if it didn’t have that fund, and sold for
£9OOO a property that cost £12,000, it would
have to take £3OOO off the half-year’s profits, though
the loss might not be, and actually would not be,
rightly chargeable against that half-year. That is
the merit of an internal reserve: it saves a half-
year from having to carry the transgressions of pre-
vious terms. But something else may happen. Say
£3OO has been put into the stocking to make good
the expected loss on the sale of that property. The
property is sold and brings in more than £1.2,000;
or, what more often happens, it is revalued at over
£12,000. In that case it will probably be re-
garded as unnecessary to retain the fund any longer,
and it is accordingly dropped in with the profit.
Something of this sort has evidently been taking
place in the National of Australasia, with the result
that in the past 12 months £60,000 has been shaken
out of the internal reserves into the visble funds.
The Bulletin is glad to know that the National’s
partly-new management feels so good about things,
though as there" is a slump in nrospect it is quite
possible that the management will vet be sorry that
it didn’t keep up its sleeve all it had there.

in 12 months:—

That’s better. Even now, though the bank has ap-
parently been calling in money (anyway, its ad-
vances are over quarter of a million less than thev
were 12 months ago), it has only 32 p.c. of the
deposits and other public money represented by
liquid assets. The chief trouble about a bank which
works on such a narrow margin of coin, etc., is that,
especially if things don’t look too bright ahead, the
concern must often turn down good business olfered
by most desirable clients—probably old clients. It
may seem a waste of money to keep 45 p.c. (or
more) of the deposits, etc., in liquid form ; but that
sort of bank is usually getting excellent business
and making lifelong friends while the other fellow
is losing both.
Profits nowadays, calculated on capital and reserves
combined, are rather better than ordinary :

the shareholders’.

But while profits have been growing fast, capital has
been growing too; so though more money has been
coming in, there have been more shareholders to
divide it amongst. The business is a fairly old one;
it was transferred to a limited-liability co. in 1902,
though the public wasn’t invited in till about four
years later. At that time (1906) the capital was
£250,000, half of that very large sum being neces-
sary to finance the time-payment part of the business.
With good times the trade grew, and a little over 12
months ago the capital was raised to £350,000:
£200,000 of it is in ordinary £1 shares, fully paid;
the balance in £lO pref. shares, which stop at 6
p.c., but are cumulative. The business has been ex-
ceedingly profitable, always assuming that stock and
book debts have not been over-valued; and a very
prudent policy has been followed in the cutting-up
of the profits. This shows what has happened in
the past five years:—
Paid away in divs. .. .. ..£107,004
Written off leaseholds, plant and fix-
tures 14,532
Put by, to reserves .. .. .. 84,012
Total profits £205,548
The profits there include the small insurance fund
which was not disclosed till two or three years ago,
and was no doubt mostly built up within the five-
year .period. Only 56 p.c. of the net profits (after
deducting the amount written off) has been divided,
the business being strengthened by the other 44 p.c.
The price paid by the co. for goodwill was
£72,377, and that amount is being formally written
off. In the foregoing table it has not been regarded
as an asset at all, and the reserves have no paper
in them at all, sb far as the eye can see. It is a
remarkably fine record.

There is £150,000 of 6 p.c. cumulative pref. capital,
and that means £9OOO a year. A net profit of
£48,000 thus makes £39,000 available for the
ordinary shareholders = 194 p.c. on their capital. It
is very doubtful indeed whether that sort of thing
can last. There has been a burst of extravagance
lately, the Labor Government in N. S. Wales leading
the way and being mainly responsible for it by its
stupendous borrowing. It is during such periods that
the time-pavment trader flourishes most luxuriantly.
How he will flourish when a slump comes is another
matter. Not only is it certain that he won’t sell as
many goods, but the chances are that he will have
to take back a good deal of what he has sold, or
“hired.” In that respect the dealer in pianos, etc.,
isn’t very much different from the dealer in time-
payment building blocks or residences. This point
must, however, be noted: Marcus Clark and Co. have
a sizeable business, apparently, entirely apart from
the t.p. section.

The capital is now £1,100,000. Two years ago it was
£900,000, but since then two lots of bonus shares
have been issued. Now something else is in the
wind: “Your directors have under consideration a
project of re-valuing the co.’s assets, and adjusting
the capital accordingly without issuing new shares,
which they are satisfied will be to the advantage of
the shareholders.” It is a cryptic remark which
might mean almost anything; but it will probably
be found to mean something solid in the way of a
cash bonus before long—a cash bonus instead of
another share bonus. The position is, to all appear-
ances, extraordinarily strong. Look at how things
have changed in 10 years:—
1902. 1912.
Liabilities.. .. .. £35,656 £43,653
Assets.
Breweries and proper- ,
ties 677,223 856,369
Book debts .. . , 193,550 212,644
Stock 101,184 131,468
Liquid assets . . .. 144,965 383,357
Meantime no new capital has been got in, and lia-
bilities have only moved up to the extent of a trifling
£BOOO. So, apart from that trifle, the business has
provided the regular corpulent dividend, added
£179,146 to the book value of the premises, plant,
etc., and besides other things has put £238,392 on
to the liquid assets. _ Those liquid assets are made up
of deposits and public securities, and the management
probably has an idea that it would be less likely to
cause trouble if the bulk of them were divided
among shareholders; and as the co. certainly isn’t
likely to have much need of these assets itself, this
baper makes a guess that there is a division in the
wind. Probably it will be found, on re-valuation of
assets, that so much writing-up can be done that
the visible reserves needn’t be interfered with. In
other words, perhaps £lOO,OOO or so may be added
on to the book value of the plant, premises, etc ■
so the balance-sheet totals will not be affected if’,
for a start. £lOO.OOO is withdrawn from the liouid
assets for distribution. It is quite possible that The
Bulletin will be told that nothing of this char-
acter has been decided upon. It was told the same
thing three years ago when it announced that a dis-
tribution of bonus shares was impending. On this
occasion it cheerfully admits that it doesn’t know.
It only puts two and two together and tries to make
the answer four.

So the co. has come out of darkness and got back
into light. When, a few years ago, it reached the
end of the mine more quickly than it had ex-
pected, things looked so hopeless that the pref. share-
holders wanted the assets sold and the thing wound
up: they felt pretty sure of getting their trifle of
capital back that way, and were not too sure about
it any other way. However, that plan was de-
feated, and in 1905 a new property was bought at
Cessnock. The co. had very little money or liquid
assets; but it had a fine lot of coal waggons, and
there was a great demand for them at the time ; so
they were hired out, and this helped the co. to get
across the stream. The new property was very
cheaply opened up ; and when production started it
was luckily found that the co. hadn’t lost its old
business connection. Profits consequently came very
soon ; and now, the arrears of cumulative pref. divi-
dend having been paid off, the ordinaries get a bite —
the first since 1901. Just before the Cessnock pro-
perty was bought, the capital stood at £103,939
It stands now at £108,747. Thus, the new mine
has been bought, equipped and opened up at a cost
to the shareholders of less than £5OOO. As at
March, 1904, the book debts and other liquid assets
were £4583; the liabilities were £3162, leaving a
surplus of £1421 of liquid assets. The liquid assets
are now £34,217 and the liabilities £10,719; sur-
plus, £23,498. Thus, besides paying for the mine,
and putting £15,000 into plant, and wiping out all
but 2§ years of the arrears of pref. dividend, opera-
tions to the end of September had added £22,077 to
the liquid surplus; and, as shown above, this is to
be used, or partly used, to wipe out the small arrears
of dividend and pay the ordinaries something No
wonder the shareholders opened their hearts and
voted the chairman 200 guineas. What the
shareholders need to remember now is that a
mine is not immortal, and that you can’t divide all
your revenue over and above expenses without finding
some day that you haven’t got a mine to represent
your capital. In other words, if all profits are
divided, some of that dividend must be regarded as
repayment of principal.

And The Bulletin has an idea that the average
trading concern will presently show that the pendu-
lum has swung still further that way. When trade
eases off, as the result of the enforced stoppage of
wholesale public borrowing, coupled, maybe, with a
poor season, the average trading co.’s expenses won’t
ease off to correspond. Last year or the year before
showed extra-good results, because the trade got a
spurt on ahead of wages, etc. ; but the spurt that
wages, etc., put on will probably outlast the spurt
in trade, and that won’t be good for the look of
profits.
“The co. will proceed to registration and
allotment when 1000 A shares have been applied for.”
This is from the prospectus of the Progressive Stores
of Australasia, Ltd., issped in Sydney the other day.
The promoters yelled on the roof-top of the document
that the capital was £45,000, and they declared that
their object was, “for a commencement,” to “open
with grocery and provision stores in Sydney and
suburbs ; and as the progress of the co. warrants it,
100 branches will be opened in the principal towns
of Australasia.” All of which is going to be done
by a co. that takes to itself the right to retain the
public’s money, even though only 1000 shares Of £1
each are applied for. There have been a good many
cases of this sort in Australia during the past two
or three years. Cos. have been formed with high-
sounding intentions, and with the right to go to allot-
ment on the receipt of applications for a trifling
number of shares—-a number utterly insufficient to
allow the cos. to carry out their alleged intentions!
It ought to be made impossible for a co. to regard
itself as floated unless shares sufficient for the main
purposes of the co. have been applied for ; and regis-
tration in any other circumstances ought to be
refused.
* * *
N.S.W. Government Geologist Pittman
says of the lodide that “the deposit which has been
opened out exceeds in importance any silver-lead
ore-body that has been discovered in N. S. Wales for
some > years”—which sounds fine and large, but
doesn t mean much when you come to remember that
no silver-lead ore-body discovered of late in N S
}' ales has got near dividends. The thing, however"
is certainly promising. Not, of course, that the rich
stuff and the stupendous assays so much talked about
are of any consequence at all. These secondary en-
richments are liable to be serious traps for the un-
wary. There may be a big thing underneath or there
may be a large hole in your pocket. The C.S.A.
i iear L “°” ar > ls a case in point. There was the
leached-out capping, and the enriched ore underneath,
and beneath that, again, as G. H. Blakemore and
other good judges decided, there might be almost
anything As things have turned out, there doesn’t
seem to be much—which was just about what there
was at the somewhat similar Day Dream, the first of
the Barrier shows in which Sydney took an interest
25 years ago. On the other hand, there was the more-
or-less leached outcrop at Broken Hill, then the zone
of enrichment, and, underneath, something great.
Only time can tell which of the two the lodide will
most resemble.

Bleichert and Co., of London, have issued various

consult and advise.*

From June to September the Bacchus Marsh Shire
Council (Vic.) crushed and spread 3000 cub. yds. of
road metal, at an average cost of 2s. 6d. per cub.
yd., and the cost of the spalls per cub. yd. averaged
2s. 4d., making a total cost of 4s. lOd. per cub. yd.,
broken and spread. Their last contract under the
hand-breaking system amounted to 6s. 3d. per cub.
yd., stacked on the roadside, and spreading Is.;
total 7s. 3d., as against 4s. lOd. — a saving of 2s. 5d.
per cub. yd. This result was obtained by using a
Bigelow 16in. x 9in. Patent Stone-crusher, and the
Austral Otis Eng. Co., Ltd., of Melbourne, are the
manufacturers of these breakers.* f3

The localitv is full of iron-cemented breccia (a con-

The Carpathia has a 15ft. hole on it. Most of the
hole is in decomposed granite; the rich outcrop or
cap is but two or three feet thick. There is nothi §f
startling in the hole in the way of tin or lode, the
channel seems to be 3ft. or 4ft. wide, although tl
outcrop is 20ft. wide. The White Crystal is -oft.
deep, in a lode channel 3ft. or 4ft. wide, dipping
west. The same rich cap existed, and was dug- out,
and most of it bagged up without any great ettort.
The material from the bottom of the shaft 1S T'°' ; ln }~
pressive by comparison with the stuff from the out-
crop. The Champion has the same channel as tne
Carpathia, and from appearances the owners nave
successfully bagged most of the rich cropp ng. here
is a hole 10ft. to 12ft. deep, now in decomposed
granite; it contains tin, but in very small quan-
tity as compared with the gorgeous cap. the Home-
ward Bound is down 35ft. to 40ft. Here, again, is a
pile of bags with beautiful ore in them, left to im-
press the unwily visitor. The owners have also suc-
ceeded in getting most of the rich outcrop into bags.
The Carpathia show has been more considerate m
this regard. A large quantity of tin ore is still
lying around, and it is certain there are several
thousands of pounds sterling to be yet gathered up.
But after that is done the owners will have to bring
themselves down to common earth, and get to work
on a narrow lode of probably very medium value.
Ardlethan tin shows in the past have all been of
the same character- —very rich on the top in the cap,
and very poor underneath. If The Bulletin owned any
of the “promising shows,” it would sell to the first
enemy it met.
* * * *
The Golden Gully, Tambaroora, at the
north of the Hill End field (N.S.W.), has been
promising big things for many moons; but some-
how it doesn’t appear able to get going. A start
was attempted last August, but the fortnight’s run
gave £25 for the gold obtained: the plant only put
through about 15 cubic yards of material per hour.
As the ground was hard and stony, and didn’t give
any promise of improving, camp was shifted to the
present site, where the initial difficulties are said to
have been now overcome. The co. ends its first year
with £1557 to the bad.
* * * *
New Wylie Creek (N.S.W.) is wondering
whether life is worth living. It made a loss of £137
last half-year, and if tin had not been an abnormal
price it shudders to think what might have hap-
pened. There doesn't seem much hope ahead, either ;
so the directors are keeping their eyes open for some
better job for their plant. #

Westralia’s State batteries have now
treated 879,750 tons of public ore for a recovery of
£3,268,532. Including the amounts paid for sands
and slimes, the total is £3,804,403.
* * * *
Collie (W.A.) looks like putting up a re-
cord in coal production this year. An increase of
50,000 tons over the previous year's figures was
shown in 1910 as a result of the Newcastle strike,
but the 1911 output did not soar quite so high. For
the first nine months of 1912, however, the produc-
tion has been much better than even the oldest in-
habitant anticipated, and the full year’s tonnage
promises to exceed that of 1910 by 40,000.
After thinking the matter over. Great
Boulder (W.A.) has decided to take a 12 months’
working option on the Great Victoria, at Nevoria.
The show has an area, of 96 acres, with a long run on
the line of lode. Like the Bullfinch it is situated in
a break in the country, which has thrown the lode
east of south by north of west, with an underlie to
the north-west. Since the Boulder took a prelimin-
ary option in August a bit of money has been ex-
pended in development, with, on the whole, rather
low-grade results. It is, however, possible that the
property may give a prof, if worked on a big scale,
and it is on this that the Boulder has decided to
gamble.
* * * *•
Luptons’ (W.A.) is calling shareholders
together to discuss the future. Owing to the shoot
shortening between the 100 ft. and 150 ft. workings,
only 200 instead of the 1000 tons estimated by the
manager were disclosed. Disappointing intermediate
stopes also helped to embarrass the co., and as there
was nothing left to crush, the mill had to be shut
down. It is proposed to further test the few small
capricious shoots in deeper ground, but these are
poor assets to buy shares on.
* * * *
Bullfinch Prop. (W.A.) has met with
some good values at 210 ft. in what is known as the
northern series. These workings are some distance
from the original holes and excavations, and are
turning out a good deal better in depth than they
looked at surface. All going well, the treatment
plant is expected to be running before Christmas,
and the mine will then have a chance to show its
real capacity.
Kurnalpi township (W.A.) is again in
reduced circumstances. Applications for leases have
ceased, and as an exodus of “shepherders” has started,
it is doubtful if much of the ground applied for will
have a pick put into it. Binti Binti is now the ren-
dezvous of most of Kurnalpi’s recent admirers, and
quite a number have already begun to “speak highly”
of its prospects. Until more proof of Binti’s value is
forthcoming, however, it had better be viewed with
the cold eye.
* * * *
For the life of them, local optimists can-
not understand why Gwalias (W.A.) don’t soar. Some
fairly good developments have certainly taken place
since the slump, but as the aggregate amount paid in
dividends in the past nine months is more than has
been earned, and as the present rate of distribution
is not likely to be maintained much longer, it looks
as if the market is not far wrong in this casp.
After all, the valuation of a developed irlirie depends
on what is in sight; and to make a share worth £1
there should be at least 12s. per share p
sight, with no Sign of impoverishment in the bottom
workings. Unfortunately, this basis of valuation is
rarely adopted, with the result that most mines are
over-valued. . *
* * *
In announcing that it still exists, Golden
Pole (W.A.) states that royalties amounting to £9l
were received during the half-year ended September
30, that the profit and loss account now shows
a debit of £125, and that an additional £o7B has
been written off the now well-worn plant and
machinery. The tributors’ agreement expires at the
end of this year, but a six months’ extension has
been granted, in the hope that the mine will be kept
alive a little # »
Marvel Loch (W.A.) has decided to do
some more development, and let ore-treatment
slide for the present. If the erection of the mill
had not been hurried while the mine was allowed to
take its chance, this stoppage would not have hap-
pened. There was very little payable ore available
when the stamps were dropped; consequently the co
was soon in trouble, and the shares were knocked
out. 9

* * * *
There was a “river picnic” on the Queens-
land Government steamer Otter the other day, fol-
lowed by an inspired par. to the effect that the con-
struction of two N.Q. railways by Private Enter-
prise had been discussed. Later, another par. “under-
stood” that the lines in question were an extension of
the railway from the Duchess, at Cloncurry, and one
to Mount Mulligan. The latter might have been ex-
pected, in view of the Chillagoe Co.’s wire-pulling.
Also, Railway Commissioner Evans lately had a trial
made of the coal, with results described as “emi-
nently satisfactory.” Mount Mulligan is about 12
miles north-west of the old Thornborough goldfield
township, on the west side of the Hodgkinson River,
and is a remarkable feature in the landscape. The
range runs north and south for about 11 miles. The
eastern face is extremely precipitous; in fact, so far
as is known, there is only one track to the summit.
Coal was discovered there some four years ago by
the late W. J. W. Richards, an old identity of the
district, and the seams were prospected by John
Moffat. At first ash contents were high (about 20
p.c.), but, according to late reports, the quality has
improved as the seam has been driven on. The start-
ing point of any railway would be between Mareeba
and the Walsh River, on the Chillagoe line. One
objection to the construction being passed on to
Private Enterprise is that the Government is pledged
to connect the Cooktown-Laura line with Mungana,
via Maytown, and this private line would be a link in
that chain. Further, on past experience, P. Enterprise
would charge from 50 to 70 p.c. higher freights than
the Government lines. Still, the line is needed. Apart
from its value in opening up this far-north coalfield,
it would, in a short time, open up also the Mitchell
lands, which are eminently suitable for agriculture.
There is a movement afoot to give Mount
Molloy (N.Q.) another chance; and as the copper
market is favorable, the present seems a good time
to do it. The co. which holds the property is in
50,000 pref. and 100,000 ordinary shares. The idea
is to issue 50,000 paids (not prefs.) to the old pref.
shareholders, and offer the old ordinaries share for
share on payment of 2s. The latter shares will then
be counted as paid to 16s. The old co. overtaxed its
strength in building a railway from Biboora, on the
Cairns line, and had the bad luck to get into a poor
zone when it wasn’t feeling very well. Besides the
railwa.y and the mine it owns a smelting plant.
Under the improved conditions the mine certainly de-
serves another chance. It is a case of back-up or
forfeit, and the chances are much too good to think
of forfeiting.
* * * *
During the first nine months of this year,
Charters Towers treated 103,011 tons for £237,099,
which is not quite up to last year’s average. Bril-
liant Extended heads the list with 26,200 tons for
£48,008. Mills’ Day Dawn and the New Brilliant
Freeholds follow close at heel. Brilliant Stockholm
(5669 for £24,166) maintains a worthy aver-
age of nearly £4 55., spread over 5669 tons. Bril-
liant Deeps, though it hasn’t hatched a dividend,
tops the field with an average of £7 per ton from
1684 tons. The Golden Horn, at Mount Leyshon,
was a big ore-producer (21,268 tons) in the early
part of the year; but the value (£6190) shows
where the trouble lies. Falling under the profit-
margin, it sold its surface furniture to the Standard
Tin Mines, which hope to find better use for it.
Mount Morgan at £3,000,000 (£3 per
share) is the highest-priced stock on the Australian
mining market. Three years ago (Sept., 1909)’, with
the red metal quoted at £59 Is. 3d., shares were
735., or 13s. higher than they are now, when copper
is £75. The fact is, Mount Morgan has only recently
emerged from an unforeseen struggle with smelting
difficulties, and, to date, dividends have fallen far
short of expectations. But things are not going to
stay like that. The metal is there, and the co. has the
means to procure the best brains and machinery td
get it out.
* * * *
The striking feature of the Mount Cuth-
bert (Q.) half-yearly report is the small quantity
of concentrating ore disclosed —70,000 tons of 4 p.c.
copper, compared with 150,000 tons estimated to
average 8 p.c. Assuming that very high recoveries
are made—9o p.c. from high-grade and 80 p.c.
from low-grade ore—there is 13.040 tons of copper
available. Valued at £65 per ton, this means
£847,600. Very nice figures to look at, no doubt;
but keep in mind that that value is in 220,000 tons
of ore, so the stuff doesn’t average £4 per ton.
Mount Elliott’s costs for mining, smelting, convert-
ing, etc., cannot be much under £4 per ton of ore.
Before the mining costs increased the cost on blister
was no doubt occasionally cut down to £2B per ton,
or (say) £3 10s. per ton of ore. But under ordinary
circumstances, and counting administration and re-
newals in, even £32 (£4 per ton of ore) is an
optimistic est'mate. In view of this, Cuthbert’s
figures are not very cheerful.
Mount Cuthbert’s Warwick Castle lease,
Cloncurry (Q.), is showing encouraging prospects. A
shaft is being sunk on the north-west outcrop, and a
sample taken from 15ft. assays 25 p.c. copper. This
lease has been held for a long time, and a good deal
of pottering work has been done on it with very little
success. But it is easy to miss a shoot of ore, as the
old owners of the Young Australia, near Hampden,
now know to their cost.
* * * *
Harry Dodd and George Bryant fill the
eye at Cathcart meetings (Vic.). Dodd does the
sneaking, and George Bryant, as manager, prods the
plan with a big stick, to let as much light as
would come through a church window on to the
mystery of this particular alluvial mine. The co.
seemingly has about 2000 fathoms cut up and fairly
well drained; this is off No. 3 shaft, whence from
the drives 83 fathoms have given 2530 z. This is a
better record than the wash from No. 2 shaft, and if
Bryant’s estimate of a length of 2000 ft. of gutter
to the Central Cathcart isn’t 500 ft. too long, there
should be a nice string of dividends yet to come
out of the claim. The “bull” points are that the
Central Cathcart is draining the gutter fob the
Cathcart, thus reducing costs for the latter; that
Cathcart has every chance of at least 2oz. dirt; and
that such a yield means more in divs. than the
present market price of the shares. Up the gutter,
some 1700 ft. of the lead gave only 20s. in divs.;
but the far-up part was poor. Good ground was left
in the face when the level was lost off the No. , 2
shaft, so the bulls may again have a victory. All
the same, the market has the tired feeling about
Cathcarts, and it will continue that way unless
Bryant can jerk in some gilt-edged wash of the
Central Cathcart kind.
Long Tunnel, Walhalla (Vic.), won’t re-
spond to the eloquence of Chairman Rowe or the
language of ’Enery Hartrick, the local orator.
Whether the mine should be shut down or the reserve
used up was fought out some months back. Wal-
halla naturally wanted the money spent, because a
liquidator’s div. wouldn’t do the township any good,
whereas, while money is being spent on it, there is
always a chance of a hit in a mine with a good
record. The co. has already chewed up £7OOO of
its reserve, leaving only £4OOO to carry on with,
and its hope of picking up gold in the bottom levels
is small enough to go through any needle’s eye. Ore
reserves now are under 5000 tons of about Bdwt. stuff,
which won’t do much more than meet expenses. Wal-
halla wants local directors and a try at the big block
of ground between the incline shaft and the old Long
Tunnel ground. The drain on reserves ought long
since to have led to prospecting here as a stand-by to
the old mine, but the board seems to be too beastly
superior to do more than shepherd the block. Any-
way, if shilling monthly calls have to come, as is
threatened hy Chairman Rowe, Walhalla won’t have
to worry over getting control of the board. Hart-
rick’s hope of an English float to test the neglected
slabs of the co.’s big lease may come quite easily
then.
## * #
Alexandra Reefs, in N.W. Vic., has hauled
down the flag, after pottering about for five years
or so, trying to find the gold that the old miners
didn’t leave behind. Finlayson was brought from
Long Tunnel to pull things together, but he couldn’t
do the impossible, whatever he could achieve as a
shaft-sinker. All told, the co. has got £6600 from
gold. It has cost £22,000 to put up this record, so
the truth that the Alexandra’s glory had departed
ought to be now firmly established. Nevertheless,
another consultation is to be held to decide whether
to wind up or spend more cash. The obvious is hard
to see sometimes.

Mount Lyell has two or three strings to
play on while the North Lyell is being flooded, but
they are all thin. It can hunt round the Crown Lyell
for ore: it could help the Tasman and Crovvn Lyell
to open up its much-vaunted galena lode; it might
go to the Peaks or take the Blocks on tribute, or
have a cut at the South Thar s's, or the Lyell Tharsis;
or it might even dig into its own South Lyell, and
make a stab at its lately-bought Comstock leases.
As it wants to keep its hold on the mining population,
it will probablv hunt up ore? in the Lyell Tharsis,
the Comstock and the Blow. Is it luck or what that
a co. like the Lyell should be so hard pressed for ore
in a big field like the West Coast? Why didn’t the
board fix its eye on the Rosebery district, where big
Bowes Kelly has bought in so heavily?

The Great Finch is moribund for the want
of a mine. Floated in Melbourne on the top of the
Bullfinch rush, it has tried its luck in other fields,
only to dribble out the pence that ought to have
been pushed back at once into shareholders’ pockets.
What is wanted is a cinematograph picture to illus-
trate the line-uo of the shareholders for their final
div. of 10s. per 100 shares. It might save other
people a lot of money some day.
H.A.D.: It’s a pretty dreadful result—
£s4o paid in premiums to the society during 15
years and £566 now payable by the society—but it
isn’t as dreadful as it looks. You survived the 15
years; plenty of other insurers didn t, and their
representatives have called for the insurance money.
Don’t forget that the people who live have to pay
for those who don’t —a point which most people
forget when they fall foul of. an insurance co. Also
vour assumntion that the £l3O “profit” goes to the
Equitable shareholders is wrong. The society s con-
tention is that it gave you insurance for 15 years at
an actual cost of £lO4 (which means that that was
your share of the cost of policies which became
claims —the policies of the people who died early),
and you now get £566; total £670. Premiums
paid," £540; balance, being benefits received in ex-
cess of premiums paid, £l3O. You certainly didn t
pocket and won’t pocket £lO4 of the total; but you
bought insurance with the money.... Inquirer: The
proposal, apparently, is that you shall get business
and whack it with a concern that doesn’t do a single
thing towards getting it. If it could offer you the
agency of half a dozen big, or even moderate, cos.,
it would be a different matter; but it can’t and
doesn't. #
This shows the history of the Advances
to Workers’ Department of the Maoriland State
Bank:—
Loan Exes.
Advances Written

Strictly, £4451 ought to be written off those poor
reserves, because the assets include a paper item of
that size, “Loan notation charges.” The Department
was established by an Act of 1906, its object being
to assist persons with comparatively small incomes
to get rid of the rent fiend. At the start it was
provided that the loans should be for 361 years.
That long, fixed term was a handicap, because either
•in many places the Supt. couldn’t see 361 years life
for the settlement, or would-be borrowers wouldn’t
tie themselves or their families for so long. Subse-
quently the Supt. was given more latitude; and then
the demand for the State’s'money at 41 p.c. interest
spurted ahead fast. The funds of the Dept, are avail-
able onlv to persons doing manual or clerical work,
and in receipt of not more than £2OO a year; also
the applicant must be prepared to show that he does
not own any land except that on which he proposes
to build. The advance is limited to £450, though
of course the building may cost more. The Dept,
provides plans, etc., free, from its stock of about a
score. The loans are not restricted to freeholders,
though the advance to leaseholders is naturally much
less, as a rule. At present the average freehold
advance is £302, and the average leasehold advance
£lBO. The advance is secured by a mortgage on the
property, including, of course, the land.
As the Department’s easy terms have become
known, the demand for help has grown fast. The
applications last vear numbered 2223, and 1624 ad-
vances were actually made. The money is raised by
the State, which can get it cheaper than any private
individual, because it has the entire taxable assets
of the country to offer as security. Hence it can
pass the money on at an exceedingly low rate of
interest; also, not being out after profit, it does pass
it on at a low rate. Certainly it wants to show a
profit, but that is merely in order to create a con-
tingency fund—a provision for probable bad debts.
The advances are so liberal—they may be up to three-
fourths of the value of land and building—that there
are bound to be losses at some time dr other. In the
12 months to March, 1911, £BB was called for in
that way, and in the past year £3 —not bis: sumS,
it is true, but liable to be much bigger should a,
slump hit Mabriland; For this reason it is highly
desirable that no further concessions should be made
to borrowers until there is a much more solid fund
than at present exists.

Sydney, Novembeb 4, 1912.
My Dear Arini. —
Rumor boldly asserts that Governor
Chelmsford would not want much pressing
to come back as Gov.-General of Australia.
This would follow the departure, about two
years hence, or less, of the Denmans, and
would fit in with the Chelmsford parental
duties, which now hurry them off to Britain.
If the G.-G. business does come to a head,
there will be many tea-fights and thanks-
giving services to welcome him back on
behalf of those who enjoy his Ex.’s conversa-
tional method of laying large clutches of
foundation stones. The last stone, by the
way, before the flight for the Cup, was a
half-grown brick wall in the Sydney Show
Ground. This inspired the sheep society’s
president, Francis Suttor, to very nearly let
pussy out of the vice-regal bag. “If he
(Lord Chelmsford) ever came back to Aus-
tralia in any capacity,” etc. Then the feline
jumped back to its hiding-place, and the
brick wall was declared to be well and truly
laid.
But can a poor peer wrestle in comfort
with the glory of being a Governor-General
on £lO,OOO a year? The Dudleys were a
financial mystery, and no outsider is likely to
know whether they were very mean and rich,
or merely hard-up. The Tennysons are the
only admittedly impecunious nobility who
have grappled with the prospect of saving a
bar of soap and a few candle-ends out of the
G.-G. wages. Hopetoun got through £30,000
or £40,000 of his personal cash; and the
Northcotes were wealthy and glad to share
their luck. The joyous Denmans hand out
their money with both hands on the slightest
provocation, and the N.S.W. Government
ought to have stuck to them like wax, as
being desirable immigrants who might even
have money to lend. All things considered,
and even with this State cut out of a Gov.-
General’s visiting list, the managing Chelms-
ford lady might have to reduce her household
to bread and dripping to keep pace with the
fiendish expense of being Top Lady in this
continent. Or else she might be sensible, and
simply let her husband do the serious job
which he was appointed to do, without troub-
ling about the frills of the business at all.
The Chelmsfords are rushing back from
the Cup on Friday morning for the purpose
of blessing the annual commemoration in St.
Andrew’s College. On Saturday his Ex. will
resume his favorite occupation of laying
foundation stones. This time, by the way,
it is a memorial stone in Pymble’s Anglican
Church, and, presumably, the nest will be
built of lmlf-mourning twigs lined with
black-edged bunting.
, Sydney’s Mayor and Mayoress departed
for the gilded Cup last week. The tall
Anderson girls are showing their pictur-
esque appearance in Melbourne’s dizzy race-
frivols. The Frank Brennans and Mrs.
Spark are sheltering at Menzies’ during the
same fury.
In the days when Victorian Customs
officers put their fists into Cup lingerie, or
turned a tulle hat inside out to see if it
concealed contraband loot, the wails of the
dressy ladies who flocked to Melbourne in
early November made the Albury welkin
ring. It was hard luck to see 12st. or so of
brawn and muscle stamp on the ephemeral
glory of a chiffon bow. But the wreck
wasn’t always past repair, and every
searcher in brass buttons didn’t wipe his
boots on _ gauzy petticoats. Now a new
terror smites the dame who lets her glad
rags out of her sight for five minutes. Some
special brand of race-time thief swoops
down on the baggage, boldly signs for the
lot, and vanishes without leaving a finger-
mark. Profound sobs are still shaking the
corsage hooks of a dressy Mosman matron,
who was the victim of an impudent baggage
robbery on the eve of her departure to the
Cup. Not a stitch was left wherein she
might appear “amongst those present,” ex-
cept the clothes on the lady’s back ; and they
belonged to the usual travel-order of hack
garment. So she made for home with leaden
feet.
T Mr- Justice Street unveiled the A. B.
Weigall memorial tablet in the Sydney
Grammar School.. The late and great head-
master was Plain English right through,
with no fancy trimmings or anti-Australian
prejudices. Yet the lines that trail across
this tablet, given by the youngsters who
once obeyed the Weigall birch, are buried in
a dead language. When the tail of the
Latin inscription is flogged into English it
has_ a Pecksniffian flavor, as a tribute from
Knickerbockers. “What does the Lord re-
quire of , thee.... and to walk humbly with
thy God, never grew in the quotation-patch
of the average healthy, noisy little animal
who yells for joy of life in the Grammar
School playground.

Two attempted garden parties were blown
to smithereens on Thursday afternoon, when
a sudden southerly lifted this season’s mil-
linery. several feet in the air. The long,
rambling pile of St. Paul’s College, which
usually breaks out in mild festivities at this
time of year, with Warden Radford in
charge, housed its garden-partying refugees
under the academic roof, or in any other
old place where the wild hullabaloo of wind
couldn’t uproot them. Victoria Barracks,
where it generally blows several
zephyrs strong even when the city
is lapped in calm, can produce the
finest cyclone effects in this town
when a southerly rampages across its ter-
raced gravel. Major Christian and other
warriors responsible for the R.A.F.A. struck
Thursday’s blast for their afternoon on the
lawn. The blast hit back so ferociously
that the visitors dashed indoors to scoop the
loose ends of Oxford-street out of their eyes.
The surprise wedding of last week was
the quiet orange-blossoming of Dorothy
Owen and the popular polo-man, Denis Gar-
van. The engagement was hinted at in this
letter some weeks ago, but I don’t think it
ever wandered into print in the usual court-
circular verbiage “arranged” or an-
nounced.” The bride is the eldest daughter
of Mr. H. P. Owen, Master-in-Equity, and
one of the best-liked men within the stiff
precincts of that dry-as-dust profession. Law
is a habit in the Owen family. A late
Judge of that name was the parent of the
Equity man. A half-brother to H.P. is
Percy Owen, sen., a solicitor by trade, but
mostly drawing something comfortable in
coal, dividends from down Wollongong way.
Various junior members of the tribe have
dived into the red taoe and parchment busi-
ness as soon as they cut their first whiskers.
The Bush Brotherhood, which is celibate
and ascetic within the Anglican fold, and
has its headquarters at Dubbo, has been
making its annual cadge for cash in the
drawing-rooms of our Best People. The cadge
is under vice-regal patronage, and generally
extracts a moderate amount of coin after
an extravagant interchange of tea and
shrimp sandwiches amongst the more serious
members of local society. The tall willow-
wand figure of Bush brother Feetham
swayed with enthusiasm as he discoursed
on the Necessity for More Cash in the vice-
regal parlor at Rose Bay one day last week,
ihe .willow wand had scarcely finished
swaying, by the way, when it received word
that it had just been chosen to be the new
Bishop of North Queensland, in succession
to the overworked (and underpaid) Frod-
sham.
Some vivid blue and green impressions of
Samoa, by Lucie Peapes, hang in a meek
corner of the Women Painters’ Show. In
this water-color section, Dorothy J. Stephen
is a clever young exhibitor, who leans ten-
derly on her brush when she dabbles in the
shadows that chase a summer sunset into the
phantom arms of Night. “Evening Glow” is
a soft remark in Dorothy’s best manner, with
a faint blush under its wan grey sky. Winnie
G. Elliott is unshaken in her devotion to
sacred tiles. . It is only for five minutes that
she steps aside from church architecture to
whisk her brush through a Cremorne bush
path.
Blanche Murphy, of Hobart, sent the
Women Painters’ rally some attractive sam-
ples of Tasmanian scenery, preserved in oils.
Bessie Pnng, of Queensland, is another long-
distance exhibitor who ought to be encour-
aged to send her wares this way. “The
Grey-hooded Even” (water-color) is mostly
blue mist cleverly handled, though its name
suggests a frilled lizard on the scoot.
1 The witty repartee of that brisk political
lady, Grace Watson, is about to be removed
from the South Australian platform. A
post-card fluttered in here the other day, men-
tioning her projected departure to England
some time this month. Grace has resisted
the entreaties of various “Liberal” bodies to
remain and help them slay their adversaries
m the next Victorian scramble for votes.
She’s also alleged to have said No to a Tas-
manian Moral Education League—a heavy-
weight which falls on frivolous youth like
40.000 bricks. A dark hint that the Watson
lady has been “offered a position in London”
doesn’t satisfy one’s hunger to know whether
she is going to take the militant suffragette
by u.i- uair of her head, and drag her back
to paths of peace, or merely goad domestic
servants to rush these shores.

That ancient charity, the Female School
of Industry, will get another lump or two of
sugar in its porridge as the result of the
recent tableaux in Concordia Hall. The
sprightly Mrs. Tom Watson took a hand;
Miss Ross-Johnson gave a push ; Mrs. Bob
Macdonald, Mrs. Buchanan and Miss Wil-
kinson also helped in the nerve-racking rush
to get hold of the Right People for the
tableaux. For weeks before the event
damp-blanket friends and enemies poured
cold water down the backs of the harassed
arrangers, and told them they were in dire
peril of getting the Wrong People for this
or that. “What! Is she going to be So-
and-So ? Good gracious! Why did you
choose her? Of course, she’ll be a dead
failure,” etc. Then the committee ladies
buckled on their fighting expression, stuck
their necks into the collar, and pulled the
show through to a glittering finish.

by Sydney’s little Mayoress one afternoon
last week.
The Arts and Crafts girls have been pol-
ishing up their grey matter to snare the
nimble shilling for their wares. November
4 and 5 are the days, so the show will have
slammed its door before this reaches you.
Australian designs on beaten brass, leather
and other likely mediums have been ex-
ploited, and the purchaser is expected to
forward the gifts by mail to catch the Eng-
lish Christmas blizzard.
Singer Emilie Lambert, of this town
(when she’s at home), writes from Paris,
where she is practising her vocal chords
under the ear of Bouliy. The ear is for-
tified by a studio designed to deaden echoes.
It is full of heavy furniture; and a thick
carpet on the floor aids in the capture of
false notes. In this abode, off the Champs
Elys6es, does the eminent baritone listen
for the ghost of a tremor in the voices
of hjs pupils. “It is not too enjoyable sing-
ing in such a room,” gasps Emilie in paren-
thesis ; “but one is there for work, rather
than for pleasure. Both Bouhy and Santley
(who gave me lessons while I was in
London) are complimentary about my voice
and general musical ability. Santley says
the quality is purely Spanish (for which I
must thank a Spanish grandmother), and is
never heard in an English voice.” Then
Emilie digresses into notes of admiration re
the Paris Opera House and other ornaments
of the French capital.
James Edmond drops in from the smok-
ing-room of the Kurnano Maru, in Northern
waters: —•
I am becoming acquainted with the Captain and
the cookery and the ship’s cat. They are all good.
The only bad thing on board is the ship’s pen. There
are two pens on the inkstand, and each one of them
is worse than both of them put together. Passed Cape
Moreton this morning. I had my early education
there on the old Rodondo, now, I hope, dead and
gone to its great reward. It was many years ago,
and the cargo consisted chiefly of myself and cock-
roaches. Owing to insufficient cockroaches, the ship
stood up like a wall, and when the gale struck us
we tottered on our last legs ,on the face of the waters
and put one foot in the grave. For two hours we
struggled to get round. Cape Moreton, in the hope
that once round it we might crawl in shore in the
lee of a prawn, or a blade of grass or something. I
had one companion on deck on that voyage, a man
very much older than myself. He was swearing in
three dead languages—l knew they were dead
because they were s'o high. I proceeded to make
a few observations also, but he said my style was
feeble, and I was only watering the milk. So Cape
Moreton is associated with my early education in the
days when the Rodondo wallowed with us and a
heavy cargo of cockroaches.
Mr. J. Spencer Brunton came back from
Europe’s war-worn face ahead of his family.
They still linger in the grey old city by the
Thames.
Who’s the Hqn. Thomas McClintock
Bunbury, I wonder? When lie turned up
his cuffs for work, he was private secretary
to Big George Reid. A guarded remark that
Bunbury is about to step out of his Com-
monwealth billet to draw a wage at the
Imperial Institute floats through the Eng-
lish press.
When last heard of, the seraphic smile
of Percy Grainger was located in Berlin. If
the Adelaide pianist has found time to get
engaged or married, the news hasn’t reached
this side of Australia, to the best of my
belief. When the ethereal-looking Percy
was in Sydney a few years ago, Mamma
Grainger was so frequently at his elbow that
impressionable maidens could only fling an
enraptured glance at his golden thatch from
a 10 or 20 yards’ range.
Baronet Joe Ward (M.L.) is reported to
be packing up his hairbrushes for another
jaunt to London. The rumor that reached
my ear had a determined pretence of being
on the mark, and mentions details. The
flight will be soon and hurried, and Mrs.
Baronet and the daughter are likely to be
at Ward’s right hand on the trip.
Mr. R. Morris, who is postmaster in
Christchurch (M.L.) when he’s at work, is
a tennis enthusiast in his spare moments.
He found the health he was searching for in
a recent holiday saunter round the South
Sea islets, and takes it with him to Mel-
bourne after a look-in here. The Davis Cup
is the sight he’s going to sample in Victoria.
The wedding of Barnum-Powell was pre-
ceded by a vigorous course of judicious social
advertising in the London press. The bride-
elect, Miss Soames, was perpetually inter-
viewed and profusely illustrated with that
fine showman, B. P., by her side. Barnum,
of Mafeking, practically admitted that he
couldn’t teach this Commonwealth how to
shoulder arms. But I notice he still pushes
the small boy scout to the front in his en-
gaged and wedding press ads. Lady B.
Powell (then Miss Soames) interviewing
an awe-stricken little urchin on a Scout
launch, seemed fairly on the track to be a
Mrs. Barnum of some tonnage.
Tenor Sid Macdonald will be handed a
complimentary concert in the Town Hall,
towards the end of this month. A strong-
minded, able-bodied committee is fighting
for success. It remarks, in a pale-green
circular, that this local singer has been
always ready to uplift his voice in the cause
of charity. H. N. Southwell, one of the
conveners in charge of the affair, is at pre-
sent viewing the Horse at Flemington.
In a tame week it is exhilarating to dip
into the London half-penny fashion notes
which provide a suitable diet for the silly
season. “Only one thing,” says the shrill
voice of caustic comment, “will be lacking
from the well-dressed woman’s outfit this
season —a whipping.” Style, it appears, will
clothe the female form in peg-top skirts.
These garments are after the Turkish-De-
light harem cut, only rather more so. They
bag at the hips and waist, and harrow down
to a yard or less about the knees and ankles,
to which they cling like a glove. They are
slit at the sides to give the enclosed lady a
chance to say her prayers. Variety being the
spice of the dressmaker’s bills, the 1913 lady
who objects to lobk like a top will swathe
herself in penguin drapery. This Antarctic
fancy is made in the form of a black-and-
white beaded-cloth coat. Its small stiff
sleeves are built to look like the abbreviated
side-whiskers or wings of the Emperor pen-
guin.
Prom Chicago comes a fat roll of songs,
written and published by Olive Chryse
Parker, of the City of Pork —the place from
which the Self-Made Hog wrote the Letters
(on crackling) to his Son. As a handsome
sample of the American art of advertising,
this alluring music pile leads ordinary shop-
window goods by a few lengths. The songs,
set to Mrs. Olivh’s new compositions, are
“You,” “A Twilight Song,” “A Winter’s
Day,” etc. They ought to sell like hot pies
for their covers, which are decorated with
violets as large as life, and as perfect as the
best three-color process can make them.
The melodies that flow inside are full of
new digressions from the conventional rut.
The W.C.T.U. Convention usually combs
out of the morals of this city about the time
the Horse is whisking round the Cup in
Melbourne. At present, the Cold Tea-party
is getting its elastic-sided boots into march-
ing trim, so as to make the little grocer with
the wine license fly for his life, or drown
himself in a tank of lemonade. The Order
of the Swift Get Out will also he handed
to the barmaid when the W.C.T.U. can place
its serious black-cotton gloves on some vul-
nerable place in that haughty damsel’s
armor. The Ginger-beer ladies affirmed,
the other day, through the mouth of Mrs. C.
Stewart, that they heard “from reliable
sources” that the Japanese have wo war-like
intentions. You will also be pleased to hear,
Arini, that the sick and poor round about
Sydney are being fed, gratis, by the
W.C.T.U. —on temperance tracts. In return.
I move a resolution that a book called “The
Tragedy of Korea” be placed beside the
peaceful water-jug of the next Band of Hope
convention.
Chappell and Co., Ltd., send along a small
library of music. Before dipping into the
score, I notice, that this firm is breaking out
in the American manner by “featuring”
daring covers. “The Pelican’s Parade,” a
simple one-step hop, by Dorothy Forster,
has a procession of educated pelicans, of
the afternoon-block species, stamping across
its cover. “The Clown’s Serenade,” de-
scribed as an intermezzo two-step, by a
composer with the florid name Pedro de
Zulueta, is so gorgeously covered that it's
inside is sampled with the respect it de-
serves. It is a melodious trifle suitable for
the technique of Miss Flapper’s younger
sister. “Beyond the Meadow Gate” (a
vocal duet), “Sweet Eyes of Blue” (same
old idea in a new dress), and other honeyed
ditties are up to the average love-song.
Dorothy Forster sets some tuneful chords
to accompany Harold Simpson’s “Wonder-
ful Garden of Dreams,” which has the
fanciful refrain that floats on “Wings of
Love” and similar aeroplanes of uncertain
temper.
Yours affectionately,
VANDORIAN.
J. Moore Robinson, who is F.R.G.S. as
well as journalist, has given up the job of
sub-editing the Hobart Daily Post, to be
secretary of the Tasmanian Tourist Associa-
tion at £350 a year. As Robinson can
enthuse mellifluously anent the Speck’s
numerous beauty spots, he ought to prove a
success in the billet. Last year he pub-
lished an interesting booklet on the origin of
Tasmanian place-names, and reaped a har-
vest of considerable praise.
