MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, November 4, 1912.
My Dear Yarrie, —
The padded breast of the Naval and Mili-
tary Club expanded for its ball on the night
before Derby Day. Colonel Burston shared
host duties with Admiral Creswell, and their
wives—the one in black-and-white elegance
and the other with a Rule Britannia-look-
lng drape flung nobly over one shoulder of
her otherwise pale gown —both helped in the
initial handshaking of the thousand-odd
guests. The Government House people
arrived from the Royal almost as late as 11
o clock, and so kept the usual pompous open-
in S gyrations hanging fire, what time Ad-
miral Creswell remarked with heavyweight
wit, “Baffles has committed a social bur-
glary.” The G.-G. looked sleepy—not a
good advertisement for the other amateur
show he had come from. Lady Chelmsion,
was unassumingly frocked in a black filmy
thing over a grey silk. Several red-coated
land warriors and black-and-gold naval men
swelled the string of aides that followed.
Ten minutes after this turn the Fuller mus-
tard waggons sprinted up to the door and
dumped a flustered party. Following on
the yellow streaks came whirring cars of
every color and size, containing other
theatregoers eager for a three hours frolic.
The black dresses scored in the medley of
color. Mrs. Atlee Hunt was distinguished
in sable attire; and about the most attrac-
tive female on the premises was a girl in
all-black, with pink cameo earrings
and cameo-pink cheeks. George Fuller’s
daughter, from Sydney, created a sensation
in a gold and blue dress, like a sunny day
on the harbor. Little Miss Rouse, another
smart Sydney girl, was a dainty spectacle
with her very black hair and very white
dress. Bride Mrs. Lebbeus Hordern was
one of those who enjoyed the dance; but
lots of Sydney visitors stayed indoors and
spent the evening taking the creases out of
their Derby finery for the morrow. Ivy
Clarke inhabited a pink dress and sported
a red rose in her hair. Bigjohn Forrest
wore a large smile, his dress clothes, ana a
broad belt of . ribbon round his well-ironed
chest. Captain Crespin, the new Gentle-
man Help, had his curls shorn close to avert
jealousy. It was a glad, gay scene; yet,
perhaps, the decorations had symbolical in-
tentions. The dome of the big hall dripped
with blood-red roses and the lights shone
through scarlet carnations.
The Derby Day rain fell very softly until
the women decided to risk it, and go forth
in their frail frocks. But by the time the
luncheon interval had arrived, it was obvi-
ous th'at there was something seriously
wrong with the heavenly plumbing, and that
the repairs would be a matter of hours —or
days. Hundreds of tables under the elms
had been eagerly secured, and the anxious
hostesses measured with their eyes the dis-
tance between grandstand and feeding shel-
ter. The wet Terrace tiles were as slippery
as glass when society, with its filmy dresses
held up to safety mark, scuttled lightly over
them like a vast flock of birds with colored
legs. At least one woman went down in all
her finery.
. The Government House people drove out
in ceremonious fashion, with horses and
postilions. They crowded the ofiic.,.! pen
in the grandstand, and most of them stayed
there. The various Governors dived off the
steps at intervals as into a river, and re-
turned dripping from the paddock, with bet-
ting tickets in their mouths. The G.-G.,
neatly garbed in black, was businesslike and
serious. Chelmsford looked expansively
bored in a grey frock suit and grey top hat.
The Denman lady wore a criss-cross orange
and black top to her black gown; her head-
gear comprised expensive and unruly white
plumes. The Chelmsford lady looked like a
soft little grey squirrel. Mrs. King-Hall was
in soft, mouse-colored tailorings; and the
Bosanquet twins brightened up the box with
gay little nodding pink roses in their hats.
Auntie Phipps was impressive and handsome
m a hat that shot heavenward at one side.
An adequate supply of Gentleman Helps
stood shoulder to shoulder with her, and she
was one of the few women who dared to ven-
ture from shelter.
Many egrets were ruined. Mrs. Jack Wal-
lace wandered round with a big bunch of the
feathers waving loosely on the extreme back
edge of a wide hat brim. Mrs. Norton Grim-
wade had a bashed collection hiding somewhere
in the meshes of a startling, red-spotted veil.
Mrs. Trenchard had brown bits of the plun-
der set above a rose chiffon, fur-trimmed
gown that never should have been taken out
m such weather. Mrs. George Kelly was
wrapped, regally in an expanse of purple oil-
skin, with purple ostrich dusters weeping
from her crown. Eugenie Duggan Anderson
wore some plumes on the port side of her
hat.. Maud Ogilvie, a Sydney girl who is
staying with one of the Governors, showed
a glimpse of pretty white neck under the rim
of a canary-colored hat, and turned neat
white linen shoulders to a staring world
while she talked to Chamberlain Nevill.
Some brave woman buried in the massed mil-
linery of the vice-regal box supported the
allegedly unlucky peacock feathers. Mrs.
Everard Browne’s sporting enthusiasm
took her into the open, despite the fact that
she was supporting a priceless hat.
The Grey-Egerton lady was almost ex-
tinguished under a rain-coat; but daughter
Aimee Clarke walked bravely in blue, and
dared the weather. Mrs. Leslie Macdonald
wore a big, white plush collar ''to her black
velvet motoring coat. She hostessed her
lunch party; then, having done her social
duty, motored home through the mud. Mrs.
“Dewar” Thiel walked handsomely in sap-
phire blue with a well-cut blue surcoat
swinging smartly.
It was a big day, and the great crowd
stayed bravely till the last race, hoping
against hope for some sunshine. The foun-
tain on the lawn fired back bravely at the
unrelenting heavens; but to the very end of
the day people were cowering under acres
of black umbrellas.
Maoriland sent over a fairly big contin-
gent of Cup junketers this year, amongst
them the Studholmes, who own sheep and
acres in fertile Wairarapa. About 10 years
ago they were married from the Hopetouns’
whare, the bride, Miss Wauchope, being a
cousin of the Linlithgow lady. On this visit
Mrs. Fred Payne is their hostess.
Last week, Mrs. Davey slid gracefully out
of the centre chair on the mayoral dais by
giving a party to welcome Mrs. D. V.
Hennessy. It was a very well-dressed party,
yet very informal, and with a unique homely
charm. Hennessy and the outgoing Davey
distributed brotherly smiles on the staircase.
Mrs. Davey had Mrs. Charlie Beauchamp, a
vision in blue, to help her greet the guests.
Mrs. Davey’s black ostrich feathers eclipsed
anything yet torn out of the exaggerated
fowl that is foolish enough to grow them.
Mrs. Ilennessy’s principal outbreak was in
beautiful lace.
Mrs. George Chirnside’s ball for her
debutante daughter excited St. Kilda last
Thursday. The local Town Hall, which
still shows a bare, unfinished face of brick
to the world, was made more presentable by
outside decorations, consisting of potted pop-
lars and silver beeches, with lights in their
branches. The same fairylike glims dimly
illuminated cosy sitting-out corners cushi-
oned in red. (While on the subject, I may
say that some of the Very Best People re-
cently discovered that English girls were
brought up under glass. The system is being
carefully imitated here. It promises to be
irksome for the local flapper, who has quite
a different environment.) The supper-room
of the raw edifice was also rescued from its
plain everydayness by green silk wall-hang-
ings and rose-patterned cretonne covers for
the chairs. Mrs. Stewart McArthur’s dress
was the most sensational garment of the
evening. It was like a leopard’s skin—
tawny dull silk, with brown, velvety spots,
and almost as close-fitting as tbe big tree-
cat’s pelt. The small debutante Chirnside
daughter was a lovely, fragile picture in
white; and Mamma, in blue and silver,
looked not more than two and a half years
older.
At last week’s Chirnside dance Lord
Dicky Nevill was in charge of Governor
Bosanquet’s twin girls, the handsome Miss
Ogilvie (Sydney) and the Barttelot couple.
The Madden dame, besides daughters Lesly
and Ruby Osborne, had niece Bingham
Hordern and her diamonds under her wing.
The Grey-Egerton lady, in paddy-green, wore
her tiara well over her eyebrows, according
to the latest fashion; but it didn’t prevent
her from keeping a watchful eye on daugh-
ter Aimee and the Grey-Egerton miss. Mrs.
Everard Browne’s three flappers, with their
hair hanging down their backs, followed the
doings of a bravely-clad mommer. Mrs. Ber-
tram Chambers wandered about in a
shadowy lace garment, and Mrs. Klug
carried a butter-hued train of ninon encum-
bered with ermine. Miss Aileen Bell, from
Queensland, looked well in amber; so did
the pretty Lascelles girl, from Geelong way,
in leaf green decked with roses. Mrs. Fred
Keats contributed a Burne-Jones sunset
effect to the gathering.
Louise Smith-Dyer and her husband
brought off their rather solemn frivol in the
picture room at the Exhibition. The youth-
ful matron, wearied by many hours of wait-
ing for a promised Paris garment, eventually
clothed her sadness in a two-weeks-old gown
of pale banana satin, top-dressed with gold
thread. Mrs. D. V. Hennessy, in a magpie
arrangement, clutched a bunch of roses and
looked about with a nervous smile. The
Weedon dame was a comfortable figure in
blue. The function aimed at novel effects,
and small dancing maidens loaded the guests
with vegetable offerings. The programme
of music was divided into two parts, the
latter half being given in the fernery among
the parrots (caged variety). Meanwhile,
occasional cooling drops from the overhang-
ing foliage fell with deadly precision on bare
shoulders and caused various alarms. One
victim pulled her top skirt over her bare-
ness, and looked like Mrs. Ajax defying the
rainstorm.
The Denman lady, with summer flowers
in her rustic hat, made the opening remarks
at the Rose Show last week, when the in-
terior of the Town Hall was transformed
into a dewy Eden without the snake. The
Botanical Gardens contributed barrowloads
of non-competitive blooms ; Rossi and Alis-
ter Clarke (who boasts one of the show gar-
dens of Victoria) had stands in the centre;
Rosarian women hung baskets brimful of
blooms round the balcony; and somebody
had scattered a huge armful of full-blown,
pink and white Mildred Grants. Poor Mil-
dred is no longer fashionable; but she is
fragrant and her tinting is lovely, so she is
still received in rose society. It was a
record year for entries —and, judging by the
talk, a record year also for the collapsible
caterpillar and other plagues.
After attending to the flower function, the
Denman lady ran upstairs to give a fare-
well pat to the Arts and Grafts Exhibition,
which, for a prosperous fortnight, had been
a tenant of the upper supper-rooms.
“Raffles,” for sweet charity’s sake, drew
only a smart and motored crowd to the
Royal. Even the front rows of the gallery
had been reserved at 6s. a space, and the
mere public was represented only by a hand-
ful of folk ’way back among the gods, who
looked as though they had wandered in by
accident. The vice-regals in the centre of
the d.c. were flanked by the Mayoral Daveys
on one hand, and by a young parson with a
lilies-of-the-valley buttonhole on the other —
apparently the chaplain-in-ordinary to the
Excellencies. “Raffles” turned out to be a
supremely foolish melodrama under non-
professional treatment. The company did
well enough individually—nobody failed at
every point—but the wild impossibility of
it all verged on the ridiculous whenever the
plot thickened. Captain Curwen struggled
pluckily with the difficult character of Bun-
ny, Mrs. Arthur Harris was weaker in her
walk than in her talk, and Mrs. Guy Miller,
Miss Athol \Levey, Miss May Ryan and other
amateurs of importance wanted some of the
confidence of Spencer Holroyd, the old-
stager of the crowd.
Matthew Davies’s daughter Muriel, who
already wears a scientific train of letters
after her name, took the degree of Doctor
of Medicine the other day. Now she is to
have the degree of Mrs. to wear at the other
end of her name. Dr. Konrad Hiller and a
clergyman will be the other parties to this
ceremony, which is to happen on November
6, at Mentone, where the one-time very
wealthy knight and his family have been
living lately.
Nina and Joan Bagot and Edith Hope-
Campbell, three of Melbourne’s dog-
admirers, have cheerfully cut out the races
and gone off for a motor tour. Shadow, an
aristocratic black Pomeranian, is included
in the party. The Bagot sisters can man-
age a car as well as their brother, Harry
Bagot, who fills the place in motoring and
cycling circles that his father, the late R. C.
Bagot, used to occupy in the horse-racing
world. The adventurous girls are making
west, and will soon have the Ninety-mile
Desert within the field of their acetylene
searchlights. They carry food and cooking
utensils, as well as other light luggage. If
they happen to reach a hotel at nightfall
they will condescend to sleep there; but all
meals will be prepared and taken on board
the travelling; gas-engine. Time is ,no ob-
ject, 'and delighted progress reports have
already been wafted back from Cadiper-
down, Warrnambool and Port Fairy.
■ Walter Kirby had about an audience and
a half for his concert in the Masonic Hall
last Wednesday evening. A Gaud Mayoral
party, consisting of the waning Daveys and
the waxing Hennessys, flanked a Madden
detachment in the centre seats. General
Gordon and daughter Eileen, with a red rose
in her hair, sat still from beginning to end
of the lengthy programme. Mrs. Reggie
Clarke-Power, who is occasionally guilty of
writing little songlets, was wrapped in a
soft blue silk cloak. Mrs. Bowes Kelly
showed an expanse of fine shoulders to the
house from her seat in the second row. On
the platform, pretty little Margery Grey
played her violin brilliantly, and May
Griffiths attended to the tenor’s accompani-
ments. May’s soulful black eyes and yel-
low dress provided a contrast which fasci-
nated most people while she was visible.
Blanche Browne, in a slender blue sum-
mer frock, was recently observed on the
dummy of an up-town car. When she had
found her blue ticket, she placed her glove-
less left hand flat on the back of the seat.
Then the other passengers observed with
breathless interest that a wedding-ring was
shining on the third finger, and began to
.lump to conclusions. By this time I esti-
mate there are 27,842 relations and
friends jumping to conclusions. Personally,
I think Blanche is either ( a ) married, or
(b) just wants to see how many people
would jump.
Colonel liupert Carrington’s son, Rupert
the second, is returning to Australia after
paying >a visit to his uncle, ex-Governor
Carrington, of N.S.W. By the way, Auntie
Isabel Horsfall is booked to wed with a
Scotsman in the near future. The name of
the bridegroom-elect is a secret which her
closest friends have been unable to extract
from her. His business is understood to be
squatting in Maoriland. The lady announces
that she intends to proceed to the altar with-
out bridesmaids or wedding guests.
The big hotels are bursting with a record'
Cup crowd. The Oriental, in Collins-street
completed its re-furbishing for the glad
event, and the noiseless automatic lifts that
go up like saints’ prayers and come down
like feathers dropped from angels’ wings
take tired people from the cool, enclosed
garden where the climbing roses bloom, to
the flat roof where the breeze caresses the
head superheated by fatigue, betting losses
and other annoyances.
The Grand gave an “Arrival Party” on
Friday—the day when Sydney and Adelaide
fall into Melbourne for the race carnival.
Her Majesty’s grand opera orchestra played
a dozen or so selections, and tenor v\ alter
Kirby sang some Puccini.
St. Ivilda is watching the development of
Luna Park, ’way down wnere ' L)ream-
land” came and went some years ago. The
park just now is a mass of scaffolding and
hurrying workmen; but the huge moon-face
that is to be the main entrance is flashing
with gold-leaf, and some of the side-show
corners are taking shape. Sentimentalists
are to be provided for by a sort of covered
gutter, along which boats for two will
slowly drift. "Dreamland,” of yore, had
this in a smaller way, and patrons generally
found an Australian small boy ready to hoot
at them from the mountains of Switzerland.
After voyaging down the tunnel or _ drain,
the couples will be able to climb a flight of
marble steps, sit on a Family Herald ter-
race, and view the sea while absorbing coffee
or American drinks.
Beatrice Yaldwyn, a Bananaland beauty-
girl who has relations in Toorak, has at last
realised a stage dream. She has been in
Beerbohm Tree’s school for two years, and
recently “walked on” in a Tree production.
Now she has been engaged by Marie Tem-
pest for her tour through Ireland, and hope-
ful relatives are expecting Beatrice to turn
every rising star green with envy.
A. E. Delaney, who advanced on Europe
by the Siberian route, has proved that Dad
sometimes makes a good chaperone. He has
brought daughter Doris back to the family
circle at Kew with the significant diamond
ring glittering on the promised hand. The
fiance was attached in Paris, but the en-
gagement is to remain “a dead secret” till
it has been well tested by absence. Hence
the profound silence of these remarks.
' From my Adelaide correspondent: —
The Victoria Park racecourse was packed with Mayor
Bonython’s ratepayers on Saturday. Stirred by the lav-
ish efforts of his predecessors, Cohen, our present Mayor
used the course for the purpose of a “lawn fete,” as
they called the thing in the “Belle of New York.”
Heaven knows where civic hospitality is going to
end up. A trip round the world in a specially char-
tered P. and O. liner will probably be the next de-
velopment.
Mrs. Parsons received her brother’s guests in a
frock of ivory ninon, with lace attachments, and a
waist touch of apricot and mi»u,c. Lady Bosanquet
was in soft grey, with relieving touches of violet.
A daughter (the one that was noc in Melbourne) was
in apricot crepe de chine, with a wide hat. Chief
Justice Way’s dame wore blue, and her daughter,
Mrs. Herbert Rymill, was in white, with a be-
flowered hat and yellow sash. The Downer lady
looked smart in black and white; and the Mayor’s
youthful fiancSe, Miss Jean Warren, wore embroid-
ered muslin and a large hat with blue flowers. Also
there were the usual freaks.
On Friday the Zoo was inspected; for once in
a way it was the’ visitors who were fed. The cere-
mony is an annual affair. The animals were de-
clared to be doing as well in the matter of health and
spirits as was to be expected. By the way, the Zoo is
not only a wild-beast preserve, but also a species of
Botanical Gardens. Mr. Minchin grows there some
of the best roses in S.A. Mrs. Way, the Simpson
Newlands, E. T. Smith, Dr. and Mrs. T. G. Wilson,
the Alfred Simpsons, the Jessops, and the Misses
Hornabrook were among the animal scrutinisers.
Mrs. James Harvey and Mrs. Willie Milne and her
niece, Miss McLaren, leave for foreign parts on Nov.
7 ; and Miss Ethel Wright, of Victor Harbor, is off
early in February.
What are things coming to? At a recent show in
a big pastoral district of this State the sheep-dog
trials called forth but three competitors, and they
were all tragic incompetents. Old hands say, too,
that the horseflesh isn’t what it was. And other
pessimists aver gloomily that the drinks are growing
poorer and the women plainer week by week.
Mr. J. Cudmore went back to his Queensland
station at the beginning of the week after a brief
visit. This Cudmore is the eldest son of the late
J.F., of Paringa Hall. His mother’s failing health
called him to Adelaide.
Bertram Mackennal writes from London that the
plaster model of the King Edward memorial is
practically completed. Talking of statues, the
Sturt memorial movement is to be set going again.
An Adelaide girl has taken an office in King
AVilliam-street, and started in business as a shop-
ping agent. Her idea is to shop for country and
suburban folks who haven’t the time or inclination
to come to town and do it for themselves. I shall
be sympathetically interested to note what effect her
maddening profession has on her brain and physique.
The Harris-Scarfe burglar turns out to be a mere
lift boy. Hi's partner in crime was a duplicate key.
In two things, at least, the lad showed discrimina-
tion —namely, in the jewellery he seized and in his
choice of a companion in infamy.
Touching the imbecility of the break-of-gauge.
In a recent week 4000-odd tons of goods and about
17,000 head of stock; were transferred at Terowie,
and that is only one of the break-of-gauge stations
which we possess.
• Miss Claxton gave a tea at the May Club on
Saturday morning. The festivity was in honor of
Mrs. Ransom, the theosophical lecturer, who is pay-
ing Adelaide a visit.
The Adelaide City Council has started to fall with
suddenness and weight on the hatpin menace. There
is a widespread opinion that it is worse even than
Socialism, and henceforth hatpinners are to be
prosecuted.
Miss Alicia Bosanquet left for Melbourne on
Thursday. She is staying at Federal Government
House for the races. On the same day, I grieve to
record, the following male backsliders left for the
Great Orgie: Messrs. Neville Colley, Keith Bowman
Louis von Doussa, R. E. Hope and John Barker'
Miss Hilder Fisher and the Harold Fishers went
earlier in the week, as did Mr. T. R. Bright.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

Some time ago the Meterological Department
arranged a series of flag signals to indicate the wind
and weather forecasts. In Sydney these are flown at
the Custom House; but in order that people in an-
other part of the city may have the information
arrangements have been made with David Jones, Ltd.,
to also fly the signals. A card explaining the mean-
ing of the flags is displayed in one of the windows.*
The Union Co., of M.L., has issued an Index Guide
to its various services and the principal ports of
Australia, Tasmania, M.L., Pacific Islands, and mail
routes—to- Canada, United, States and Britain. It is
a good hand-book, and a good hand-book is one-third
the battle in touring. Another third is a good'bank
book. The last third is etceteras.*
Australian Ladies Blackmailed.
PARDONABLE INDISCRETION HEAVILY
AVENGED.
A woman who has once committed a certain very
natural and pardonable indiscretion is ever after-
wards at the mercy of her own act, unless she learns
; —as will be shown in a moment—how to free her-
self.
It is a very obvious figure of speech to say that
any lady who, afflicted by superfluous hairs on face
or arms, once uses a depilatory or cuts off the hairs
with scissors or a razor, can only be freed from the
effects in o-ne way. Unless it is treated again and
again with the cutting steel or the burning paste or
fluid, it will crop out again and again and humiliate
her by its degrading, masculine appearance. Just as
a human blackmailer must be paid again and again,
so' this silent blackmail of superfluous hair must be
dealt with again and again.
The only escape is by destroying the enemy at the
root: and this can only be accomplished in one way.
She must send for a self-acting electrolyser which
Mme. Tensfeldt, 120a Princes Street, Edinburgh,
Scotland, has invented specially for the benefit of
ladies who cannot go to her for treatment. This
apparatus, which only costs £5 5s., carriage paid,
will dissolve painlessly the root of every individual
hair. The hair cannot possibly reappear on the same
root: and this work is quite simple and painless.
When the apparatus has done its work it can be
sent back, and 25s., the second-hand value, will be
returned in exchange for it, p’-ovided this is done
within six months. Those desiring full particulars
of this treatment ought to send for a free copy of
the Booklet, “The Face Perfect,” by Madame Tens-
feldt. *

POLITICAL
POINTS
The Vic. Women’s National League, the
Very Naice Society which abused the mater-
nity allowance as a vote-buying scheme on
the part of the Labor Government, now in-
structs its dames to take the fiver every
time, and pay it into the political propa-
ganda fund of the W.N.L. So the League’s
criticism is to be justified. The Baby Bonus
is a vote-buying device. The League was
mistaken about the identity of the buyer,
that’s all.
THE NEXT STEP—PROVIDING THE
WAter:
The Minister for Home Affairs is inviting tenders
for the supply of oast-iron pipes for the Federal capi-
tal water supply. Tenders must be delivered, etc.
Another thumping argument in favor of a
State Fire Insurance department. Prender-
gast the other day elicited the information
that Victoria last year had insurances
effected on its public property to the amount
of £4,638,914, paid premiums totalling
£26,869, and collected only £405 for losses!
The Public Works, Railways and Mines and
Forests Departments were such safe and in-
combustible propositions that they made no
claims whatever.
Iceberg Irvine told a congregation of Mel-
bourne women the other day that Aus-
tralians were an anaemic lot, given to lean-
ing up against a post. And almost at the
moment the pallid Irvine was speaking his
piece a typical real Australian of the back-
backs was slowly hacking his arm off with
a penknife in order to release himself from
a bush trap in which he had been caught.
That’s the sort of anaemia the real Aus-
tralian suffers from !
Hatter David Storey, in the N.S.W.
Assembly the other evening
I am not a Conservative, but a true Democrat.
I believe in recognising the rights of all.
Then he announced that he would vote
against the Bill which proposes to extend
the municipal suffrage of Sydney to every
man and woman residing within the boun-
daries. The “rights of all,” in Storey’s
view, appear to be the rights of all who own
Proppu ty.
Things don’t take long to change in a
country given to wild bursts of loan-
drunkenness. Back in last May, when
winter was just setting in, and it
wasn’t much fun to be out of doors without
an overcoat, an immigrant caused a
sensation by calling in at a Sydney police
station and asking the constable to lock him
up. He said he was hungry, and was willing
to go to gaol to get food. At that time the
great drunk was still on, and people felt
indignant with the stranger for being hungry
and workless and wanting to get into gaol.
He represented the skeleton at the feast, and
there didn’t seem to be any excuse for a skele-
ton. If he had been a ghost haunting a per-
fectly new mansion, he couldn’t have excited
more scorn. It is only six months since that
stranger stalked like the advance agbnt of
Hunger through the daily papers* but already
the aspect of things has changed wonderfully.
The bottom has fallen out of the loan boom,
and a small army of men have been told not
to trouble the pay-office again. And the
hungry immigrant of last winter threatens
to be too frequent to provoke any astonish-
ment whatever.
The alleged backbone of Australia needs a
terrible lot of financial stiffening. Victoria,
not content with buying and cutting up land,
bringing'tons of water to it from long dis-
tances, and then trapesing round the earth
inducing farmers to come and cultivate irri-
gated blocks that have been acquired on ex-
ceptionally easy
terms, has now taken
to teaching the “back-
bones” the rudiments
of their business.
Three experts have
been appointed to go
round and tell the
newcomers what to do
and when and how to
do it. There remains
little more for the
State to do for the
self-reliant Anti-Sosh
soil - tickler beyond
getting in his crops
and banking the money for him.
What a farce is the Commonwealth Gov-
ernment’s refusal to carry letters addressed
to Tatt., or any “black-listed” agent thereof!
Tatt. issued 200,000 tickets at ss. 2d. for
the Caulfield Cup, and half a million at ss.
2d., besides 100,000 at 10s. 4d., for the
Melbourne Cup. The Postal Department’s
action has served only to create a new army
of agents for the firm which is operating
legally in Hobart, and which gives in com-
mission to those agents the money formerly
devoted to other brands of advertising. And
besides being futile, the law against Tatt.’s
is demoralising, since it floods the land with
law-breakers, Whom the community in gen-
eral regards as boons.
While the iron is hot from the Mount
Lyell furnace, the Tasmanian Government
ought to weld it into a Miners’ Accident
Fund. N. S. Wales has had such an insur-
ance and pension scheme going for more than
10 years, so there is plenty of experience to
go upon. For ninepence a fortnight contri-
buted by everybody working in or about a
mine, reinforced by an equal amount con-
tributed half each by the employer and the
State, the battered workman can get 15s. a
week while he lives and the widow can be
provided with 10s. a week for herself and 3s.
a week for each child, besides a few other
benefits. The figures are not dazzling enough
to make you blink, but they do away with
the humiliating cadge that follows every big
mining accident elsewhere. Also- —and this
is the most important thing of all —when
John Jones is killed in a plain, unfrilled and
unnoticed way by the fall of a casual stone,
his widow gets just the same help as the
relict of the man who loses his life in a
sensational smash which shakes up public
sympathy.

TRYING HIM WITH NEW BAIT.
The failure of the N.S.W. loan is ascribed to in-
sufficient advertising. It is proposed that one of the
Ministers should visit London, with the object of
personally interesting financiers; but none of the
prominent men can be well spared at the moment.
News item.
Something having mysteriously gone wrong
w ith the N.S.W. Government’s scheme to
borrow £3,000,000 in Paris, Agent-General
Coghlan was, a week or so ago, put on to the
job of extracting £1,500,000 from good old
J. Bull Cohen. But good old J. Bull Cohen
was so frigidly unresponsive that, in spite o.
all sorts of temptations, he applied tor much
less than half the total, and left the ex-
pensive underwriters to supply the balance
and unload it on to the market as the chance
offers.
The Federal Workers’ Compensation Bill,
pushed through the Representatives last
week, threatens to do a lot of good, especially
in Victoria. It provides that any employee
may recover for injuries if his disablement
lasts longer than a week and notice of in-
tention to claim is lodged inside six months.
In the event of his death his relations or
executors may claim. The rates of com-
pensation are distinctly moderate, not to ?"y
niggardly, which is, indeed, about the only
flaw in the measure. For the death of a
person wholly supporting others, a sum equal
to three years wages may be claimed, but
the amount must not go beyond £5OO, which
seems a poor price for an able-bodied wage-
earner. Total or partial disablement will
entitle the victim to half wages, but the sum
paid is not to exceed 30s. per week, and that
seems a poor price, too. Where a disnute
arises regarding a claim, the matter will go
to arbitration, the Commonwealth paying
the costs; and, failing a settlement that way,
the County Court will decide the issue.
The doddering Argus is enraged at Pre-
mier Watt for daring to ask that an Aus-
tralian should fill an Australian billet, the
billet in question being a Professorship, of
Economics at the Melbourne University.
She moans a column full of old lady’s logic,
and concludes her tirade by exhorting the
members of the arid University Council to
suffer no dictation, but to learn up their dead-
est Greek and hurl it at Watt, unless he gives
them public money to use according to their
own moth-eaten ideas. Presumably the
Argus reckons that its own economics are
of as much importance as the University’s;
yet when did the Argus, foreign-souled as
it is, last import an editor?
The Victorian House of Landlords jus-
tified itself in the eyes of its upholders, the
other day, by heaving out of Premier Watt’s
Closer Settlement Amendment Bill the clause
providing for the compulsory purchase of
land by the Governor-in-Council instead of
by resolution of both Houses. There must
have been, at different times, about a score
of attempts made by the Victorian Assembly
to build an effective compulsory-purchase
scheme. Each time the House of Fat has
put a rotten brick into the foundations, so
to speak, and the structure has fallen to
pieces. Justice and the public weal would
be served if the edifice fell heavily on Fat
one day and blotted him right out.
WRITTEN ON THE NEW LEAF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Minister for Defence (Senator _ Pearce) re-
ceived a cable message from the British Admiralty
to-dav, stating that the steam trials of the Aus-
tralian cruiser Melbourne had been started on Tues-
day. The warship reached Greenock on Wednesday.
The preliminary trials were satisfactory. —News item.
The flagship’s buoy no cable holds —Farm Cove is
empty quite ;
No more the winking Morse lamp sends its message
through the night;
And though you walk for hours along the winding
Sydney streets,
You’ll find few cap-bands blazoned with the names of
British fleets ;
It’s Parramatta, Warrego and Yarra all the way,
With now and then a Tingira, on shore-leave from
the Bay.
New names for old, and though the old in some ways
saw us through,
The times are changed and new ships come the
larger work to do.
By Garden Island, huddled close, three little hulls
crouch low
Upon the grey-blue sea to which their namesake
rivers flow ;
In honest daylight hard to find, by night what human
eye
Can hope to find these hounds of death when through
the seas they fly?
But cuddled there, inert, at peace, they look like
dogs that wait
Until their cruiser-mistresses approach the wide sea-
gate ;
Then squat Australian Death will race to meet the
proud sea-dames
That bear across Australia’s seas the rival cities’
names.
The time is close at hand; the weight upon our
shoulders slips.
But who will murmur or complain? These are real
fighting ships—
Not foolish scrap-heap hulks sent out a bargain to
complete,
But racing monarchs, staunch, well-armed and fit for
any fleet.
Who mourns for British cruisers old, or dancing tars
who flew
When warm November came around the Melbourne
Cup to view?
No! Young Australia, growing up, is done . with
follies old ;
No more for sea-pie messes will his heritage be sold.
N.S.W.
E. FRANCIS.

DYSPEPSIA.
WHAT A SYDNEY WOMAN SUFFERED
—AND HOW RELIEF CAME FROM
TAKING DR. MORSE’S INDIAN ROOT
PILLS.
There is no person more to be pitied than
the dyspeptic, who, if he eats at all, is in
dread of the consequences which he knows
from experience are sure to follow. When
a remedy is found to relieve the terrible
agony endured after every meal, a feeling
of gratitude only remains. Such an one is
Emily Barry, 98 Dowling Street, Surry Hills,
Sydney, who writes :
“I have in the past suffered the torture of
dyspepsia and indigestion, which lasted for
a period of eight months, during which time
I was under treatment in St. Vincent’s Hos-
pital, but that did -very little good. I used
'to gasp with pain, which I would get be-
tween the shoulder blades, like the stab of a
knife. I would plhce my hands behind my
back to ease these pains, and keep them
there until the sinews in them would become
contracted. The sharpness of these attacks
was so dreadful that I could scarcely breathe,
and hardly walk about. At last, seeing Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root Pills advertised, I
thought I would try them. I had not fin-
ished one bottl'e before I noticed an im-
provement, and continued their use until now
I seem entirely free from the trouble. I have
pleasure in recommending them to others,
and if you wish' you can use this for publica-
tion purposes.*

burn, in the State? of New South Wales. WHEREAS

Inkster) who died on the twenty-eighth day of July
One thousand nine hundred and ten gave and be-
queathed all his estate to his mother sisters and
brother to be divided equally amongst those still
living. AND WHEREAS Letters of Administration
with the said Will annexed were granted to
FREDERICK WALLIN by the Supreme Court of
New South Wales in its Probate Jurisdiction on the

eleven, NOTICE IS HEREBY GIVEN that all
creditors and other persons having claims against the
estate of the said Andrew Inkster whether under the
said Will or otherwise are required to send in their
claims to the undersigned on or before the thirty-
first day of March One thousand nine hundred and
thirteen in default whereof the said estate will be
distributed amongst the parties entitled thereto with-
out regard to any claims of which no notice has been
given.
WILLIAM ALFRED GTLDER,
Proctor, 92 Pitt Street,

SPORTING
NOTIONS
By next week the Horse Editor will have
10,000 reasons to explain exactly why Uncle Sam
did not win the Melbourne Cup, instead of running
only third. At present he is receiving consolation
from the Religious Editor, whose own fancy was
Piastre, the winner. Hallowmas, the mysterious
English horse, ran second.
* * * *
Horsey authorities declared last week that
Uncle Sam couldn’t win the Melbourne Cup on the
track work he had been getting. Certainly trainer
Whitty seemed to be letting the Caulfield hero off
very lightly in his preparation for the two miles
test, although the old inflexible axioms of the train-
ing class are no longer accepted as gospel. Alec
Taylor, of Ballarat, used to say that one couldn’t
reap where he had not sown. To get a great two-
mile gallop out of a horse, with the colors up, it
was necessary to put a lot of long, strong work into
him, the old-time trainers had been taught.
* * * *
Goes without saying that hundreds of
holiday punters at Flemington this week are amazed
to read the starting-prices recorded in the news-
papers next morning. A rank outsider for the Cup
figures at 66 to lor 100 to lin print. But the
artless punter who backed it with the first bookie
he appealed to was content to take 33’s or 50’s.
And even in the case of a favorite the difference be-
tween the price offered to the mug and the quota-
tion given by the newspapers is almost as wide, pro-
portionately, as in the case of a rank outsider, or of
one in the middle of the betting. A “20 to 1’
chance in the newspaper report was only “12 to 1 to
you, sir,” when the hot-headed punter backed it.
He might,, perchance, have got 20 to 1 somewhere
had he pushed his way from Ikey to Ikey, always
refusing Shylockian offers, until he struck one who
wanted the sovereign very badly indeed. For the
majority of backers at a Cup meeting the lotalisa-
tor would add fully 50 per cent, to the average
value of their sporting chance, and the trouble, annoy-
ance and headache that the Tote spared them would
be worth another pound or two.
Talkipg of Trafalgar’s long list of achieve-
ments, nobody has remarked that he was naturally a
more popular idol in Sydney than in Melbourne.
He won more races, and was not nearly so olten
unplaced, at Randwick than on the Melbourne courses.
As a three-year-old Trafalgar was seven times un-
placed at Caulfield and Flemington, whereas his only
visit to Sydney that season gave lnm three wins
(including 'the Cup) and one second. As a fiye-year-
old he won three times in Sydney, once only in Mel-
bourne, and once in Adelaide; and, whereas Comedy
King beat him three times on the Flemington course,
he beat Comedy King twice out of three times at
Randwick. # *
A “good old sport” in a deeply sentimental
mood is as unconsciously humorous as a fat man in a
fury. The retirement of Trafalgar from racing busi-
ness has been a grand opportunity for the g.o.s. to
express his finest feelings. One of him suggested
that Trafalgar, with jockey Smith on his back,
should walk around the course at Flemington before
the Cup is run, followed by the admiring roars of
an excited populace. Another old sport went one
better than that. He wrote: ‘ I would suggest that
Trafalgar be ridden by Australia’s premier trainer,
Walter S. Hickenbotham, of Carbine repute, and to
whom Trafalgar in the main owes his brilliant
career. There are hundreds like myself who remem-
ber ‘Walter’ as a jockey, and would be delighted to
asrain see him in the saddle, in the unique circum-
stances suggested. It would be a history-making scene
of delirious applause.” Only the imagination of a
Good Old Sport could picture with delight the great
circus act of grave and reverend Hickenbotham,
spectacles "on nose, disguised as a^ -jockey, whilst
Trafalgar bore him gently around the course, and
the band played “Auld Lang Syne. ,
The dampness of Derby Day at Fleming-
ton seemed to take all the curl out of the jubilation
of even those who backed more winners than losers.
For that matter, the weather was so bad for betting
business, especially when the Melbourne Stakes was
on hand, that hundreds of depressed punters pre-
ferred to remain under cover. Finally, they re-
turned to town haunted by a suspicion that two or
three of the results might prove to be misleading,
so far as future events go, on account of the dirty
conditions. It was a beast of a day.
The Derby market must have persuaded a good
many pirnters to take four to one about Ciuer or
Wolawa in preference to two to one or five to two
The short price against the Caulfield
Guineas’ winner was not justified on paper. Burra-
wang had looked like a good one when he cantered
home for the mile race; but as compared with the
champion two-year-olds of last season, one of whom
had recently captured the Sydney Derby, the favorite
was an unknown quantity. The race suggested that
D miles is too far for Burrawang in the best three-
year-old company. Otherwise it was unsatisfactory.
Cider, bolting away, with- the lead for want. of a
pacemaker, appeared to be fighting with his jockey
for the first half of his journey. Wolawa, followed
closelv by the favorite, caught Cider before they
turned into the straight, and eventually wore hint
down. Perdita, coming fast out of the ruck, finished
a good third for the slow run race. So, for the third
year in succession, the Randwick Derby form was re-
versed at Flemington. Wolawa had improved Very
considerably in .the meantime, ahd on appearances
he was the best of the bunch last Saturday. All the
same, if he had made the pace and Cider had waited
on him, the result might have been different.
Evidently the books thought they knew something
against the character of Duke Foote before the Mel-
bourne Stakes, or they would never have invited
backers with offers of nine to four and five to two.
Certainly the star’s restiveness in the saddling pad-
dock was discouraging to his idolators. He “jumped
about a bit,” like a horse not thoroughly wound up
or quite easy in his mind. Moreover, he was said
to be looking not so bright as he had looked at
Randwick. On the other hand, Lady Medallist was
reckoned certain to run a great race for the Stakes,
although she seemed a trifle spiritless. If the mare
had a chance to beat the Duke under ordinary cir-
cumstances, the driving rain must have spoilt it.
She never made a show. Duke Foote beat Eudorus
fairly, but not easily; and of the others, only
Piastre, Artesian and Eye-glass gave any promise of
plaving important parts in the Melbourne Cup. But
then, such as Flavian and Cadonia were not supposed
to be unduly exerting themselves.
At Flemington, on Derby Day, all the talk was
about the rejuvenation of old Matouree since his
recent return to Melbourne for the Cup meeting.
This time two years ago Matouree was a pretty good
horse for a long journey, and if he starts for the
V.R.C. Handicap on Saturday, his previous victory
in that 14-furlongs event will commend him to this
paper’s regard.
Beragoon won the Maribyrnong Plate, as Moe had
won the Maiden Plate, without being troubled. If
the course was alreadv beginning to soften, it
helped the well-grown colt to assert his superiority
over his less matured rivals, and he had so strong
a lead as he neared home that he was able to finish
two lengths ahead after losing a little ground by
swerving. When the Ilotham Handicap came on for
argument, the rain had palpably affected the going.
Maybe the Sydney horses Shuja, Hallowmas and
Sandbath found it foreign to their feet. Anyhow,
they were all behind at the finish when the big
S’Australian candidate, Lord Alwyne, came away
from Nangar, and won in what was very good time,
all things considered. Then the crowd perceived
that Lord Alwyne’s performances on the training
track, in company with sluggish Duke Foote, had
not been any real evidence of the latter’s suspected
staleness.
* * * *
Apparently the Upstairs Powers view the
alleged Sports of Kings with more favor than they
do England’s national pastime, for on Saturday they
spilt enough moisture on Melbourne to put the
stopper on all cricket, but not enough to rule out
the great Fleinington gamble. On Friday the
Westralians began their struggle against an unneces-
sarily strong Victorian eleven. It is 19 years since
the two States collided, and there was little in the
play to urge the Westralians to call in again for an-
other great space of time. Only A. Randell (61)
and ex-Tasmanian H. W. Edmondson (23) shaped at
all confidently.
*■ * * *
The winner of a little Trials race at
Gawler (S.A.) has been disqualified, having previ-
ously won over £5O in a stake. The former win
was only a second, but it was worth £6O, and as
the Trials conditions read “have never received a
stake, or part of a stake, worth £50,” the case was
clear. The totalisator, not being out to make money
on animals that are Dead, returned all investments
on the disqualified one; backers couldn’t possibly
win, so they shouldn’t lose. R. M. Ilawker, the
owner, wanted to know why someone didn’t give him
a hint before the race, instead of after; but, after
all, it was his own business to know what he was
doing, and he stands above any suggestion of trick-
ery. It has been suggested in England that no
objection of the kind should hold unless lodged
beforehand. For this reason: anyone discovering a
flaw in entry can now lay against the horse as a
certainty. If it doesn’t win, all is simple; if it
does, it loses just the same, on the protest. But
with the Tote you can’t lay against a horse, , and
much possible villainy is thus removed. You can
only back him.
* * * *
“0.K.”: There are days when it is good
to take your Scawtch pedigree to the races with you.
The rule applies also to Irish parentage, but not in
the same degree, the average Irish' punter being more
apt to bet on form, or information, than to back
Mavourneen or Erin’s Isle for the sake of the name.
Still, it is not always good to remember that you
are a Scot. When the late scenic-artist Brunton
happened to go to Flemington, he was invariably on
the rank outsider that had anything suggestive of
Scotland in its cognomen. I was reminded of Brun-
ton’s method of picking winners when Royal Scotch
won a w.f.a. race at Caulfield recently—-price 20 to 1.
Then I met Erskine Scot, manager at West’s Pic-
tures in Melbourne, who is understood to be dimly
related to Bonnie Prince Charlie on his aunt’s side.
He had backed Royal Scotch, of course. Unfortu-
nately his royal blood had not boiled in his veins,
and he had merely put half a sov. on the animal.
“Boswell”: Cricket enthusiasts on the
Northern Rivers (N.S.W.) appear to follow the
game right through from first to second childhood.
Recently, at Murwillumbah, two juniors, Dinny Harti-
gan (80), and E. W. Blakeney (78), played a single-
wicket match for the benefit of the hospital, and
the former boy patted the ball to the boundary fence
several times. No sooner was this game finished than
the ground was occupied by a local (earn and one
from Coraki way. The skipper of the visitors was
T. Yabsley (70), who was a district champion 40
years ago. In a waning light he batted for half an
hour, and in the field his return was sharp and
sure, in contrast to the white whiskers which waved
round the upper part of his enthusiasm. No cen-
tenarian has yet been caught playing the game, but
Judging by ■ the way in which matches inclined
to drag out, a time may come when only centen-
arians will have time enough to see the thing
through.
* * * *
If North Sydney cricketer K: Walton is
not leading the simple life and getting home at 6
o’clock sharp every evening, he had, better reform.
Playing for his district agqinst the'Police’last week,
he gathered in 10 wickets ,for ,28 runs, and'left the
Police team a wreck. OnJ,v„ the best ind ----h of
us who have a gift of speed can afford to do things
like that.
The close of Sydney’s third round of
First-Grade cricket took place in a dull, sweltering
day, fit only for drinking. It wag a batsman s day,
with perfect wickets and well-diffused light, and it
is a surprising circumstance that only two centuries
were put up in such conditions. The mighty
ones were L. A. Cody, of Paddington, 127 ; and H.
Goddard, of Redfern, 105, n.o. Close-ups were C. J.
Tozer, of. University, 90 n. 0.; S. Moore, of North
Sydney, 89; and H. A. Maclean, of Gordon, 83.
Mailey, of Redfern, headed the bowling list with
five for 82. i F.' S. Middleton, of North Sydney, took
four Burwood wickets for 17 runs; his club-mate,
Long, took another four for -62 Single, of the
University, snatched four Glebe wickets for 23 ; and
Folkard, of Balmain, smashed up four of Peter-
sham’s for 39. As for the matches, University,
having declared with 320 ,for the loss of five
wickets, overwhelmed Glebe with 141, thus winning
by 179 on the first innings ; a ghastly doing with a
Bulgarian flavor about it. Paddington, in spite of
its serviceable 296, only made a drawn game with
Central Cumberland (otherwise Parramatta), which
had lost sijX wickets for 180 when time ran out.
An unsatisfactory tussle. Waverley had a comfort-
able win over Sydney by 77 on the first innings—
-244 and 167 respectively. Burwood, having amassed
240, utterly failed to shift North Sydney, which
bashed up 306 for four wickets; therefore it went
under by six wickets ail'd 66 runs on the first innings.
Balmain, having notched 218 for five wickets, de-
clared, and then routed Petersham for 115. But in
the follow-on, it had got only five wickets for 80
when time gave up the ghost; and so it had to be
content with a one innings’ win of 103 runs. Gor-
don made 222 at the first attempt, and Redfern
replied with 301 for six wickets, and thereby won
by four wickets and 79 runs on the first innings.
The result of the upheaval is that Redfern and
North Sydney are level at the head of the competi-
tion with six points each, and all the rest are away
to Satan behind, four of them with only two points
each, two with no points, four with two less than
nothing, and one six below zero.
* * * *
Another glorious tradition falls with the
sickening thud of a decayed pumpkin leaping from
the tree. M.L. Rugby is feeling sore. In ’O5 a team
blew over to the cold country and mangled the repu-
tation of that far land. A S’African team followed
and mopped up the remains. As the main smash
was already finished, it mopped them with a tender
hoof. M.L. Union expected an invitation to return
again to the slaughter, but the card didn’t come to
hand, and now the information has arrived that
S’Africa has got the invitation out of her turn, and
M.L. isn’t wanted. The reasons appear to be:
Maorilanders are (1) addicted to drink, (2) to need-
less asceticism and over-sobriety, (3) to making
themselves fit, (4) to trying to win, and (5) to de-
vouring their food with a knife. All of which goes
to show that the Ancient Briton can be a rotten-bad
loser.
# * * #
The gentle Standard Oil Company is at
it again. The price of petrol has taken another
jump, and it has been jumping so vigorously of
late that a lot of the bloom is off the rose of
motoring. There’s a fortune waiting for the man
who can meet this constant rise in price by im-
proving the efficiency of the engine. At present the
internal combustion engine turns only 20 per cent,
of the fuel into actual power for driving purposes—
the rest gets away, chiefly in the form of heat,
through the water-jackets and exhaust-pipe. A man
would make half a dozen fortunes if he could devise
a means to obtain even 40 per cent, of the thermal
units of the fuel. At present a motorist spends Is.
6d. on a gallon of petrol, and gets, roughly, 3id.
worth turned into power; the other Is. 2-Jd. is
sheer waste. The only apparent remedy is to get
the makers to instal carburetters to volatilise alcohol,
and agitate for the abolition of the duty on spirit
used for power purposes. But for the excise alcohol
should be about half the price of petrol, if manu-
factured in sufficiently large quantities under eco-
nominal conditions, and with proper carburetters it
should be just as effective for ordinary purposes.
Anyhow, the automobile clubs ought to go into the
matter carefully, and collect data to put before the
Australian Government. Australia is a land in which
both wood and water are scarce, and alcohol for
power purposes is a valuable consideration, as it
makes users of power independent of both those
scarce commodities.
* * * *
Tasmanian cyclist Brenda made a mighty
fine effort against Frank Kramer, the 11 times cham-
pion of America, for the mile championship of the
world, at Newark track, New Jersey (U.S.A.). The
first news received in Australia was that Grenda had
won the event, but that misconception was due to
the fact that in the first attempt to run the final
Kramer punctured, and the race was re-run. There
were only three in the final—Kramer, Grenda and
Perchicot, the Frenchman. Each tried to induce the
others to make the pace, and at last Kramer was
forced to the front. He went off with a terrjfic
bound. a^' l reeled away in front for four laps. Then
Grenda flew past him, and strove to get a clean
breaic-away. But the track was too heavy for that
sort of caper, and both the Frenchman and the
Yankee got his pace. Kramer made the supreme
effort of his racing life when he passed Grenda ; and
the Frenchman seems to have literally hurled himself
and his machine across the line. But the judges
gave the Australian second place. Kramer’s win
was by inches only. So evidently the long, powerful
Tasmanian has a future before him.
N.S.W. cyclists had a field day at Bris-
bane in the amateur championships meeting, S. R.
Burtenshaw winning both championships—the one
mile from E. C. Hooper (N.S.W.) and J. Bell
(Q.), and the 10 miles from Cutliffe and Beck (both
of;N.S.W.). Why the Queenslander doesn’t grow as
good legs as his N.S.W. brother is a mystery ; but on
the evidence of the bike, he doesn’t.

Dick Arnst has either a splendid opinion
of ! himself, or a mighty poor one of sundry other
Australian scullers. Felton, Paddon and Towns are
all very anxious to row Harry Pearce before he
departs Bull-wards to butt Barry. Richard cheer-
fully proposes to give them a flutter for a sweep-
stakes of £lOO each, the winner to take the Jo.t. ’
He; generously offers each man lOsec. shirt, and
draws no line —not even against Harry Pearce him-
self. It must be glorious to feel like that!
i * * * '•• *
Railing on Sydney Harbor on; Saturday
was a :joke or a farce as far as the 18ft. craft were
concerned. Almost immediately they were let go
the wind gave a few dying kicks and faded right
out, and the boats just drifted aimlessly, spine pro-
pelled momentarily by a faint whiff from this way
or j that. The open boats have to be over the winning
line by 6.30 p.m. or forfeit all prizes. Out of 19
starters only two got home in time —Australian
(scratch), handled by veteran Webb; and Kismet
(also scratch), in that order. The rest were left. It
was about the most fearful and wonderful sailing
race ever seen on the harbor. Nearly every-boat had
a lead on the fleet at one time or other, and then
was in turn cut down by some tail-ender who hap-
pened to catch a straggling puff. But for the
moderate southerly which came along when it was
too late to be of service to all but those right in
front, some of the boats wouldn’t have got home till
some time on Sunday.
* * * *
111-luck still dogs the French boxers in
Australia, and on Saturday night Jean Audouy vyas
disqualified in the eighth round of his fight with
Yankee Pat Bradley. It was a battle between a
boxer and a bruiser; by which it is intended to
delicately hint that Bradley knows no more of
boxing than a Berkshire hog knows of Plantagenet
descent. He has a neat knack of covering up his
point; that done, he bores in to get home a knock-
out wallop. If he gets the wallop home he wins.
If he doesn’t, he loses on points. Of course, he can
only play that game with a man who is not a hard
hitter. Had Audouy carried a knock-out about him,
the gentle Patrick would have been killed stone-
dead about four times in each round. The elusive,
clean-hitting Frenchman simply rained blows on the
head afid face of the American. But the Yankee,
staggering under the shower of blows, never relented
or hesitated —just went on boring in, and when he
thought' the time was ripe, smashed. Three times
he caught the Gaul with smashes that sent him to
the boards; but the Gaul came back, and on one
occasion put up a splendid exhibition of punching,
beating the Yankee to a standstill. But although
he could hit all the time, and how, when and where
he liked, the Frenchman could not knock out the
American. These fellows who survive Chicago pork
seem to be impervious to anything but an axe or a
dynamite cartridge. There's only one way for a
boxer of the Audouy type to beat a Chicago-fed iron-
head, and that is to devote all attention to one spot
—an eye for preference, and land three out of every
four blows on that eye till it is closed up, and then
get loose on the other. Constant tapping will break
even granite.
To come back to the fight, the end came in the
beginning of the eighth round. Bradley rushed in
and smote at Audouy. Whether he hit him or not
writer cannot say. He certainly seemed to. Audouy
dropped on one knee and was promptly disqualified
by referee Scott for having gone down without being
hit; and Scott was in the right position to see, and
had a better view than anyone in the Stadium. It
was a fairly exciting scrap while it lasted ; but the
chances are, that sooner or later, Bradley would
have got home with one of those haymakers. As
long as the Frenchman could come up he was,
nevertheless, a winner on points.
There is one thing about the Stadium that is
unutterably dreary, and that is the awful habit of
“announcing coming events.” A debonair gentleman,
with a smiling countenance, climbs laboriously into
the ring, advances to one side and begins, “Gentle-
men,” etc., etc. Then he goes to the next side of the
ring and yells it all over again, and then repeats it
yet again at the two other sides ; and by the time
he has six coming events four times each,
it becomes wearisome. The Stadium is well managed
with that one exception. There is no other place
of public amusement in Sydney where the visitor
gets such courteous treatment and polite attention.
Even when the white-coated attendants pass out a
gentleman for being over-demonstrative, they do it
with a mingled courtesy and firmness that is abso-
lutely ideal.
* * * *
For the last week or so the cable has been
quite silent as to what has happened to Johnson;
so, presumably, the hysterical Chicago crowd did not
fl.v off the handle and lynch him. The case at the
latest advice has been postponed while the police
collect more evidence; and it is a' charming com-
mentary qn American justice that Johnson’s cafe has
been closed by having its license cancelled before
Johnson has been found guilty by the courts. Mean-
while, fight followers are discussing how to discover
his successor, for by common consent Johnson’s
title has been torn from him. He is still champion
of the world, but no one will acknowledge the fact,
and appearances indicate that a sort of Armageddon
of bruisers will be organised to discover the new
champion. The cruellest part of the business is that
decent colored fighters like Langford, Jeanette and
McVea are included in the boycott of Johnson the
Dreadful. The world's championship is to be re-
served entirely for the white race, although, of
course, the respectable fighters already mentioned
will be able to get just as many matches as before.
* * * *
Australian light-weight Hughie Mehegan
has gained great fame in England, but little money.
He made too great a splash at his battle with
Matthew Wells; and demonstrated himself such a
liberal dispenser of sudden stoush that one after
another all the best English light-weights have
backed out of any engagements they may have, made,
or decisively declined to make any engagements.
Mehegan will have to make the trip to the United
States without “cleaning up” the English board be-
fore he goes. However, in the States he will not go
short of chopping-blocks, for the Yankee bruiser
never allows himself to be over-awed by a foreign-
made reputation or anything else. Which is one of
the few creditable things about the Yankees, either
as fighters or “sports.”

The recollections of “Strictly Anonymous” :
“It doesn’t follow that drunkenness’> means .being
the worse for liquor” (B. 17/10/’ 12). That profound
remark has set me remembering. In the course of a
mis-spent youth I knew four men—all friends, or, at
any rate, members of the same club crowd —on whom
liquor produced the most absurdly diverse effects.
No. 1 was a brilliant intellect. He could, on oc-
casion, drink till the cows came .home; but always
after the second glass-his ordinary polished manner
departed, and lie became suspicious, fretful, and in-
fernally insulting to all-comers. No. 2 was just or-
dinary in everyday life; with three drinks inside
him he became genial, unexpectedly witty, and the
delight of any company he might be in. No. 3 was
a “sport,” of the type which ebbs to and fro between
the billiard-table and the racecourse, loves a good
dinner, and never walks the length of a block if a
cab can be got. He ought to have been a seasoned
vessel. Yet a single drink would unbalance him, and
h& generally stuck to, soft things. When he did have
the one it always aroused a demand for mtirp ;j and,
cards with, him, I have been under the. pain-
ful necessity of reaching behind him and. drinking
up those he ordered before lie could get them, to
save his becoming a public nuisance. No. i was for-
ever “having one,” and never showed any effect at
all. Come to think of it, I suppose I never' saw him
not under the influence of liquor, but certainly lie
never seemed otherwise than sober. He simply had
to have a whisky every little while, as a; whale has
to come up to breathe. Only once did I hear him
refer to the subject. We were playing billiards one
evening, and he fancied he was off his game. “Had
too many whiskeys,” he said to my amazement.
“Only two,” T objected. “Oh, yes; but I must have
had 30 or 35 during the day.” He could feel them,
apparently, but he never showed them. The same
quantity of alcohol divided between the other three
men would have seen No. 1 locked up, No. 2 physi-
cally sick, and No. 3 dead to the world. Once again,
what is “normal” ? And what is “perfectly sober” ?

“Hassan” makes a proposition :
What about developing the Northern Territory by
means of prison labor ? The time for the small man in
the North is not yet, and won’t be yet for quite a
long time. If you offered a white settler a 10-acre
block of very best land on the easiest terms in-
vented by the most vote-hungry Fusionist, or on the
most beautiful leasehold terms which could be
dreamed by Hen George, he would turn up his nose
at it, and stay away down near our beautiful harbor
or our lovely Yarra. And, really, what is there to
induce him to go? He’d be as far away from the
ordinary decencies (and indecencies) of civilisation
as though he were dead. Whether he would regret
the decencies or the indecencies most does not mat-
ter, nor does it matter whether he would be men-
tally, morally and physically improved by living the
strenuous pioneering life. Very few men prefer to
eat the wholesome things, and when one wears the
wholesome clothes he is put in a lunatic asylum, a
fate which recently befel a literary gentleman who
sunned himself about Melbourne and Sydney. We
may take it then that the settler won’t settle in the
Northern Territory either for the good of his health
or the future of Australia.
When Australia was discovered nobody came out
here because he wanted to. Our hardy pioneers
mostly came chained together. Had it rested upon
choice we would not have had as many people here
as we have now, and that isn’t enough. We would,
in fact, have had less than not enough. It may be
that the race is getting luxurious, but pioneering is
not a job which appeals to very many men, and it is
very much less popular with the women. Just as
Australia was originally populated and developed by
persons who had no choice in the matter, and who
came Out her to make roads and build Government
buildings and make themselves generally useful, so
it may be necessary to turn our gaol-birds into the
Northern Territory. We have any amount of them,
and we ought to have three times as many, only our
police are insufficient and ill-paid and incompetent.
They could make the roads and do the digging and
the ploughing and the hewing of wood and the draw-
ing of water. Then, when they had made the place
comfortable they might be given small blocks on
nominal leasehold terms for the rest of their lives.
These unfortunate gentlemen are in the way down
here. Nobody loves them. The unions will not let
them Work because they compete with the unions.
There wouldn’t be any unions to compete with up in
the neighborhood of the Daly River or Alice Springs.
There is only one difficulty in the way. If the Com-
monwealth proposed to take the long-sentence pris-
oners and make them useful, the States would cling
to their burglars and bunco-men and garrotters and
other rotters as they cling to their debts and their
muddling, and their inept and foolish little Parlia-
ments.
“Hamer”: More haste, less whisky, or
something, as the old adage has it. The
roystering suburbanite who leaves Sydney
by the very latest train has always a chance
of being home before the one who tries to
get early off the*mark. Which thing is a
great mystery until the ways of the mid-
night train are marked and known. The
officials connected with the departure and
running of that altruistic convenience are
the patron saints of the late drunk. They
search for him throughout the length and
breadth of Sydney station, and they see that
he is safely delivered at his correct destina-
tion. Their careful system may be some
slight disadvantage to the man who lives at
Parramatta and who objects to being
awakened at each station of call to answer
anxious inquiries as to the situation of his
domestic rooftree, but to the overburdened
wight of the intermediate suburbs it is a
boon and a blessing. Recently had an ex-
perience as an after-effect of attendance at
a revival meeting where all our spirits were
much moved. I placed myself in charge o
a ioyous neighbor, and when we reached the
Central Station I found that we had a
chance to catch a train which ran through
to our select suburb. But my triend a
suburbanite of vast experience -expressec
his inflexible determination to wait tor the
midnight train. I pointed out that, while l
felt I had a moral obligation to look alter
him, I wanted to get home as early as rea-
sonably possible, and that the train I pio
posed to take would land us home an hour
and a half ahead of the twelve o clock am-
bulance which has neither use nor regard toi
time. He was obdurate and insulting. He
said he didn’t think a man in my state ot
sobriety need worry much about such obli-
gations, and he himself was prepared to lay
any responsibility for his safe delivery on
the shoulders of Tom Johnson. If he took
the 12 o’clock Tom would see him safely
home. I left him nestling down to slumber
on one of the station seats, and took the
through train. When I woke up in the
sheds at Homebush I realised that they, do
not trouble about the sleeping or the weary'
on any but the midnight caravan. My own ,
suburban platform, where I should have got;
off, was four stations back, and it was a
cold walk home. My friend not only got
home before me but he acquired on the train
a packet of fish and a large heap of beer
that had been abandoned by some roysterer
more roysterous than himself. Thus I
learned my great lesson, and now when tired
out with a long night’s work I never dream
of looking for any other vehicle to take me
home except the ohe blessed contraption
which links night and morning. The only
time I have known that last train to fail
was in the case of a friend who tried to get
to Manly by it and couldn’t on account of
the fog. He got to Parramatta, anyhow,
and that was somewhere. It is indeed a
cold drunk who blows nowhere.
“Maori Mac” inspects the Maoriland Min-
istry, but awards no diplomas : —-
Apparently Bill Massey will reign long as Maori-
land’s Premier. Not that Bill has any particular
effulgence hidden about his person. Nor have his
more or less disguised Tory colleagues shown anv
reason why sentence of death should not be passed.
William is like Dick Arnst before he ceased rowing
at the brewery. There is nobody in sight that can
lick him. The Opposition is a disorganised and
leaderless rabble. Moreover, it is fast disintegrating.
How could a beaten party, which contained big
brewer Myers, bigger Wowser Isitt, and biggest
rangatira Jimmy Carroll have cohesion? The Oppo-
sition’s worst charge against Massey is that he has
pinched Ward’s policy. It is a serious indictment.
Ward’s policy was a void. If a discerning public
had been able to recognise any sign of a policy in
Ward’s vicinity Joseph would still be boss borrower.
Already Massey has acquired his predecessor’s shuffle.
He talks vaguely of the next session, or the jhext
century, or the next car for home. And the back-
bone which frantically acclaimed the , overdue mil-
lennium when Bill staggered home by a short nose is
beginning to look slant-eyed at its hero. Attorney-
General Herdman has persevered with his Civil Ser-
vice Bill. Its chief features are the elimination of
political patronage in the Service, and the appoint-
ment of a “big chief ” Commissioner. The poli-
tician who hankers after a job for his'great aunt
will incur a penalty, and the female, relative will be
disqualified or warned off the course. The united
howls of the Civil Servants and of those who want
to be Civil Servants are only being drowned by the
bellowing of the M’s.P. who desire that all their con-
stituents shall be Civil Servants. The only other
Minister to display any convictions, beyond the farm
belief that he is the-best man for his -lob.ts Hemes.
Thirty years ago Herries was an Englishman. He
has not*forgotten the fact. Next Railway
Manager Ronayne retires. Hemes will import his
successor. When some misguided cove P ol "ted out
that a good railway boss in Cold Hmgland might be
a damned poor boss in Maoriland, berries became
speechless with indignation. then a bloq
knowledge and horse-sense fixed. Hemes with hi i eye,
and announced that in the Motheaten Land the bg
railway is run by a number of specialists, and, the e-
fore, Herries would have to import a troupe, galaxy,
circus or menagerie. Herries was not seeking in-
formation, and the local applicant, however com-
petent he may be, will be turned down Of the re-
mainder of Massey’s team, Fraser and Allen are
aged Tories, Bell and Rhodes are less aged, but more
Tory, and Poiuare is a dark gentleman boasting a
Yankee ’Varsity degree. I have left Fisher till last.
He has brains but no balance ; can play a good game
of tennis and heave the shot quite a distance. He
is not a Torv, but herds with them because- —-
Well, why the Sheol does he herd with them l i
don’t know; neither do I care.
“The Ejected” writes: —
When the Hungarian Opposition gets busy, it can
accomplish more in three days than our N.S.W. Op-
position can in three years. At the end of last
session one of its members shot at the 1 resident.
Count Tisza, and the whole Opposition was turned
out. Next session the members who disapproved of
the Ministry brought motor-horns, fog signals and
trumpets with them, and when the President • put
in an appearance they opened a concert, and re-
minded him, between tunes, that he was a :paid
rascal, and various other uncomplimentary things.
They called him a hog- and six bigamists tor two
and a half hours, during which time there was
a general mix-up on the floor of the House, and
several members were bitten by several other mem-
bers. Then the sitting was suspended. When busi-
ness was resumed, the police were called in to keep
an eye on proceedings; but when the Opposition
began to warm up the bobbies proved useless. Meet-
ing again adjourned. Next day, before the Presi-
dent took his seat, someone called the Minister for
Commerce a scoundrel. (In this respect our N.S.W.
Opposition, I believe, Can hold its own.) The Min-
ister resented it, and strode across the Chamber to
biff the offender, but he was knocked down and
wkllted on. Then the President put in an appear-
ance, and the motor-horns, etc., commenced. He
called in the police, but the Opposition left en masse.
And yet people accuse our Legislature of being'noisy
arid riotous. In Hungary they would have eaten
Willis.

No More Pains in the Feet. The Comfort Experienced
in Wearing Asbestos Socks is Absolutely Marvellous.
Hot, Perspiring, Inflamed and Tender Feet. Corns,
Bunions and Bad Feet Prevented and Absolutely Cured
by wearing Patent Asbestos Socks, (soles).
An Army Surgeon discovered that soldiers after marching suffered more from
[soreness of the feet than from actual physical fatigue. After long study he
(found that this was due to the irritation set up by the smooth leather insoles of
| their boots. The Asbestos Socks, made from impregnated asbestos with a
slightly rough grained surface prevents the foot slipping inside, and waiting is
thus less tiring. Asbestos being a non-conductor the feet are kept
from the variations caused bv hot or cold weather, a genuine joy
and blessing to the tenderfooted. In fact the comfort experienced
in wearing these socks Is absolutely marvellous. Try a
pair at once. Satisfaction Guaranteed or money refunded.
They will renew the feet of anyone who has to stand long or do much
walking, such as shop assistants, policemen, nurses, sailors,
soldiers, postmen, cyclists, athletes etc. Made in two qualities, 1/- and
2/- per pair, postage 2d pair extra. Six pairs either quality sent for price of
five pairs State size and name this paper.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped. ?<ame
and address must appear on MSS. No liability is
undertaken re contributions voluntarily submitted,
whether sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will
be returned unless stamps accompany.
J.F.H.: One par usable, but the verses are
out of our line N.C.: “The place where the Gov-
ernor’s cow died’’ is not interesting. Now, if the
Governor's whale had climbed a tree and died, that
would have been something.... George L.: What the
mischief any of it means we know not. Even our
stipendiary necromancer and anagram-solver has had
to own himself licked. However, herewith one verse
in token of good faith and kindly feeling: —
Nightfall. The welcome night blots out the scar,
Earth's leprous knuckle spot; the grim forboder ;
Dust and ashes of the ages ; a world’s Golgotha.
( Heavenl send no moon to-night.)
. ...M. Cairns: “The best book on Australian Poets"
is not yet written. Nor is any other best book. .. .
Henry li.: One verse:—
Seated at a Faro table
Was a villain strong and able.
Who had plied his trade at Cairo,
Masquerading as a hero ;
He was playing with Lord Marlow,
At the bank at Monte Carlo,
Where the Lord sought to forget
All his care with Margarette.
A creditable bit of potted melodrama, Hepry ; but
that is all we can say in its favor.... Manin: Dis-
tinct streaks of humor in “The Awakening,” but its
flimsiness slays it. .. .Carnsdale: Joe Cook was re-
turned to the N.S.W. Parliament as a Labor member
in IS9I. He represented Hartley in the Assembly
from 1891 to 1901. About two years after his elec-
tion he was chosen leader of the Freetrade section of
the party, but declined to sign the solidarity or
caucus pledge, and cut loose. He is still loose. He
has never been tight, being a strict teetotaler
Muldoon: Done better too often before... .Brother
Engineer: Verse no good, and sent in months too late
to have any topical interest... .H.C.J.: Verse not Up.
. ...A.8.M.: Echoes only, and not skilful ones at
that....Hee K.: Poem misses by the width of two
strong cooees and a kangaroo hunt... .Elbe: We will
pass your splendid joke suggestion on to the public
exactly as received. It sometimes does the public good
to be handed these things—teaches them humility and
sympathy for editors: "What Darwin Didn’t Know”
—a nice (sometimes) little girl student, who, among
things, was studying chemistry, was lunching
tete-a-tete with Uncle, who also knew something of
the subject, likewise a trifle of anatomy. The
weather was of that description conducive to the un-
invited presence of our old friend, the Buzz-fly,
whose music considerably annoyed Girlie, causing
remarks of different varieties. Uncle recommended
an application of sodium chloride a la caudal ap-
pendage (of the Buzz-fly). Girlie forgot her chem-
istry for a moment and questioned : “What is sodium
chloride?” “Don’t you know what sodium chloride
is?” remarked Uncle. “Oh, yes, of course,'”' point-
ing to the salt-cellar. “That’s it. You know, what
caudal appendage is, don’t you?” Girlie: “Potassium
chloride!!!” . ...B.J. (Vic.): Sincere verse, but
too wild and rocky... .J.J.D.: Hold on to your
job; large quantities of such verse would make no
money, but many enemies. .. .M.D. (Q.) : No in-
terest .... Bellerive: A marine poem :
MUTINY ON THE HIGH SEAS.
While a vessel cleaved her way
O’er the wild ocean Blue
Mutiny prevailed among the
Fierce and desperate crew
Prone fell the brave captain
With a bullet wound in the neck
The officers were overpowered
And brutally slain on the Deck.
Pour out the grog cried the Leader
Comrades we are free
Dump over the dead to the
Reptiles of the sea
Flames burst from the hold
During the adventurous trip
Maddened by grog the mutineers
Set fire to the ship.

Ex.: Starkly disagree with your view that
the time is ripe for an export duty on youthful
singers and players who graduate from Eisteddfodan
and amateur competitions, and then yearn for Europe.
They come back to Australia in due course, rather
broke, with far less of the beri-beri effect about
the head, and with a truer appreciation of the merits
of their native land. Later, they usually abandon
Art and take to work, which is all to the good. If
any legal process is called for, we are inclined to
favor a Prohibition Order, or something, against
Eisteddfoding.. .Boheme: The Bulletin has written
probably 100 or 200 leading articles, sub-leaders and
Plain English items in favor of Irish Home Rule
during its lifetime. If you haven’t read them it
isn’t our responsibility... ,Effebb: The best we can
say for your work is that the gags are somewhat less
rotten than the drawings. However, mortification
has set in briskly as regards even the gags.... Biff:
