wer Lytton’s “Money!’ in Adelaide, in 1869. But is
it a kindly act to throw up a man’s 40-odd-year-old
m ; stakes at him in-this way ?.... Cybele: Yon” re-,
marks shall be brought under the notice of “O.i . ’
and if he wilts utterly the loss of a valued c .-
tributor will be on your fair shoulders. They (the
remarks, not the shoulders) are a shade too intimate
for publication in a mild old home journal like
ours Gerald B.: Lines beginning—

Pierre: No, “picture” doesn’t rhyme with “creature.”
It doesn’t even rhyme with “quadruped.”... .Queens-
lander: Oh, yes; plenty of European States have
sinking funds for the extinction of national debt. In
Belgium “there is a sinking fund for all description-
of the debt,” except a few trifles incurred in the old,
dark days—-the days of unenlightenment, which Aus-
tralia hasn't got out of yet. Austria and France
have amortisation funds. Norway pays approximately
1J per cent, into a sinking fund. Switzerland pays
rather more into its sinking fund than it pays in
interest. In Sweden “all the Joans are paid off
gradually by means of sinking funds.” (“States-
man’s Year Book.”) In Holland the sinking fund
amounts to about 4 per cent. And there are others.
Australia, in fact, is about the only country that
makes no regular effort to get out of pawn.... A-
ntonia: That story was nlregav old when Bearuppides

M.D., etc.,
writes: “Taking an interest in all
subjects relating to food, I have
procured samples of Sanatogen
and subjected them to experi-
ment. . . .
“ In my opinion, it is the or-
ganic phosphorus which makes
Sanatogen of such value. . . .
A series of experiments proved
that Sanatogen is easily and com-
pletely digested. I have arrived
at the conclusion that Sanatogen
is a substance of the highest
nutritive value, containing as it
does a large amount of organic
phosphorus—that is, phosphorus
which is offered to the tissues in
exactly the form in which it can
be easily absorbed. It is an ex-
cellent nerve food.”
“The Medical Times”
says: ‘ ‘ There is no doubt what-
ever that the nutrition of patients
taking Sanatogen improves won-
derfully, due, in all probability,
to its being easy of assimilation
and to the organic absorbable
phosphorus which it contains.”
A thousand times this number of Physicians endorse
SANATOGEN
The Royal Tonic Food.
Over fourteen thousand practising physicians have recorded in writing their high
opinion of Sanatogen, and many thousands more are daily prescribing it with the
most beneficial results in cases of debility, brain fag, lack of vitality, sleepless-
ness, disordered digestion, poverty of blood and various wasting diseases.
Can you ask for more convincing testimony of Sanatogen’s value than the
fact that it is thus publicly endorsed and recommended by practically the whole
medical profession ?
Among the original letters in praise of Sanatogen which are filed for public
inspection, there are many from world-famous physicians whose names stand
for the highest scientific achievements. Their advice, and the example of
distinguished men like those mentioned here, it is safe for you to follow.
Irresistible as this evidence is, the reader can only realise the wonderful
revivifying effects of Sanatogen by taking it himself when his nervous system
and bodily strength have been undermined by overwork, worry or disease.

A
Phone-y
Dialogue
She : “ That you, Phil ? ”
HeYes, dear; what’s wrongP”
She: “ Well, you’re a very naughty boy. I asked
you to bring me a bottle of Odol t and you’ve brought me
some other dentifrice that 1 can’t possibly use. What
did you ask for, stupid ?”
He: “Oh, 1 was in a hurry and I forgot the name,
and the man handed me something that I presumed
was all right.”
She: “Oh, Phil, you should do as you’re told.
Here have 1 been using Odol for months and months,
■and feeling and looking very much better for it, as you
know. Why, you said the other day it was a treat to
see me smile nowadays, my teeth looked so lovely—and
then you go and forget 1 ”
He: “I’ll bring you half-a-dozen bottles to-night.
1 admit it was an awful blunder on my part. But wait
a minute, dear. Hold the line. (Resumes after a pause.)
Of course, Odol— o—d—o 1. Odol—the scientific
dentifrice. The dentifrice that the society beauties are
all using, and which the actresses crack up so.”
She: “What do you know about actresses?”
He: “Only as much as they show me from behind
the footlights when you go with me to the theatre,
darling. Of course, I know Odol. Don’t 1 share your
bottle P 1 find it makes the mouth so fresh, and
keeps the teeth in fine fettle for eating —(sotto voce)
and drinking.”
She: “That’s it.”
He: “The stuff that the doctors and the
dentists recommend.”
She: “Yes, yes, that’s it —Odol.”
He: “The liquid antiseptic dentifrice, that
puts all other dentifrices and tooth-powders, tooth-
pastes, and tooth-soaps out of the running.”
She: “That’s it, you darling.”
He: “That keeps the teeth from decaying, and the
mouth from bad tastes and the breath from malodors,
and really costs less than other dentifrices because it
lasts so long—and all that.”
She: “Why, you splendid Phil, you know all about
it, after all.”
He: “Yes, my typist, Miss Smith, has just been
posting me.”
She: “What’s that you sayP”
He: “Yes, love, I’ll bring
you the bottles—Sweet
Rose - flavored Odol
for you —the Stand-
ard Odol, with the
stronger flavor,
shall be mine.
I’ll not forget.”
She : “ But
what’s that
about Miss —
what do you call
herP ” Miss —
Miss —”
He: “Oh, I’ll
not miss anything this
time, sweetest. By-by.”
(Rings off.)

Chunder Loo
Of Akim Foo
Flies at top speed
In the blue.
Upturned faces
Far, far down,
Watch him skimming
O’er the town.
Suddenly from
Out a cloud
Tins flash downward
Tc the crowd.
And they hear a
Clear voice call,
“Cobra! Cobra
Over all 1"

a coarse beard just as smoothly and easily

H. B. Irving has scored a success as “Nobody,” in
the Drury Lane production of “Everywoman”
vised and polished up by poet Stephen Phillips.
Critics agree that H.B.’s “Nobody” is the triumph
of the acting. By the way, Cassell and Co. have
just published a book about the young man -an
appreciation of his career as actor and author,
“J.F ”: Sunday shows are still being vigorously
discussed here in London. A leading American
manager, Colonel H. W. Savage, has been telling
how the system works on his side. ’Frisco and St.
Louis insist on Sunday night shows, and managers
have to meet the demand. Chicago is the same.
People there are . paid on Saturday night, and they
would not spend a happy Sunday if they did not
wind up the Sabbath diversion with a visit to a
theatre or music hall. In other cities, where the
German element is numerous, Sunday night shows
are a necessity. But there are a few centres of
culchaw—like Boston and Philadelphia—that do not
want such frivolities, and will not have them. It
is largely “a question of local conditions. But,
adds Col. Savage, “we don’t get our legitimate,
critical, residential audiences until the Monday.
They give us the real verdict.”
“J.F.”: Another of the old brigade on the rocks—
Richard Temple, creator of the Mikado, Dick Dead-
eye and other leading roll's in the Gilbert and Sulli-
van operas. He was discovered in poverty and
obscurity at Southend, near London. A fund has
been started, and a big benefit matinee performance
is -being organised.
From London: Sydney-born beauty Marie Lohr,
who recently became Mrs. Prinsep, does not mean
to allow that small matter to interfere with her
stage ambitions. She is rehearsing the leading she-
part in “Doormats,” a new play by Hubert H.
Davies, to be produced almost at once at Wynd-
ham’s.
“Rosna” (from London) : At the Scala Theatre,
where the kinemacolor shows the Durbar, Corona-
tion, Investiture and similar Royal jinks, you can
actually see gay crowds of Sydney surf-bathers now-
o’-nights, with pictures of beauty _ competitions and
other things indigenous to Australia Felix.
Edmund Gwenn, the brainy comedian .whom the
Williamson firm imported with the original Ben
Hur” co. for Australia, has broken out as a manager
in London, in partnership with a Miss Trevelyan.
Their first production, “Little Miss Llewelyn, ’ is
said to be a draw. For that matter, the first at-
tempt of any new management ought to be a success,
otherwise the venture doesn’t justify itself.
Dena Cooper, an Australian girl who was in “The
Fatal Wedding,” but escaped, was at latest collect-
ing complimentary press notices in Seattle (U.S.A.).
Her work in a one-act playlet called “The Confes-
sion” incited one critic to call her “the Olga
Nethersole of vaudeville.”
“Badnib” : The announcement that the newest Ade-
laide theatre is to be named the Princess leads me
to ponder as to whether the naming of theatres as
“The King’s,” “Her Majesty’s,” and so on, is mere
habit or pure grovel. The naming of theatres seems
to be a study in the inappropriate. For instance,
a stranger hearing there was an Opera House in Mel-
bourne would naturally imagine the building was
specially erected for high-class operas, but on enter-
ing the place lie would be surprised to find, maybe,
an individual performing musical selections on a
couple of jam-tins, or a pair of coons singing rag-
time. If he heard there was a Lyric Theatre in
Sydney, he’d guess it to be the home of poetry and
music. Instead, he would get a dose of flickeritis.
And he would get some more at the Lyceum, which
was never an intellectual gymnasium in its life.
I’m waiting to crown with a wreath of gumleaves
the individual who is plucky enough to give his
show place, a real Australian native name. Wouldn’t
seem loyal, p’raps, but ’twould be patriotic; ami
Patriotism covers a multitude of errors.
“Ruru”: The comedian seems to have the best
end of the theatrical stick. The two actors who
of recept years left most cash behind them were
J. L. Toole and Edward Terry. Remember Terry’s
“Churchwarden” at the London Olympic Theatre,
somewhere about ’B7. The play was an uproarious farce
in three acts, in which Terry kept the middle of the
stage nearly all the time. It was in danger of being an
awful frost. The Olympic was then a house in the
theatrical byways, and nothing produced there had
Scored heavily for some time. Terry evolved a notable
advertising dodge. Scores of sandwich-men paraded
the streets, carrying on their shoulders enormous
churchwarden pip.es. The strange implements were
about 10ft. long,.'and the procession made an impos-
ing spectacle. Tne scheme worked, the play went
like a bush fire/and Terry stayed long in London; as
its best laughter-maker. By the time of his death
he had accumulated £44,056, and if death hadn’t
cut him short he might have heaped up £44,057.
“Screen”: .Australian biographs give Bernhardt
as Elizabeth. Sounds odd to write that Sally Heart-
burn is too fat for anything, but she is rather over-
plump for Bess. What a make-up she could have
done in the generation before last! I recall your
Elizabeths in Australia: Ristori, Mrs. Majeroni,
Nance O’Neil and Miss Elva Rogers, who quite
embodied ZuccherO’s portrait in a recent Melbourne
amateur production of German’s opera, “Merrie Eng-
land.” Mrs. Majeroni made up fairly. Riston and
the O’Neil were out of it. The play given by all
these three was a very indifferent one by the Italian
Giacometti, who wrote this and Marie Antoinette
for Ristori. Elizabeth figures prominently m Louis
Parker’s “Ifrake,” produced by Beerbohm Tree, and
to be seen in Australia next year.
“Ivanger” : Has Australia ever produced a snappier
work of local manufacture than the jiu-jitsu turn
which Fred Leslie and Ivy Schilling contribute to
“Florodora” at Her Majesty’s (Melb.) ? The combi-
nation of noveltv, agilitv, grace, adroitness and
humor is. remarkable. . The glorious figure of the
woman is equally well matched and contrasted with
the figure of the man, who is only a trifle the tallei
of the pair. They go through their strenuous man-
oeuvres with a perfectly balanced skill. “Some exer-
cises in the gentler arts of jiu-jutsu is the pro-
gramme’s definition of these exciting gambols, but
they would seem dangerously rough if the artists
were less expert. Leslie is said to have devised the
“turn” from two years’ study of the tricks and man-
ners of Jap wrestlers, and Miss Schilling is the apt
pupil who has bettered his-, instruction. .They stand
for Gravity and Gaiety, she being the joyous one.
They lack only one charm for an Australian aud-
ienc;e__they were not imported at enormous expense
from London or Paris. Their turn is not “the talk
of Europe.” It would be if they took it there.
A STREET SINGER.
[FOR THE BULLEITN.]
Veiled and with slow and wavering step she comes
The step that ‘tells the secret of her years.
Her broken voice is hoarse and thin with tears ;
Homeless, she sings old songs of loves and homes,
And the wide streets and skies hear all her pain.
Men pause and listen —laugh and pass again.
One knotted hand holds o er her aching breast
The worn plaid shawl to shield her from the wind,
And loitering youths, kerb-herding with their kind,
Fling to accustomed ears a coarser jest,
While from some window high above the throng
A woman's face leans out to heed the song.
A paltry gift of coin! She curtseys low.
Her stooping form still keeps an Old World grace,
And if the breeze should lift from her white face
That guarding veil, old beauties it might show,
Something that Time has not yet garnered in—
A regal line of brow, a rounded chin.
Veiled, with a slow and dragging step she comes,
The step that echoes of the hopeless years,
And all her voice is full of unshed tears.
Homeless, she sings old runes of loves and homes,
And the uncaring skies hear all her pain !
QUEENSLAND.
M. FORREST.

BRISBANE
IN BANANA LAND
Saloon!
[For The Bulletin.]
By the Kuranda, which left Iniiisfail (Geraldton)
for Townsville on Thursday, were 10 Chinese in the
saloon and 19 Europeans.— Brisbane paper.
I saw Ah Sin, who travelled by saloon;
He gaily smoked a fragrant, fine cigar:
lie wore a tailored suit, and hummed a tune,
And called for sundry whiskies at the bar:
His wife was with him in her satins clad,
Her baby feet encased in tiny shooii:
And with her went a little saffron lad.
I say. Ah Sin was travelling by saloon.
I saw Ah Sin as in the days of old,
When packed he came like herrings in a
tin;
When like a beast of burden he was sold
By brother Ghows who shore him to the
skin;
I saw the rout in Palmer’s early days.
When diggers drove the slant-eyed crowd
before;
A thousand pigtails flew a thousand ways,
A thousand heathen yelled a wild “Whaf-
for!”
Back to my mem’ry flashed a vision then
Of squalid hut wherein he once abode:
Of swinish filth that gathered round his den.
Of baskets twain that held his weary
load ;
I heard his morning cry of “Cabbagee!”
And saw him smile on Jane or Maly Ann:
I heard the gibe of burly Pat Magee—
“Begob, the crayture’s talkin’ like a
man!” •
I saw them dig his brother from the coal
Of some big liner bound for Sydney town;
I saw the starvling snap at any dole
The kindly seamen’s pity flung him down;
I fancied then the foetid life he fled
In horrid slum of Canton or Han-Kow;
When he could face a coffin-trip so dread.
What was behind this gaunt and coaly
Chow?
But now the times have changed. At home
his kin
Have done their Manchu rulers fairly
brown;
Have cut their six-foot pigtails to the skin,
And turned their ancient China upside
down;
And Sin. who once a scapegoat seemed for
all
Who fancied he was weak and they were
slick,
Now looks as if his back were to the wall.
And he who once was football now would
kick.
And so Ah Sin now travels by saloon.
And in the deck-chair takes his portly
doze ;
He wears his silken ’jamas ’neath the moon,
And spreads abroad his genial adipose;
No more unkempt and ragged as of yore,
He bears his load beneath the burning
noon ;
With rings and diamonds flashing to the
fore,
I say, Ah Sin now travels by saloon.
QUEENSLAND. F. B.
NOT AN ACTOR.
"Is he much of an actor?”
"Not much. Pretty fair in Shakspeare. But,
Gad, sir! he can’t patter or do a clog
dance for sour apples.”
T. G. Gibson, a citizen of the Ma State,
who has been roughing it in the Peninsula
that pokes its nose up towards the Equator,
says that Japs are everywhere in that coun-
try, and they know every detail of It. They
could tell Gibson the anchorage places, and
where to get water, and put him on to other
useful information. The pearling and beche-
de-mer industries are in their hands, the
provision debarring them from holding
licenses being grotesquely unsuccessful.
Queensland municipal authorities, backed
up by a metropolitan daily, are whining for
a restoration of the old endowment system
under which State doles relieved Bumble of
the duty of effective taxation. About two
years ago a shire council deputation wailed
to Denham about the burdens of taxation,
and asked him for a loan of £l5OO for a
bridge. Denham put a few deft questions,
and ascertained that that council’s total in-
come was only f 4 OO. and that the rate struck
was id. in the £!
A NOCTURNE.
“ Hi, you fiend, wake up! I can stand a plain snore, but I bar those damnable variations .”
Tom Glassey, Queensland’s first Labor
member, who might still have been the Nor-
thern party’s leader if his head hadn’t gone
on growing in girth long after his feet had
stopped, is prodding the Liberal party, and
asking it to consider his great claims to be
that piebald horse’s rider for the new Lilley
(Federal) electorate.
The I'pwnsville Bulletin is tasting all
sorts of luck, Its weekly, the N.Q. Regis-
ter, lately came out with 92 pages, making
what the paper itself averred was a record
for the North. Two days later the premises
were ablaze, and the Daily Bulletin issued
itself on one small sheet, with apologies for
its sad bereavement.
At the recent wool sales in Brisbane there
was a record yarding of 50,000 bales. Bris-
bane first had the joy of hearing the wool-
buyers bark in 1898, when 11,000 bales were
sold. Since then the figure has been climb-
ing all the while; but a check will pfobably
come now from the Central Queensland
sales, which are to be held at Rockhampton.
The evidence given at the inquiry into the
disappearance of William Roseby from the
s.s Wodonga between Townsville and Bris-
bane makes an astounding sea-story. Roseby
was missed, and inquiries were made: but it
was not till some days later that a fireman
casually mentioned to the boatswain that
the missing man must be the bloke he saw
go over the side. The boatswain told the
chief officer, but that worried functionary
does not appear to have taken the yarn seri-
ously ; he made no mention of it in the log,
and did not even report it to the captain.
That he did not send for the fireman and
question him is quite understandable—a fire-
man who does not mention till two days
later that he saw a bloke go over the side in
mid-ocean is quite an impossible subject, and
liable to cause an officer to lose his temper.
It is only fair to say that the fireman’s ver-
sion of the story remains untold, and as he
has quitted the ship and forgotten to leave
an address it may possibly remain untold.
Still, as there are suggestions of drunken
rows, stolen money, and possible murder, it
is highly desirable that the casual fireman
should be hunted up and requested to tell
exactly what he did see when the “bloke
went over.”
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, November 2, 1912.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
This has been a dreary week without the
thrill even of a tea scramble. The only
flutter in the hamlet was the Munro-Tanner
wedding on Thursday. The ’groom is one
of the pearling Munros, and the pretty little
bride is a daughter of Mrs. Tanner, of South
Brisbane. Tanner pere, when alive, had in-
terests in the Solomon Islands. The splicing
was done on the main deck of the Cathedral,
with Canon Pattinson as officer in charge.
The wedding garment was of lawn and lace,
topped off with a headpiece of ruched aero-
plane. Another of the Tanner family was
bride’s understudy, and Dr. Shaw did the
giving away. Later in the day, the newly-
wed clambered aboard the China boat Em-
pire, and started on a two-years’ tour in the
worn tracks of the globe-trotter. After that,
they will be glad to return and Settle down
in Australia.
His Scotchellency Macgregor, who is
“batching” these days, was present at a con-
cert given by the burgoo-and-haggis section
of the communityh-on Thursday in honor of
the purchase of the Centennial Hall by the
Scawtch Association and Burns Club. A
kilted band droned the chiel into his seat,
and a programme, which included not more
than 23 performers, unwound itself slowly,
with frequent stoppages for entirely un-
necessary encores. In the interval, sonsy
bairns bit at string-suspended buns, and
endeavored to lift apples from a tub with
their unassisted molars, and joined in other
pastimes peculiar to the aborigines of Cale-
donia.
The temperature to-day was about 1050
in the shade of the telegraph wire, judging
by the feel of the atmosphere ; nevertheless,
hospital “tag” day was the occasion of in-
defatigable labor on the part of a consider-
able number of responsible enthusiasts. The
system is certainly an improvement on the
old method of collecting or commandeering
small change from man by wheedling him
with the goo-goo eye or pursuing him even
to his last stand in the sanctuary where the
peaceful barmaiden soothes his brow (or
something) with milk and turpentine. A
“tag” cost a shilling, and having obtained
one, man was, for the nonce, unhunted. He
deserves a rest occasionally.
Mrs. John Hicks returned from America
this week, leaving daughter Gladys to winter
in California with Mrs. Spencer Smith.
Mrs. Nixon Smith, who has just come
back after a trip to England, fed about 200
friends at the Dutton Park kiosk on Friday.
The kiosk is high, overlooks one of the pret-
tiest parts of the river and catches the
breeze, all of which makes it a popular out-
door browsing nlace at this time of year.
Mrs. Nixonsmith (all these Smiths ought to
be compelled by law to make one job of it
like that) wore a grey and lavender crea-
tion and a pansy-encircled hat built in
Paris. That makes you wince, Thargomin-
dah —you who have to wear a mere Aus-
tralian cadey !
The bicycle sports drew big crowds to-
day, but everybody who is anybodv got into
its latest garments and peacocked about the
lawn at Ascot. Celia Cooper, the Chief
Justice’s daughter, sported another white
frock and a blue hat: Mrs. Stanley Chap-
man looked her graceful self in cream lace;
and a host of girleens wore white muslins
or voiles, many of them embroidered in the
queer, dull colors that are so much seen at
present.
A particularly shocking thing occurred
this week. A popular girl in Warwick,
whose marriage should have taken place in
about a fortnight, received a box of choco-
lates by post, and after eating two, died.
A friend who partook of one chocolate from
the box very nearly went the same sad road.
The why, what and who are still a
at time of writing.
' Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
The Denman Government has just introduced
a Public Servants’ Superannuation Bill, in
which it is provided that the State shall
subsidise the fund to the tune of £5OOO per
annum —a quite unsound principle. The
taxpayer has already contributed £260,000 in
the last 20 years to the rotten Police Super-
annuation Fund, so he knows the meaning
of State subsidy.
Election appeals are the rich man’s game
all the time. Labor has just appealed in
two cases and forced two new elections, and
in both of them the Liberals have come off
victorious. The Liberals appealed in one
ease, got the election declared null and void,
and won that seat, too. Labor seldom wins
an appeal in Bananaland, and when it does
the expense is crippling.
Brisbane boating men are perpetually
bombarding Minister Appel about turning
St. Helena into a pleasure resort. Appel
obdurately refuses, and the rowing and
yachting people whisper that he shows them
no consideration. Appel alleges that he
considers them all the time —a delicate way
of alluding to the fact that he is enlarging
the accommodation and improving the cell-
comforts of the prison which occupies most
of the islet.
On the second appeal to Port Curtis elec-
tors, Laborite Breslin lost his seat by 96
votes. Kessell, the new member, is the
Mayor of the lethargic city of Gladstone,
where he runs the Observer in a ponder-
ous way. Likewise he is part owner of
Turkey Station, which was often mentioned
in the sensational Daniells murder case.
Kessell will now be a busy man —that is, if
he makes any attempt to keep his half-score
of large promises.
JUDGE CHUBB (QUEENSLAND).
Denham’s Liquor Bill, which renewed its
acquaintance with the Assembly the other
day, has lost several teeth in its absence.
When it was first hatched it aroused so much
opposition from some of Digby’s Fat friends
that the general strike and the political
upset came as a stroke of Providence to the
Government by giving it a chance to shelve
the obstreperous thing. The original Bill
prohibited the employment of discolored
cooks in hotels. That clause has now been
thrown into the slop-bucket on the plea that
it would be impossible to find enough cooks
with white skins and clean habits. To make
things pleasanter for Bung, murder has
been done to the old provision that a light
in the bar during prohibited hours should be
prima facie evidence that the pewter was
passing over the bar. Per contra, to make
things pleasanter for Cold Tea, it is pro-
vided in connection with local option that a
simple majority shall prevail, providing 25
per cent, of the voters have not been too
tired to visit the polling booth.

Amount
LhoG
THE NEW FEATURES OF THIS REGISTER.
Nine separate totals in pounds, shillings and
pence of different kinds of transactions and of the sales of different
assistants, in addition to grand total of all cash sales.
Amount of each cash sale made by each assistant is
automatically added into a separate total. Every time a sale is made
the amount is added to the total previously shown for that assistant.
Every penny received for cash sales is recorded twice by
this, register—on the adding wheels of the assistant making the sale
and on the ‘ ‘ secret ’ ’ or grand total adder. A printed record giving full
particulars is made at the lime time.
Amount in pounds, shillings and pence received in payment of
accounts is shown on another set of adding wheels. This obviates the
necessity of going through dockets or cash book to find how much money
has been received on account —the total is ready at all times.
Total value of all goods sold on credit is recorded separately, A
glance will show the amount of credit business for the day or to date
from any stated time. This record enables the shopkeeper to see at once
what his total turnover has been.
Every penny paid out is added to previous total amount of
disbursements.
Think of the advantages of having all these separate totals and of
being able to obtain such valuable information by merely turning a key
and glancing at the records.
THESE ARE SOME OF THE IMPROVEMENTS.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will be glad to
hear of a simple and scientific device which restores
hearing, no matter how old you are. No mystery or
secrecy about it; we describe it fully, and explain
just how it acts. Yoii use spectacles as sight magni-
fiers, and in the same way you use this sound magni-
fier to hear better. Unlike spectacles, the Wilson
Common-sense Ear-Drums are invisible and comfort-
able. No one can tell you are wearing them. They
rest the ear nerves by taking the strain off them —the
strain of trying to hear indistinct sounds. What a
relief to have done with that tiring strain. Being
made of the softest Para rubber they cannot be felt,
even when the head rests on a pillow. For the same
reason, a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. They protect the tender inner parts of the
ear, that are so sensitive, from cold winds, dust, sud-
den and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand how the Sound
Magnifiers restore hearing, we give an illustration of
one as it lies in the ear. You will notice that the
Sound Magnifier is shaped like a trumpet, and so con-
veys even the faintest sounds right to the natural drum.
Being of the softest rubber, it cannot hurt the ear.
It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort.
The division that runs
across the mouth is to al-
low the removal and inser-
tion of the Ear-Drum, and
to brace it.
The shape of the Wilson
Ear-Drum has been chosen
after years of experience as
the best adapt-
ed to carry the
faintest sound.
Of course, if
you have lost
the h ea r i ng-
nerve co m-
pletely, or were
born deaf, we
cannot pretend
to cure you. Only a miracle could
do that, in spite of some of the
absurd claims made to-day. But
of you can hear the faintest sound,
do not despair. Ypu will never
regret trying the Wilson Ear-
Drums. We have letters to show
you from deaf people in Australia
and New Zealand, who have been
re 3
cured, and you can be cured, too. You ...ay wear
the Ear-Drums day and night, and take them out
whenever you wish. Children can wear them (we
have special sizes for children from six to twelve).
Even a child can insert and remove them without the
(east danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s single
visit). This includes a pair of drums (they are only
sold in pairs), a fine nickel remover, and also an
inserter. After the first pair is bought you may pur-
chase a single drum at any time for Bs., but the
pair you get at first will last quite two years. Do
not ask for a trial pair. We do not send them out
on trial, and so they are never secondhand. Use the
Order form below, and the Outfit will be sent you
at once, with the fullest instructions. Don’t delay,
but have the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.

Those who are troubled with falling hair, dandruff,
baldness, itching scalp, etc., know full well the
humiliation that they cause. There is no need, how-

quickly overcome by my True Hair Grower.
I am not an advertising quack, but am a business
man. My hair began to grow thin about the time
I reached the age of thirty. When I had reached the
age of thirty-five the top of my head was quite bald.
I had considerable pride, and sought for something
to cause renewed growth. I invested in every pre-
paration that I could see or hear of, and here let me
say that not only did I try all the English so-called
restorers, but those of Continental Europe. I also
visited specialists. My experimenting cost me many
sovereigns and considerable time- —yet the hairs came
not. I had given up the idea of ever growing hair
again. One afternoon, while enjoying a brief busi-
ness respite by an Alpine trip, I fell in with an old
collegiate chemist, whose hair was particularly plen-
tiful. I jokingly remarked that I wished he could
spare some of his hair for mv benefit. This lead him
to tell me that he understood the human hair as well
as a good dentist understands teeth. He wrote out a
formula, and suggested that I have a quantity of the
compound prepared. I did this a few days later. 1
was astonished to see the hair coming forth after
several applications, and within six weeks my head
was covered by a firm, yet soft, black growth of hair.
Since then the hair on my head has been as luxuriant
as when in my school days. Having arranged for
the legal right to do so, I have for time been
privately selling this marvellous hair-growing com-
pound. I hold over one thousand letters from people
who have actually grown new hair through the
medium of my true hair grower. _ I do not pretend
to say it is the only hair grower in the world —I am
not interested in decrying others —but I do assert
that it is the most reliable, and in the end by far the
cheapest.

The Inky Way.
The dull echoes of the Balkan eruption remind us
that modem war news is mostly froth, and that the
modern city dweller is little better off than the old-
time villager, who drowsed along in ignorance of
what was happening, until a man in dinted armor
rode down the main lane on a sorrowful horse, with
enough soldiers’ tales and loot to keep him three
months at the ale house. By virtue of electricity,
the modern war liar is no mere peddler of lies, but
a wholesaler, who claims splendid victories for his
own side, and represents the enemy as being in full
flight and strewing the landscape with abandoned
guns and camp stores. It reads very well until both
liars’ stories are printed together, and the two op-
posing armies are represented as advancing vic-
toriously to the rear, and snowing their utter con-
tempt for the accused foe by allowing him to swing
them in the air by the slack of their pants. That is when
the reader, hurrying into work, gets mad and throws
his paper out of the tram, and is fined for littering
the streets with rubbish. The most informative item
of a recent week was that a flock of 80 war corres-
pondents was yarded up at a place some 40 miles or
so from the actual front, and forbidden, under pain
of court-martial, to tell anything resembling the
truth. If the majority of the cables were correct,
and the Turks were being driven back, the firing-line
was getting further away from those correspondents
every hour. On the other hand, if the Constanti-
nople reports were right, those 80 suppressed writers
were liable to find battle and sudden death all round
them at any minute. On the face of it, war corres-
ponding appears to be one of the easiest and safest of
professions. You are not allowed to do your work,
or to crawl into danger, and your only chance of
escaping death from senile decay is to perish of
mumps. Many a tousled female with a riotous, pot-
throwing, hair-dragging husband would leap at a
war correspondent’s job.
* * * *
“Looking Backward”: Largely I agree with “Lines-
man” (B. 27/9/’l2) as to the Melbourne Herald’s
fall from gracefulness in its headlines. But he has
omitted some of the fall, and has not noticed some
of the beauties which the paper retains. The omis-
sions include “La Belle France,” which invariably
headed tales of riot in Paris during the Reay regime.
This showed a gift of subtle sarcasm, an intimate
acquaintance with the French tongue and a love of
the romantic. Again, no smelling-out of Jews ever
occurred in Russia but it produced the heading,
“Holy Russia,” another specimen of the delightful
and playful satire which lurked beneath the iron ex-
terior of the warlike Colonel. At times this many-
sided journalist lent himself to the fleshly school —
“Woes of the Flesh” topped the medical column,
and it remains there to this day. Another legacy in-
herited by Editor Davidson is “The Law of the Land,”
which still heads the advice about boundary-fence
problems, the trespassing fowl evil, and as to whether
a man may marry “another woman,” if he puts an
advt. in the paper saying that he is going to do n
unless Sarah turns up within seven days. _ Color-
Colonel Reay still operates from the fog city. He
has become hopelessly fleshly. “At the Heart of Em-
pire” is his usual heading, varied by “At the Pulse
of Empire.” It may be that the neck, the knees, the
feet and the forehead of Empire will have their
little day, and perhaps even its calves will be allowed
to make an appearance. Of this the Australian pub-
lic may be assured —that whatever is delineated by
the gallant Colonel will always be clothed in a
complete Canadian bathing costume with the skirt of
ample length.
* * * *
Thanks to “wireless,” ocean journalism is advanc-
ing by leaps and bounds. The Cunard Bulletin is a
really first-class, up-to-date daily morning paper,
edited, printed and published on the big liners of
the Cunard Company that are perpetually racing
across the Atlantic. The copy sent to this scribe by
a passenger contains 16 pages. There are photo-
graphs and other illustrations, wireless news from
London and New York, latest stock and share quota-
tions and a leading article, a magazine page, a
serial story, dramatic notes and “prelusive specials”
by eminent English and American writers. There is
a staff of newsboys on each liner, and the paper is
delivered practically every morning at each subscrib-
ing cabin-door.
A pressman writes in a spirit of enthusiastic envy
of an alleged soft job: Hansard reporters here in
Westralia have particularly rosy times. They re-
ceive £375 per annum, and Parliament seldom sits
more than five months in the year. That means they
have about half of their time free from the cares
and frequent drinks that beset newspaper reporters.
They fill in their time by writing for the West
Australian and other papers, and doing literary
work for the Immigration, Lands and other Govern-
ment Departments, to the exclusion of journalists
who can hardly find a casual tram fare. A pair of
Hansard men recently divided £l5O between them
for hashing up a lot of stale information and ancient
statistics for the State Agent-General’s London office,
and others draw quite a decent living wage over and
above their official salaries by writing football, ten-
nis, yachting and other notes. Many newspaper men,
with the inevitable wife and inseparable kiddies, are
questioning the sincerity of the Government when it
comes to the principle of “one man, one job.”
* * * *
“Chudah”: The American Israelite translates
from the Welt, a German paper, a sketch of Yid
journalism in Whitechapel, London, which has multi-
tudes of Jewish tailors, shoemakers, carpenters,
cigarette-makers, pedlars, and all sorts. Even the
policemen speak Yiddish in Whitechapel. The Welt
writer called at a newspaper office. Found the edi-
tor’s wife holding her baby, and setting type. She
calls out to husband Abraham: “Now den, Emanuel,
look sharb mid dot leading artigel.” “Ollaride, ma
tear, ollaride,” says Abraham.
The Hibernian bull is still cultivated. A
recent issue of the Irish Cyclist contained
this delightful mix-up: “It is practically
impossible for an individual rider not in the
swim to score off his own bat.” The bull is
an interesting form of humor in the hands
of those who know how to use it, and it
doesn’t force its owner to rise at absurdly
early hours as the cow does.
JANE—JEANNETTE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When Jane came up to Sydney town she wore an
antiquated gown,
With many folds about her feet. In length it but
escaped the street.
Her shoes were broad with heels quite flat. On her
short hair was perched a hat
With faded roses ’round the brim. Her gloves were
made of cotton scrim.
Jane could write prose or verse, she said, on every-
thing alive or dead,
But men glanced at her dress array, and said: “The
staff is full to-day.”
So Jane bought her a dress quite right—at thigh, and
knee and ankle tight;
Adorned her head with much false hair, above which
drooped a hat with rare
Hen plumes that wore a dye of gold to match the
hair. And I was told
Her lips she touched with carmine paint, her brow
with Chinese ink. As saint
Jane had been scratched, but as a sinner she looked
a very likely winner.
She’d gloves of kid and silken hose, and, like one
who her value knows,
She wrote herself “Jeannette”—not “Jane” —and
sought the Fourth Estate again.
Jeannette became at once the rage, and edits now a
“Woman’s Page.”
N.S.W.
J. S. GRIFFITHS.

Marg'aret Dale-Owen
EXTOLS
REXONA
Margaret Dale-Owen, who is now giving a magnificent character-study of Bessie Meets
in “Get-Rich-Quick Wallingford,” which has already put up the Australian record-run for
any American comedy, began her professional career with Ben Greet. With him she toured
England, playing Audrey in “As You Like It,” Maria in “Twelfth Night,” Nerissain “The
Merchant of Venice,” and other Shakespearian rdles. She then went through the United
States with that famous Shakespearian actress,
Ada Rehan, in a Shakespearian repertoire.
After this, she played the principal comedy-
part (Lady St. Azuline) in the long run of “ The
Price of Peace,” at McVicker’s Theatre,
Chicago. For the last nine years Miss Dale-
Owen has been leading lady with her husband,
the distinguished comedian, Harry Corson
Clarke, perhaps the greatest living artiste in
stage make-up. With him she has appeared
in his gigantic success, “ What Happened to
Jones” ; and in his many other triumphs, such as
“Mr. Foster of Chicago,” “What Did Tom-
kins Do,” “Young Mrs. Winthrop,” etc. Miss
Dale-Owen testifies to the efficacy of a well-
known remedy, as follows :
Criterion Theatre.
Rexona Proprietary Co., Sydney,
Sydney. Aug. 18, 1912.
Dear Sirs, — During the voyage to Aus-
tralia, I ran out of the famous American
salve that I had been in the habit of using
for sunburn, chapped hands, or any chance
abrasion. Hearing your Rexona so exten-
sively praised, I purchased some. It made
such a hit with me that there will never be
a chance of any other salve displacing it in
my dressing case. I find that Rexona is a
perfect balm. Your sincerely,
MARGARET DALE-OWEN.

A Suburban Adventure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I THINK that star’s remarks were most
absurd.
She said that Helen was a nicer girl,
With white skin glowing like a Phrygian
curd,
Except in spots where it was like a pearl;
I would not even take the old moon’s word
If she should try my temper with a tale
Of Ariadne’s beauty cold and stale.
Girls are the ripe fruit of the wonder tree,
Whose blossoms are a song, whose leaves
a sigh ;
And no girl ever yet was ripe for me
Except the one maid with the happy eye,
And the dark hair all wavy as tficAea,
Who sat beside me on the darkened stair
And thought my hottest kisses good to
wear.
She wore them just as ancient Helen did
The joy that Paris pressed upon her lips,
The while with Trojan elegance he hid
His classic nose in rapturous eclipse
Within her tresses, and his lithe arm slid
Around the yielding sweetness of her
waist.
Showing his exquisite and princely taste.
I care not in the least what stars may say,
In spite of all they may have known and
seen.
Nancy, with half her dark, bright hair
astray,
Is better far than any ancient queen
Of whom some blind old minstrel made his
lay. .
Besides, the person Paris loved is dead.
And Nancy’s lips are soft and warm and
red.
As I remarked, we sat upon the stairs.
While others rioted in frosted beer;
I think we went apart to say our prayers,
Or else to drop unseen a quiet tear;
t know we felt the little rosy airs
Blowing from elfland, and our joy was
such,
My last, least whisker trembled to her
touch.
“Sweetheart,” I said, “the happy time is
ripe.
Somewhere with crimson claws the cray-
fish dreams,
In lighted halls the waiters deftly wipe
The little glasses emptied of ice creams,
To evening revel Pan’s clear reedlets pipe,
And we could slip out by the postern door
To eat the oyster in his frosted gore.
We went on tiptoe down the darkened way,
With lifted feet we crossed the sleeping
cat.
The house-dog wagged his tail with naught
to say, . . ,
We passed the milk-jug silent on the piat.
The shadows in the yard were cold and grey.
But deftly through the lane we passed and
found
That night was starry, and the white moon
round.
Away! Some silver in my pocket rang.
Enoueh to speed the gladness of our quest.
The quick blood leaping in our pulses sang
The little lilt bur warm hearts loved the
best, , J ,
And Nancy on my left arm seemed to hang
Kike some delicious and full-flavored peach
Poised on a branch within joy’s easy
reach.
Tremendous consequences often flow
From young and insignificant events.
Oaks, it has been observed, from acorns
grow,
And simple dirt produces mighty rents.
1 scarcely tiling, if vou had told us so,
- We should have readily accented then
The tale of what occurred that night at
ten.
We had some little oysters on a plate.
Some prawns and other simple, quiet tacts.
And all on tip-toe. ere the night was late,
We came as Caesar’s ghost, when it enacts
Its creepv part, back to the rearward gate.
We passed the dog, the milk-jug and the
Nor brushed the sleeping whiskers of the
cat.
The house was silent as a butter shop
Upon the pleasant Sunday afternoon,
So silent and so dark no single drop
Could trickle downwards from the melting
moon.
We could not tell the proper nlace to ston, _
But felt like Wowsers groping irt their
prayers — „
Had some bad burglar carried oft the
stairs?
We could not find them. Neither high nor
low
Was any hint pf step or balustrade.
A dreadful feeling seemed to swell and grow
Around us till it made our souls afraid;
And all at once oiir senses quaked to know
We were not where we had been hereto-
~For 'Nancy and myself were both next
door.
We went with speed and silence as we came.
But somehow we aroused the sleeping cat.
Its whiskers bristled, and no longer tame,
Its angrv tail grew larger than the mat. •
I really think the <jog was most to blame,
The' fretful beak aroused the sleeping
night— .
I left him half my trousers m my flight.
The street awoke. The night grew loud with
sound.
My Nancy, screaming, fled with wintered
hair.
I crossed the back-yard fence at one fierce
bound
And landed on a ten-legged garden chair.
Whereat a most ijnkindlv star observed
That I had only got what I deserved.
But stars’ remarks are often most absurd.
An ankle sprained and half my trousers
lost. . , , ,
A meaner soul than mine perchance had
spurred
To sit upon the ground and count the cost.
[ took that twinkler at his word.
Placed in the credit column one dark curl,
And with one kiss of solace took the girl.
Girls are the ripe fruit of the wonder tree.
Whose blossoms are a song, whose leaves
a sigh;
And one girl made a strong appeal to me
A certain damsel with a happy eye,
And much dark hair all wavy as- the sea
Who went beside me down the darkened
Some oysters and a dog’s large mouth to
share.
N.S.W.
GEORGE STREET.
Characters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHEN a Man comes' to be called a
Character, his fate is sealed—if he
is making his living in any line of
business outside of the Comic Drama. Gnlj
rich men can afford to be Characters, and
by the time they have become rich they have
seldom any character left worth speaking ot.
To be a Character means to be different from
Other People, and Other People resent the
difference. Sometimes it amuses them, but
the amusement is always mingled with a
certain amount of contempt. “W hy can t lie
be like Us?” they say. To be like Us is
their criterion of good taste and standard or
sanity. x *
All the Characters I have known have been
poor men. If they were not poor originally,
they became poor soon after leaving the lut
of right-thinking Respectability. It was not
believed that they could have peculiar fancies
of their own on certain subjects and still
continue to have solid business capacity. I
knew a man who lost his billet in a bank in
this way. He was an accountant, and
highly respected until, one day, it was dis-
covered that he held by the old creed that
the earth is flat. After that, he was looked
upon with suspicion. The manager and the
directors seemed to consider that a man
who entertained such views could not be
safely trusted with accounts. He might in-
troduce the flatness of the earth into the
balance-sheet. So upon some pretext or other
he was invited to retire. He did, and died
shortly afterwards. He was a man who was
intended to be, and had been for years, a
respectable citizen, and nothing more. But
he became a Character, and all was over.
He should have gone into the Bush. The
Bush is the proper place for Characters. It
breeds them. In my comparatively short
experience of it, I met scores of them. I
remember, for instance, that when I was
editing a N.S.W. country paper, to which
was attached a small circulating library, a
chirpy old lady came into the shop, one day
to change her book. We entered into con-
versation. She was illuminative.
“J know how these novels are written,”
she remarked confidentially.
‘‘How, madam?”
“The writers take an old novel and tear
out the pages. Then they mix the pages to-
gether —shuffle them like this” — she took
up some loose sheets of copy-paper and
shuffled them in the manner of a gambler
stacking cards —“and then you have a new
book !”
The criticism was so curious and yet so
ironically apt that I have never forgotten |
it. But the old lady believed that she had
made a new discovery. She w r as a Charac-
ter.
There was also an old man who lived
in the Ranges. He borrowed from the
library “The Story of Mary, Queen of Scots.”
He had never heard of that hapless lady I
before. He was a slow but earnest reader.
When he came int ' the township now and
then he used to call on me and talk Mary
Stuart till I would have to take him over
to tlie hotel and turn him on to somebody
else. But one day he came into the office
with tears and fury in his eyes. “They’ve
beheaded her I” he said. “The blanky
hounds have murdered her! And that”
But what he said of Elizabeth no uninsured
paper would dare to print. He said it in
the township, all the same, and broke the
windows, and half-wrecked the bar of the
Royal Hotel —kept by a Presbyterian called
Anderson, who observed that Mary had got
no more than she deserved. He spent that
night in the lock-up.
It is a most strange, yet true, thing that
Mary, although she has been more than
three centuries dead, has her lovers even
to this day. I don’t think the same state-
ment can be made in connection with any
other heroine in history.
There are plenty of Characters in the
city, but they are, as a rule, lost in the
crowd. One only comes across them by
chance. For instance, there was an old
lady and her son —the son appeared to
be about 30 years old, and always, winter
arid summer, wore a woollen comforter
around his neck —who used to call in at the
Age and Argus office every night at about
10 o’clock and spend two hours going through
the advertisement columns of both papers.
They did this for years. Nobody knew
whence they came, whither they went, or
what they were looking for. When they had
finished their search, they would pass out
into the night without speaking to any-
body. There was another strange character
whom I used to see occasionally in Mel-
bourne. A man of apparently 45 or 50
years of age. Tall, gaunt and with a most
blood-curdling eye. Wherever a crowd was
gathered that sinister apparition would be
seen hovering on its outskirts and looking
at every face with a searching glare. One
evening I was standing close by him. An
Age man was with me. “May I ask, sir,”
said the latter, “if you are looking for any-
body ?”
The tall, gaunt man stared at him for a
moment with liis blood-curdling eye and
stalked silently away. It was generally
believed that he was seeking for an enemy.
I would not care to be that enemy.
Some two years ago there was a man
living in a little ragged tent of his own on
the shores of North Harbor, Sydney. He
made a pathetic living by selling firewood-
dry branches of trees gathered in the Gov-
ernment Reserve —to the people of Manly.
He used to convey the wood on a weird boat,
made by himself out of driftwood and pieces
of old canvas, painfully tarred so as to be
water-tight. In any ordinary boat you can
row over to Manly Pier from the place
where he had his tent in 20 minutes. The
voyage used to take him two hours to accom-
plish. I had a talk with him one day. He
was a Scotchman. He had never done any-

A SURGERY IN
YOUR HOME.
''-p'HE lacerated cut or tlie nasty ulcer which once you would \
i have had to see your doctor about can now, thanks to the
invention of Zam-Buk, be treated safely and successfully in
your own home. It was the discovery of the famous antiseptic pio-
cess, and its introduction into the home by Zam-Buk, that opened
up new possibilities in self-treatment.
The old-fashioned ointments are hindered in their work by the
rancid animal fats and mineral poisons they contain. No attempt is
made to draw out of the tissues the germ life and poison which are
the real cause of itching and inflammation.
It was in trying to get over these stumbling-blocks that the
wonderful discovery of Zam-Buk occurred. Careful research was
being conducted in the medicinal properties of certain herbs, and it
was found that one of them exerted an extraordinary destructive
effect on the microbes infesting a wound.
A certain combination of healing extracts was eventually found
which not only destroys the harmful germ-life, but effectively draws
out all bad matter and corruption, and having thus purified the
tissues, ensures a clean and rapid healing.
Thus at length the great antiseptic healer, Zam-Buk, was given to
the public, and was instantly acclaimed for simplifying the treatment
and hastening the cure of even obstinate attacks of eczema, ulcers,
piles, ringworm, and festering wounds.
Totally different in character from common ointments, Zam-Buk
is also immeasurably superior to them in having a threefold action
on the skin. First, it allays pain and irritation. Secondly, it kills
harmful germs, and thus removes all danger of- a cut or sore taking
“bad ways.”
Lastly, when the bad place has been thus purified, it grows a new
layer of perfectly healthy skin.
The treatment is so simple and yet so effective that to have a
ipot of Zam-Buk handy converts your home into an efficient, ever-
ready hospital.

thing wrong, but simply recognised that he
was a Failure. There was, however, enough
of the Scottish grit in him to prevent
him loafing around the town for chanty.
One evening—there was a rough sea on tnat
day—his coracle was found floating about
in fragments. The body of the man himselt
was washed up on the beach next day. l
was more sorry than I can tell over t" l ®
poor little tragedy. It was so pathetic, and
so lonesome, and so apparently unmeaning.
Going on the boat from Manly to Sydney
the other day I fell into conversation with
a little grey-bearded man who had a pole
with bunches of bush flowers attached to it.
resting on his knees. ~,, ,
"I’ve been two days gathering these, lie
said, “and I sat up all last night making
them into proper bunches for the maiket.
I saw that there were two dozen bunches
on the pole. „ , ... ~
“I get fourpence each for these—it they
are sold,” he said. “The last lot 1 took m
brought me only Is. Bd.”
He explained that half the proceeds went
to the middleman.
He informed me that he was living in a
cave, and has been living there and gather-
ing flowers for 14 years. Yet the man is a
licensed apothecary, and speaks well.
“But how about, your bed when the cold
nights come?”
"I have a stretcher and some old bags to
cover me. I had some blankets last winter,
but the larrikins stole them.”
Gathering flowers is the most poetical ot
occupations-—when you haven’t to gather
them for a living.
CREEVE ROE.
The Magazine and the
Novelette.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
English and American magazines
I nowadays are sliding along the old
track upon which ran the novelettes
that were the literary dissipation of oui
grandmothers. The magazine certainly goes
faster and runs easier than did the novelette
—just as the tram is an improvement on
the ’bus —but, for all that, it is only trav-
elling the old road —and the old road is a
darned dull and unnatural thoroughfare.
Here are a few bones dragged at
random from the rattling skeleton ot
a yarn in an alleged first-class mod-
ern magazine.
The central figure of the story is an
Italian count, whom we will call Hon
Pedro. (He isn’t called Don Pedro in
the story, but, when our grandmothers
knew him, he was always Don bomething-
or-other.) Don Pedro is a Blade of the cul-
tured variety. The authoress . (whose front
name is Elizabeth, and who is probably a
respectable young woman with gold-rimmed
pince-nez) tells us in her very first para-
graph: “....his intimates had called him
Satanuccio ever since a wild escapade of his
at the age of 18. This adventure, which con-
sisted in his eloping with the three-weeks
bride of the Doge of Venice, fell later into
the category of pardonable failings. lliat
incident lights up Pedro’s reputation pretty
well, but a little later on the authoress tells
us this about him : “But even the spending
of a mysterious revenue with princely gener-
osity, even laying waste the lives of many
fair and noble ladies —came at last to pall
on the Italian.” Having arrived at this
depressing stage, his thoughts turn wearily
towards matrimony: “He had always in-
tended to marry some nay. The day had
arrived. .. .but the wife? Clearly she must
not be of the type he knew too well already.
The laborious vivacity of the average smart
woman was beginning to be to him a mortal
weariness—another significant symptom. In
any case, to marry one of them. . . .he shud-
dered. It would be like marrying a brass
band.” , , . , ,
While he is in this frame of mind, he
drives a party of friends about the coun-
try in “a flashing equipage.” A word-pic-
ture of the “equipage” : “The luxurious in-
side was a miracle of ingenious devices for
supplying all the comforts of home. It rom
without all the world might see the admir-
able proportions an 4 perfect finish of this
dark-green miracle of elegance, with its
touches of bright scarlet, its four grey horses
with scarlet trappings, its livered servants
and jaunty groom, with the long coach-
horn shining like new gold and sending
troops of triumphal echoes flying over the
mountains.” Herein we see why Don Pedro
objected to a brass band wife. The “flash-
ing equipage” must have looked and sounded
very like the band waggon of a travelling
circus as it was. However, dreadfully, bored
as he is, he is still an enthusiastic ruin—or
ruiner—for, in the hall of a mountain hotel,
he catches sight of two women. Thus the
authoress, in her weird professional jargon :
“The cloaks and veils removed in the hall
revealed two people guiltless of. travel
stains, the handsome mother of a still more
beautiful girl. Girl? ‘She might be 2(>,
mused Don Pedro, ‘but, by all the gods,
what a face !’ ” Don Pedro, rudely enough,
“contracts his black brows” at these people,
and, after a second’s hesitation, goes for-
ward and asks them “if they remember him
after all this time?” Whereupon “The
Woman” started slightly, then seemed from
her deliberate look of well-bred coldness
about to deny the acquaintance —reconsid-
ered and bowed without noticing his offered
hand.” The reader immediately infers from
this that the matron is one of the “fpir and
noble ladies” whom Don Pedro “laid waste”
in the long ago. The fact that she signs to
her daughter with a “straight keen look” to
go and look out the window while she repels
Don Pedro, strengthens this suspicion, and the
following unpleasant passage-at-arms in the
next column confirms it. “She flashed a look
at him over his shoulder, and, quite low. she
said : “But I know yours, Satanuccio. Good-
bye.” The look, like most of her looks, is
effective, for “He turned a dull red as if she
had struck him across the face, and went
down the long dining-room with anger-lit
eyes.” However, his aristocratic self-re-
straint and alleged noble breeding doesn’t
prevent him from telling a lie to his guests
in order to make them alter their plans, and
they forthwith all proceed to another resort
to which the two ladies are bound. Arrived
there, he bullies the head waiter about where
he is going to sit until he catches sight of
the two women he is after. Thereafter he
goes the place, “with all his being con
centrated in his gleaming eyes.”
The story now being in full swing and the
plot well developed, the experienced reader
may proceed at a hand gallop. At dinner:
“The girl was dressed in white and wore no
ornament but a long chain wrought to look
like a long, golden rope, from which hung a
somewhat insignificant ‘St. George and the
Dragon’ in enamel.” She is just out of a
convent, and has a glassy look in her eye—
as witness the gifted authoress: “Calmly as
any saint looks out of a chapel window, she
gazed across the valley toward the higli-
perehed hamlet of Fettan.” However, this
ox-like placidity appeals to the jaded and
ruinous Don Pedro. "Don Pedro observed
with pleasure that although everyone in that
particular corner of world was staring
and speculating, the girl’s own eyes left the
table before her only to rest with serenity
unparalleled on the far-off white tower of
the Convent of Fettan.” It is remarked on
all sides that the mother and daughter have
not spoken to each other during the meal—
which may be due either to natural stupid-
ity, hunger or embarrassment at being stared
out of countenance. The authoress selects
a duke to voice the feelings of the company :
“ ‘They have not spoken to each other all
through dinner,’ whispered the Due de Bou-
tray, as the two ladies rose.” Then, “with-
out looking to right or left, they made their
way indoors,” and —in the approved fashion
of select novelette heroines —“are not seen
again that evening.” Follows a new chap-
ter, in which they comport themselves in a
frantically dull fashion, “drinking the
waters” by having the fluid brought to them
by menials and fending off people who, for
some inscrutable reason, desire their ac-
quaintance. Don Pedro occupies his time
showing off with juvenile simplicity. By
way of exhibiting his abilities as a whip, he
drives about recklessly, tooting his absurd
horn, and “brings his four superb horses up
with a magnificent sweep and flourish, stop-
ping them suddenly on their very haunches,
at the door of the great entrance.” The girl
“gazes at the brilliant apparition with large-
eyed wonder.” Probably she thought he was
the local fire brigade. After further hauling
of his steeds back on their haunches, Pedro
forces his company on the defenceless women,
and the speechless maiden once goes so far
as to say “Good-night” to him. “No caress,”
he told himself, “had ever been half so blind-
ing sweet.” Which sounds curiously like
what a pushite from the Rocks might remark
of a tart. Two pages further on he is found
writing “an adroit epistle full of respectful
courtesy, admiration even,” in which he asks
if the lady mother will “consider his becom-
ing a suitor for the hand of her daughter.”
Follow four unessential columns filled mostly
with remarks of this description: “For the
love of Pleaven, tell me what you mean to
do!” “Then you have sent for me only
to” “I should have thought even you,
Satanuccio, would need to be told that
rather than give a child of mine to you, I
would kill her with my own hands.” "Then
why,” he said, white with fury, “why did
you not say as much last night?” Next
column —so we read —“As he dashed from
the Dorf into the Bad. about seven o’clock
in the evening, all St. Moritz stopped
to stare. Up on the high piazza of
the hotel Don Pedro saw, with a
sense of reassurement, madame and
her daughter, waiting to assist at his
triumphal entry/
Pedro's exhausted intellect seetns to
have extracted most pleasure from scowl-
ing and staring and driving at full gal-
lop everywhere. All he wanted to make
him a complete fire brigade was a hose
and a helmet. Three columns more, and we
And him at it again : “About a quarter past,
the ladies were in their places. Don Pedro
;prang on the box and gathered up the long
ribbons, one of the smart men in livery
sounded the gleaming horn, and off dashed
the four grey horses aimd the envy of the
assembled crowd.” Also “....he drove
with merciless skill, pleased that the girl
showed no shrinking at the breakneck pace.
Six columns more of this sort of thing, and
they are married and living in a draught-
haunted castle surrounded by precipices.
They seem to spend most of tlieir time look-
ing over the precipices and shuddering. At
the beginning of chapter five, when they have
been married two weeks, the authoress says :
“Shameless sightseers gathered of an after-
noon on the road, hoping to encounter the
pair walking or driving by”—at full gallop,
of course. Two more pages, and something
seems to be amiss, for we read (with a feel-
ing of savage satisfaction) : “Without a
word, without raising his haggard eyes, Don
Pedro left the room.” The reader —who, by
this time has grown weary of the galloping-
fool, and is flicking over the pages to. see
how it all ends —next lights on this : ‘She
obeyed dumbly, and then sat looking at him
vacantly with white hands folded.” W e
notice, as we hurry over the leaf, that lie
shouts at her to “say something for God’s
sake!” Two pages further on, the lady has
attached a gold locket to a silken thread,
and is leaning out the castle window in the
twilight listening to it tinkle against the
stone wall. Don Pedro tells her to stop it,
and goes away chamois-hunting. The next
four pages are nearly all shuddering solilo-
quies on the wind-swept top of the castle, or
aimless crawling about amongst the rocks
with lanterns at dead of night. Once we
observe that “Don Pedro’s eyes flashed an
instant at the old man’s temerity; then he
shrugged his shoulders and laid his hand on
the knob of the door.” (In the old days he
would have laid it on the hilt of his sword
and muttered “Forsooth!” or “Enough,
sirrah!” As he is a modern and does not
wear a sword, it has to be either the door-
knob or the spoons —and if the authoress had
written, “laid his hand on the spoons” the
public might have taken him for a house-
breaker.) Some more examples as the eye
bounds on its way across the pages : “I wish
you wouldn’t stand so near the edge. Are
you not afraid? She shook her head....
Cautiously he made his way toward the
castle, stopping to listen at every step... .
Once or twice he stopned and cursed his
crazy errand. .. .And still the white figure
crouched by the pool.”
It is not surprising, after all the fatu-
ous midnight wandering with lanterns
and peering down precipices and into
wells to find that, at last, they both
fell in and perished. At least she falls into
a glacier in the castle backyard, and he. in
despair, “steps off the wind-swept tower into
mid-air.” And, if nobody was very sorry
about it, his team of four greys were pro-
bably very glad of the rest.
